Buyu — Sales and Trade

Claiming Lineage of a Sold Slave Born in Possession
Ruling on claiming lineage of a slave born in possession after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a sale becomes void if the seller later claims lineage of a slave born in his possession.

Core Idea
Lineage established retroactively voids the entire chain of sale ab initio.

This short ruling from the Hanafi fiqh illustrates a fundamental principle: lineage (nasab) takes precedence over property rights. When a master sells a slave who was born in his possession, and later claims the slave as his own child, the recognition of paternity retroactively invalidates the sale and all subsequent sales. This lesson helps you understand the legal effect of establishing lineage on prior contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the object of sale
The scenario involves an original seller (the master), a slave born in his possession (so the slave's mother is the master's property, and the slave is his property under classical Hanafi law), a first buyer, and a subsequent buyer. The slave is the object of the sale chain.
In Hanafi law, a slave born in the master's house is considered his property, but the master's later claim of paternity changes this status retroactively.
2
Identify the sequence of events
First sale: master sells the slave to buyer 1. Second sale: buyer 1 resells the slave to buyer 2. Only after both sales does the original master claim that the slave is his son.
The timing of the claim is crucial; it occurs after the chain of sales is complete.
3
Determine the legal consequence of the lineage claim
According to the Hanafi ruling, once the master claims paternity, the slave becomes his son. This lineage establishment has retroactive effect, rendering the original sale void (batil) from the beginning. Consequently, all subsequent sales in the chain are also void.
The sale is void ab initio, not merely voidable. This means the buyer must return the slave to the seller, and any further transactions are invalid.
4
Apply the ruling to the full chain
The voiding of the first sale invalidates the transfer of ownership, so the first buyer never had valid title to resell. Therefore, the second sale is also void. The slave is freed from being property and becomes the master's son with full rights.
This principle emphasizes that lineage cannot be transferred by sale; it overrides property rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے ایسا غلام فروخت کیا جو اس کے پاس پیدا ہوا ہے مشتری نے آگے بیچ دیا پھر بائع اول نے اس کے نسب کا دعوی کیا وہ اس کا بیٹا ہوگا اور بیع باطل ہوگی
If someone sold a slave who was born in his possession, and the buyer sold it further, then the original seller claimed his lineage, then he (the slave) will be his son and the sale will be void.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter on lineage and sales. The ruling states that if a slave born in the possession of his master is sold, and the master later claims paternity (lineage), the slave is recognized as his son, retroactively voiding the sale. This is because establishing lineage takes precedence over the sale. The sale being void means the buyer must return the slave to the seller, and the chain of subsequent sales is also void.

Key Terms
بیع (bay'): The contract of sale in Hanafi fiqh.
نسب (nasab): Lineage or paternity established through valid marriage or acknowledgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If A sells a slave born in his house to B, B resells to C, then A claims the slave is his son, what happens to the sales?
A) The sale remains valid because B is a buyer in good faith.
B) The sale is void only between A and B.
C) The sale is void completely, and the slave is A's son.
D) Only the second sale is void, first is valid.

Key Takeaway
A sale of a slave born in the seller's possession is void if the seller later claims lineage, because lineage retroactively establishes the slave as a free son.

Iqrar of Purchase Price Without Possession
Ruling on iqrar of buying a slave without possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a person confesses owing the price of a specified slave purchased but not taken possession, and explain the condition that triggers the confessee's obligation to deliver the slave.

Core Idea
Iqrar of the price for a specified item obligates the confessee to deliver that item to receive payment.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding iqrar (confession) in a sale transaction. When a buyer confesses that he owes the price of a slave he purchased but never took possession, the confessee's rights depend on whether the slave was specified. Understanding this ruling clarifies how iqrar creates mutual obligations even when the original transaction was incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and transaction
Recognize that there is a confessor (muqir) and a confessee (muqarr lahu). The confessor admits he owes 1000 dirhams as the price for a slave he bought from the confessee but never received possession.
Key point: the sale was agreed but possession (qabd) never occurred.
2
Determine the condition of the slave
Examine the text: 'If he confessed a specific slave.' This means the iqrar must mention a particular, identified slave (mu'ayyan). If the slave is not specified, the ruling differs (though the text only gives the specified case).
The condition for the confessee's obligation is that the slave is mu'ayyan.
3
State the legal consequence
When the slave is specified, the confessee is told: 'Hand over the slave and take the thousand.' This means the confessee must deliver the slave to receive the payment. If he cannot or does not, he gets nothing.
The iqrar binds the confessee to perform his part of the contract.
4
Analyze the basis of the ruling
The ruling is based on the principle that iqrar (confession) is binding and creates a conclusive presumption. Since the confessor admitted the price, it is like a confession of the whole contract, so the confessee must fulfill his obligation.
This is a ruling statement without explicit proof, but it aligns with the Hanafi maxim: 'Iqrar is binding on the confessor.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقر نے مقرلہ کے لئے ہزار درہم کے شمن کا اقرار کیا اس غلام کے جو مقر نے اس سے خریدا ہے اور اس پر قبضہ نہیں کیا اگر معین غلام کا اقرار کیا تو مقررہ کہ کہا جائے گا کہ غلام سپرد کر دے اور ہزار لے لے ورنہ تیرے لئے کچھ نہیں
The confessor confessed to the confessee one thousand dirhams as the price of a slave that the confessor bought from him and did not take possession of. If he confessed a specific slave, it will be said to the confessee: hand over the slave and take the thousand, otherwise nothing for you.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This is a ruling on iqrar regarding a sale transaction. If a person (confessor) admits that he owes the price of a slave he bought from the confessee but never took possession, and the slave is specified, the confessee is required to deliver the slave to receive payment. If he cannot or does not, he gets nothing. This is because iqrar confirms the obligation of both parties.

Key Terms
شمن (thaman): Price or value of an item in a sale.
معين غلام (mu'ayyan ghulam): A specific, identified slave (or any specified item).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man confesses he owes 1000 dirhams as the price of a specific slave he bought but never received possession, what must the confessee do?
A) He must give the slave and get the money
B) He gets only the money without giving the slave
C) He has no obligation
D) He can choose to give the slave or not

Key Takeaway
Iqrar of a price for a specified item obligates the confessee to deliver that item to receive the payment; failure to deliver results in forfeiture of the price.

Dispute with Sahibayn on Denial of Sale in Iqrar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on confessor's liability after confessee's denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Sahibayn's ruling on iqrar when the confessee denies the specific sale and distinguish it from the potential view of Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
In iqrar, the confessor's confession binds him to pay, even if the confessee denies the exact transaction.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the binding effect of an iqrar (confession) when the confessee (muqarr lahu) denies the specific sale claimed by the confessor. The Sahibayn hold that the confessor's acknowledgment of a debt makes him liable to pay, regardless of the confessee's denial of the exact details. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the school's treatment of iqrar as a binding proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
The confessor (muqir) claims he sold a specific slave and acknowledges receipt of price. The confessee (muqarr lahu) denies that particular slave was sold, claiming another was sold instead.
2
State the Sahibayn's ruling
According to the Sahibayn, the confessor is liable to pay the price because his iqrar is evidence against him. The confessee's denial of the specific transaction does not void the confessor's liability.
3
Consider the potential opposing view
Imam Abu Hanifa may have held that the confessor is not liable if the confessee denies the exact sale, but this is not detailed in the text. The Sahibayn's view is presented as the preferred one.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the Sahibayn, the confessor pays; under the potential view of Abu Hanifa, he might not. The later Hanafi authorities adopted the Sahibayn's view as mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقرلہ کہے کہ یہ غلام تو تیرا ہی ہے میں نے اسے تیرے ہاتھ فروخت نہیں کیا بلکہ اس کے علاوہ اور غلام فروخت کیا تو میر پر مال لازم ہوگا صاحبین کا نقطہ نظر
The confessee says: 'This slave is yours; I did not sell him to you, but sold another slave besides him.' Then the confessor will be liable for the wealth. This is the view of the Sahibayn.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the opinion of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) in an intra-Hanafi dispute. When the confessee denies the specific sale claimed by the confessor and asserts a different transaction, according to the Sahibayn, the confessor becomes liable to pay the price for the slave he has. The text does not state the view of Imam Abu Hanifa here, but it implies a difference. The ruling is that despite the denial, the confessor's iqrar about the price is binding.

Key Terms
صاحبین (ṣāḥibayn): The two companions of Imam Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, if the confessee says he sold a different slave, what happens?
A) The confessor is liable for the price
B) The confessor has no liability
C) The iqrar is invalid
D) The confessor must return the slave

Key Takeaway
In iqrar, the confessor's own confession creates liability, even if the confessee denies the exact transaction.

Dispute over Quality of Dirhams in Iqrar: Default and Exception
Ruling on iqrar regarding quality of dirhams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling on the quality of dirhams in an iqrar dispute and identify the exception when the confessor's claim is believed.

Core Idea
In a dispute over dirham quality, the default is genuineness; the confessee is believed unless the confessor's claim is made in a specific connected form.

When a person confesses a debt and later disputes the quality of the dirhams, a conflict arises. Hanafi law presumes dirhams to be genuine unless proven otherwise, so the confessee's assertion of genuineness is initially accepted. However, an exception exists if the confessor explicitly qualifies the confession with words like 'except they are of certain weight' or mentions usurpation, and does so in the same breath. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
The confessor (muqir) admits a debt but later claims the dirhams were counterfeit (khūṭe) or non-current. The confessee (muqarr lahu) insists they were genuine.
Understand that the dispute is about quality, not quantity.
2
State the default ruling
The confessee's statement is given weight because the default (asl) in dirhams is that they are genuine and legally current. The confessor is claiming a departure from the norm, so the burden of proof lies on him.
This is based on the continuity of the original state (istishab al-hal).
3
Explain the rationale (dalil)
The ruling is supported by qiyas: just as the default in debts is that they are valid and binding, the default in the medium of payment is that it is sound. The confessor's contrary claim is a novelty (tajaddud) that he must prove.
No explicit scriptural proof is cited; this is a rational derivation.
4
Identify the exception
The text mentions an exception involving specific wording: if the confessor says 'except that they are five in weight' or 'I usurped from him one thousand' (or similar) and then states they were counterfeit, and does so muttassilan (connectedly, in the same utterance), his claim is believed.
The exact phrase is corrupted in OCR, but the principle is that a connected qualifying statement (istithna) changes the ruling.
5
Distinguish the consequence
If the confessor makes the claim of counterfeit in a separate, later statement (munfassilan), the default applies and the confessee is believed. Only a connected statement overrides the default.
This shows the importance of the exact wording and timing in iqrar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقر نے کہا کہ فلاں کے مجھ پر ہزار درہم ہیں۔ اسباب کی قیمت کے یا کہا کہ تو نے مجھے ہزار درہم قرض دیئے ہیں پھر کہا وہ کھوئے یا نہ چلنے والے تھے مقرلہ نے کہا کہ کھرے تھے اسپر کھرے لازم ہوں گے الا انهاوزن خمسة کے الفاظ کے ساتھ استثناء کا حکم اغتصبت منه الفا يا او دعنی کے الفاظ کہے۔ پھر کہا یہ کھوٹے اور نہ چلنے والے تھے متصلا کہے یا منفصلا ، تصدیق کی جائے گی
The confessor said: 'So-and-so has one thousand dirhams on me, as the price of goods' or said: 'You lent me one thousand dirhams,' then he said they were counterfeit or non-current. The confessee said they were genuine. Then genuine will be obligatory. Except with the words 'except that they are five in weight' the ruling of exception. He said 'I usurped from him one thousand' or 'You called me' words. Then he said they were counterfeit and non-current, whether said connectedly or separately, he will be believed.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute about the quality of dirhams in an iqrar of debt or price. The general rule is that the confessee's claim of genuine dirhams is accepted over the confessor's claim of counterfeit, because the default in dirhams is that they are genuine and current. An exception is noted involving specific wording about weight or usurpation. If the confessor makes the claim of counterfeit in the same breath (mutassilan) or later (munfassilan), his statement may be believed. The text is somewhat corrupt but the principle is that the credibility is with the confessee unless specific circumstances apply.

Key Terms
مقر (muqir): The person who makes iqrar (confession).
مقرلہ (muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor the iqrar is made.
کھوٹے (khūṭe): Counterfeit or defective dirhams that are not legal currency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi iqrar dispute, the confessor admits a debt and later claims the dirhams were counterfeit. The confessee says they were genuine. The confessor's claim is made in a separate conversation. Who is believed?
A) The confessor, because he is the one who confessed.
B) The confessee, because the default is that dirhams are genuine.
C) Both, and the court orders a retraction.
D) Neither, and the case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the default quality of dirhams is genuineness; the confessor's claim to the contrary is only accepted if made in a specific connected form.

Conflict of Claims: Two Claimants with Witnesses
Establishes the Hanafi ruling when two men claim ownership with witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling when two claimants present conflicting evidence and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
When both claimants present equally credible witnesses, Hanafi law divides the divisible property equally.

This lesson unpacks a classic judicial dispute: two men each claim a camel, each with two reliable witnesses. How does a Hanafi judge resolve the deadlock? We'll examine the Hanafi position, its direct proof from the Prophet's practice, the Shafi'i objection based on logical impossibility, and the rational response that validates both testimonies. Understanding this debate sharpens your grasp of evidence (bayyinah) in Islamic court procedure.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the operative rule: when two claimants both present witnesses for the same divisible property, the property is divided equally between them.
The non-possessor's evidence is given precedence over the possessor's, contrary to other madhahib.
2
Identify the Hanafi proof (dalil)
Locate the hadith of Tamim bin Tarafah: the Prophet divided a camel equally between two claimants who each brought witnesses. This is the primary textual evidence.
The hadith is ahad but accepted; its transmission is sound (zanni al-thubut).
3
Present the Shafi'i objection
Understand the Shafi'i reasoning: two independent ownerships cannot coexist at the same moment in the same property, so one testimony must be false. Since it is impossible to determine which, both must be rejected.
Some Shafi'is also rely on a lottery hadith.
4
Explain the Hanafi rational response
The Hanafi school argues that testimony is valid based on outward justification (mujawwiz), not inner reality. Both witnesses could be correct in their own perspective (one attesting to cause of ownership, the other to possession). Thus both testimonies are valid and must be given effect where possible—here, by dividing the property.
The lottery hadith is considered abrogated (mansukh) per Imam Tahawi.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf and intra-Hanafi variant
Practical consequence: under Hanafi, the property is divided; under Shafi'i, it remains undecided or goes to lottery. Also note Imam Abu Yusuf's view: if one claimant specifies an earlier time, his evidence is preferred.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the division, following Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يدعيه الرجلان..
۱۲
یہ جلد - یازدهم
جن میں سے ایک قابض ہو اور دوسرا غیر قابض (اسی کو خارج کہتے ہیں) تو ہمارے نزدیک غیر قابض (خارج) کی حجتbot ایbest
قوله و اذا ادعی ... پہلے ایک قاعدہ سمجھ لو تا کہ مسائل سہولت کے ساتھ سمجھ سکو۔ قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ جب ملک مطلق کا دعوی ایسے دو شخصوں میں ہو، امام احمد بھی
کے قائل ہیں۔ اور امام شافعی و امام مالک کے نزدیک قابض کی حجت مقدم ہوگی۔ پھر اگر ایک نے بینہ سے کوئی وقت بیان کر دیا تب بھی طرفین کے نزد یک خارج ہی کالبینہ معتبر ہو گا۔ اور امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک اس کلیتہ معتبر ہو گا، جس نے وقت ثابت کیا ہے۔ اب دو آدمیوں نے ایک چیز کا دعویٰ
کیا۔ اور وہ چیز کسی تیسرے کے پاس ہے۔ اور دونوں نے گواہ قائم کر دیئے تو ہمارے نزدیک وہ چیز دونوں میں نصفا نصف کردی جائے گی۔ امام شافعی کے نزدیک دونوں بیٹے ساقط الاعتبار ہوں گے۔ امام احمد کے نزدیک قرعہ اندازی ہوگی۔ یہ ایک قول امام شافعی کا بھی ہے۔ اس واسطے کہ بالیقین دونوں میں سے ایک بینہ کا ذب ہے۔ کیونکہ بوقت واحد کل شئی میں دو ملکوں کا جمع ہونا محال ہے۔ اور مدینہ صادقہ وہینہ کاذبہ کا امتیاز مشکل ہے۔ لہذا دونوں ساقط ہوں گے۔ یا پھر قرعہ اندازی ہوگی، کیونکہ آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم نے اس جیسے واقعہ میں قرعہ اندازی کی تھی اور فرمایا تھا کہ خدایا ان دونوں میں تو ہی فیصلہ کرنے والا ہے۔ چنانچہ حافظ طبرانی نے معجم اوسط میں حضرت ابو ہریرہ سے روایت کی ہے:
ان رجلين اختصما الى رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ فجاء كل واحد منهما بشهود عدول و في عدة وَاحِدَةٍ فساهم بينهما رَسُوْلِ اللهِ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ وَقَالَ اللَّهُمَّ اقض بينهما -
اس کو امام ابو داؤد نے اپنے مراسیل میں بھی ذکر کیا ہے اور عبدالرزاق نے بھی مرسلا روایت کیا ہے۔ قوله ولنا حديث تميم .... ہماری دلیل حدیث تمیم بن طرفہ ہے۔ جس کی تخریج ابن ابی شیبہ اور عبدالرزاق نے اپنے اپنے مصنف میں اور بیہقی نے کتاب المعرفہ میں کی ہے: ان رجلين ادعيا بعيرًا فاقام كلّ واحدٍ منهما الْبَيِّنَةَ انه له فقضى النبي صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ بِهِ بَيْنَهُمَا کہ آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم کے حضور میں دو آدمیوں نے ایک اونٹ کے بارے میں دعویٰ کیا اور ان میں سے ہر ایک نے ہینہ قائم کیا کہ اونٹ میرا ہے، تو آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم نے اونٹ ان دونوں میں نصفا نصف کر دیا۔
اس باب میں حضرت ابو موسی اشعری ابو ہریرہ ، اور جابر بن سمرہ پہلے سے منداحادیث بھی وارد ہیں۔ رہی قرعہ اندازی سویہ حکم ابتداء اسلام میں تھا پھر منسوخ ہو گیا۔ جیسا کہ امام طحاوی نے ثابت کیا ہے۔ قولہ ولان المطلق ، یہ ہماری عقلی دلیل ہے کہ عادل گواہوں کے فریقین میں سے ہر ایک کے لئے جو چیز مجیز شہادت ہے وہ متل الوجود ہے۔ بایں طور کہ ممکن ہے ایک فریق نے سبب ملک پر اعتماد کر کے اور دوسرے نے ظاہری قبضہ دیکھ کر گواہی دی ہو۔ اور ہر وہ شہادت جس کیلئے اس طرح کا مجیز ہو وہ صحیح ہوتی ہے۔ خواہ واقع کے مطابق ہو یا نہ ہو۔ اس لئے کہ شہادت کی صحت واقع میں مشہور بہ کے تحقیق پر موقوف نہیں۔ کیونکہ یہ تو غیب کی بات ہے۔ جس پر بندے مطلع نہیں ہو سکتے بلکہ صحت شہادت کا اعتماد ظاہر حال پر ہے۔ پس دونوں شہادتیں صحیح ہوئیں۔ تو جہاں تک ممکن ہو دونوں پر عمل کرنا ضروری ہے۔ اور یہاں عمل اس طرح ممکن ہے کہ دونوں کے لئے نصفا نصف کا حکم دیا جائے۔ کیونکہ محل قابل تنصیف ہے۔ اور تنصیف اس لئے ہے کہ سبب استحقاق میں دونوں برابر ہیں۔ دو آدمیوں نے ایک عورت سے نکاح کا دعویٰ کیا اور دونوں نے گواہ قائم کر دیئے تو بینہ کی وجہ سے کسی ایک کیلئے فیصلہ نہیں کیا جائے گا
Chapter of what two men claim. ... This volume - eleven. In which one is a possessor and the other is a non-possessor (called al-kharji). According to us, the proof of the non-possessor (al-kharji) [is preferred] ... He said: And when two men claim... First understand a rule so that issues can be understood easily. The rule is that when ownership is claimed by two persons, Imam Ahmad also holds this view. And according to Imam Shafi'i and Imam Malik, the proof of the possessor is given precedence. Then if one of them specifies a time with evidence, then according to both sides, the evidence of the outsider (al-kharji) is still considered valid. And according to Imam Abu Yusuf, the evidence of the one who established the time is considered valid. Now if two men claim one thing, and that thing is in the possession of a third party, and both present witnesses, then according to us the thing will be divided equally between them. According to Imam Shafi'i, both evidences are invalid. According to Imam Ahmad, a lottery will be conducted. This is also one opinion of Imam Shafi'i. Because it is certain that one of the two pieces of evidence is false, since at one moment the combination of two ownerships in one thing is impossible. And distinguishing between the true testifier and the false testifier is difficult. Therefore both are invalid, or a lottery is conducted, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) in a similar incident conducted a lottery and said: O God, You are the Judge between them. So Hafiz Tabarani narrated in al-Mu'jam al-Awsat from Abu Hurairah: 'Two men disputed before the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and each came with just witnesses of equal number, so the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) cast lots between them and said: O Allah, decide between them.' This has also been mentioned by Abu Dawud in his Marasil and by Abdurrazzaq as a mursal. His saying: And for us the hadith of Tamim... Our evidence is the hadith of Tamim bin Tarafah, which has been recorded by Ibn Abi Shaybah and Abdurrazzaq in their Musannafs and by Bayhaqi in Kitab al-Ma'rifah: 'Two men claimed a camel, and each presented evidence that it is his, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) adjudicated it between them' meaning half and half. In this chapter, there are also similar hadiths from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Abu Hurairah, and Jabir bin Samurah. As for the lottery, that ruling was in early Islam and then abrogated, as Imam Tahawi has established. His saying: And because the absolute... This is our rational proof: that for each of the two parties, that which justifies the testimony of just witnesses exists in a possible manner: it is possible that one party relied on the cause of ownership and the other witnessed the apparent possession. And every testimony that has such a justifier is valid, whether it matches reality or not, because the validity of testimony does not depend on verifying the reality of the matter (which is unseen). Rather, the validity of testimony is based on outward circumstances. Thus both testimonies are valid. So it is necessary to act upon both as much as possible. And here it is possible by adjudicating half for each, because the subject is divisible. And division is due to the equal cause of entitlement. As for two men who claim marriage to a woman and both present evidence, then due to the evidence, no decision will be given for either.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the ruling when two men claim ownership of the same property and both present witnesses. The Hanafi position is that if the property is divisible (e.g., a camel), it is divided equally between them. The Shafi'i position holds that both testimonies are invalid because one must be false, leading to no decision or a lottery. The Hanbali position (and one Shafi'i view) prefers a lottery. The text provides two proofs: a hadith from Tamim bin Tarafah where the Prophet divided the camel equally, and a rational argument that both testimonies are valid as they are based on possible justifications (one on cause of ownership, the other on possession). The lottery is shown to be abrogated. A key contrast is made with marriage, where division is impossible. The passage also notes the difference between a possessor and a non-possessor (kharji), and mentions Imam Abu Yusuf's view that if time is specified, the one with earlier time prevails.

Key Terms
قابض (qabid): The person who currently possesses the claimed property.
خارج (kharji): The non-possessor, i.e., the claimant who does not have physical possession.
بینہ (bayyinah): Legal evidence, usually by two just witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if two men claim ownership of a camel and both present witnesses, what is the ruling?
A) The camel is given to the possessor.
B) The camel is divided equally between them.
C) Both testimonies are rejected.
D) A lottery is conducted.

Key Takeaway
When both claimants present equally strong evidence for a divisible property, Hanafi law divides it equally, based on the Prophet's precedent and rational justification of dual testimonies.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Conflicting Claims: Purchase vs Gift, Marriage, and Pledge
Chapter on conflicting claims with preference rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rules for resolving conflicting claims over a single object when two parties produce evidence of different transactions (purchase, gift, marriage dower, pledge) and explain the reasoning based on commutative vs gratuitous contracts and the role of guarantee (daman).

Core Idea
Purchase beats gift; pledge beats gift by istihsan; purchase and marriage are equally strong according to Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad prefers purchase.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common judicial problem: two claimants present evidence for different transactions over the same property, with no dates. How does the qadi decide? The Hanafi school provides specific rules based on the nature of each contract—whether it is commutative (mu'awada) or gratuitous (tabarru'), and whether possession creates a guarantee (daman). We will walk through three scenarios and the reasoning behind each preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main scenarios
The passage covers three conflicts: (1) purchase vs gift, (2) purchase vs marriage (as mahr), (3) pledge vs gift. For each, note the ruling and the underlying reasoning.
Also note the exception for gift with compensation (hiba bi-shart al-iwad).
2
Analyse the purchase vs gift rule
Purchase is preferred because it is a commutative contract, establishing ownership immediately (bi-nafsihi), while gift requires possession to complete ownership. The same reasoning applies to charity (sadaqa) with possession.
Gift and charity with possession are equal; no preference between them.
3
Examine the purchase vs marriage dispute
Abu Yusuf holds both are commutative contracts of equal strength, so the qadi adjudicates equally. Muhammad argues purchase should be preferred because it is possible to give effect to both: the purchaser takes the slave, and the wife receives its value from the husband.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; later practice often favours Muhammad's view.
4
Understand the pledge vs gift rule
By qiyas, gift should be preferred because it establishes ownership. But by istihsan, pledge is preferred because the pledgee's possession carries a guarantee (daman), making the contract stronger. The exception is a gift with compensation (treated as a sale), which beats a pledge.
The concept of daman (liability) is key; pledge creates a right of retention with liability, gift does not.
5
Reflect on the role of istihsan
Istihsan allows departure from qiyas in favour of a stronger legal reason. Here, the guarantee (daman) in pledge outweighs the mere ownership-establishing nature of a gift. This illustrates a core Hanafi method.
Compare with the position of other schools that reject istihsan.

Source Text

باب ما يدعيه الرجلان.
قَالَ وَ إِنِ ادَّعَى اَحَدُهُمَا شِرَاء وَالْآخَرُ هِبَةً وَ قَبْضًا مَعْنَاهُ مِنْ وَاحِدٍ وَأَقَامَا بَيْنَةً وَ لَا تَارِيخَ مَعَهُمَا فَالشِّرَاءُ أولى لأنَّ الشَّرَاءَ أَقْوَى لِكَوْنِهِ مُعَاوَضَةٌ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْن وَ لأَنَّهُ يَثْبُتُ بنَفْسِهِ وَالْمِلْكُ فِي الْهَبَةِ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الْقَبْضِ وَ كَذَا الشَّرَاءُ وَالصَّدَقَةُ مَعَ الْقَبْضِ لِمَا بَيَّنَّا وَالْهِبَةُ وَالْقَبْضُ وَالصَّدَقَةُ مَعَ الْقَبْضِ سَوَاءٌ حَتَّى يُقْضَى بَيْنَهُمَا لِاسْتِوَائِهِمَا فِي وَجْهِ التَّبَرُعِ وَلَا تَرْجِيْحَ بِاللُّزُومِ لِأَنَّهُ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْمَالِ وَالتَّرْجِيْعُ لِمَعْنَى قَائِم فِي الْحَالِ وَهَذَا فِيمَا لَا يَحْتَمِلُ الْقِسْمَةَ صَحِيحٌ وَكَذَا فِيْمَا يَحْتَمِلُهَا عِنْدَ الْبَعْضِ لِاَنَّ الشُّيُوعَ طَارَ وَ عِنْدَ الْبَعْضِ لَا يَصِحُ لِأَنَّهُ تَنْفِيذُ الْهِبَةِ فِي الشَّائِعِ قَالَ وَإِذَا ادَّعَى اَحَدُهُمَا الشَّرَاءَ وَادَّعَتِ امْرَأَةٌ أَنَّهُ تَزَوَّجَهَا عَلَيْهِ فَهُمَا سَوَاءٌ لِاسْتِوَانِهِمَا فِي الْقُوَّةِ فَإِنَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا عَقْدُ مُعَاوَضَةٍ يَثْبُتُ الْمِلْكُ بِنَفْسِهِ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفَ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ الشَّرَاءُ أَوْلَى وَلَهَا عَلَى الزَّوْحِ الْقِيْمَةُ لِأَنَّهُ أَمْكَنَ الْعَمَلُ بِالْبَيِّنتَينِ بِتَقْدِيمِ الشَّرَاءِ إِذِ التَّزَوُّجُ عَلَى عَيْنِ مَمْلُوكِ لِلْغَيْرِ صَحِيحٌ وَيَجِبُ قِيمَتُه عِنْدَ تَعَدُّرِ تَسْلِيمِهِ وَإِن ادَّعَى أَحَدُهُمَا وَهُنًا وَقَبْضًا وَالْآخَرُ هِبَةً وَقَبْضًا وَأَقَامَا بَيْنَةً فَالرَّهْنُ أولى وَهَذَا اِسْتِحْسَانٌ وَفِى الْقِيَاسِ الْهِبَةُ أولى لأنَّهَا تُثْبِتُ الْمِلْكَ وَالرَّهُنُ لَا يُثْبِتُهُ وَجْهُ الْإِسْتِحْسَانِ اَنَّ الْمَقْبُوضَ بِحُكْمِ الرَّهْنِ مَضْمُونٌ وَبِحُكْمِ الْهِيَةِ غَيْرُ مَضْمُونَ وَعَقْدُ الصَّمَانِ أَقْوَى بِخِلَافِ الْهِبَةِ بِشَرْطِ الْعِوَضِ لِأَنَّه بَيْعٌ اِنْتِهَاء وَالْبَيْعُ أَولَى مِنَ الرَّهَنِ لِأَنَّهُ عَقْدُ ضَمَان يَثْبُتُ المِلكَ صُورَةً وَمَعْنى وَالرَّهْنُ لَا يُثْبِتُه إِلَّا عِنْدَ الْهَلَاكِ مَعْنِّى لَا صُورَةً فَكَذَا الْهِبَةُ بِشَرْطِ الْعِوَضِ

ترجمہ اگر دعوی کیا ان میں سے ایک نے خرید کا اور دوسرے نے بہیہ مع القبض کا۔ یعنی ایک ہی شخص ہے۔ اور دونوں نے مبینہ قائم کردیا اور تاریخ کسی کے پاس نہیں تو خریدار اولی ہے۔ کیونکہ خرید قوی تر ہے جانہین سے معاوضہ ہونے کی بنا پر ۔ نیز وہ بذات خود مثبت ملک ہے۔ اور ملک میہ میں قبضہ پر موقوف ہوتی ہے۔ اسی طرح خرید اور صدقہ مع القبض ہے۔ اور مہبہ مع القبض اور صدقہ مع القبض برابر ہیں یہاں تک کہ محکم کیا جائے گا۔ ان میں مساوی ہونے کا طریق تبرع میں دونوں کے برابر ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ اور لزوم سے کوئی ترجیح نہیں۔ کیونکہ یہ مال کی طرف راجع ہے اور ترجیح ایسے معنی سے ہوتی ہے جو فی الحال قائم ہوں اور یہ حکم نا قابل قسمت چیزوں میں صحیح ہے۔ اور اسی طرح قابل قسمت میں بھی بعض کے نزدیک کیونکہ شیوع طاری ہے۔ اور بعض کے نزدیک صحیح نہیں۔ کیونکہ یہ ہبہ کو شائع میں نافذ کرنا ہے۔ اگر دعوی کیا ایک نے خرید کا اور دعویٰ کیا عورت نے کہ اس نے مجھ سے اس پر شادی کی ہے۔ تو یہ دونوں برابر ہیں۔ قوت میں یکساں ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ کیونکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک معاوضہ ہے۔ جو بذات خود ملک ثابت کرتا ہے اور یہ امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک ہے۔ امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ خرید اولی ہے۔ اور عورت کے لئے شوہر پر قیمت ہے۔ کیونکہ دونوں بینوں پر مل ممکن ہے خرید کو مقدم کر کے اس لئے کہ غیر کے مملوک مال پر نکاح کرنا صحیح ہے۔ اور سپردگی مسعد ر ہونے کے وقت اس کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے۔ اگر ایک نے دعوی کیا مع القبض کا اور دوسرے نے بد مع القبض کا اور دونوں نے بینہ قائم کر دیا تو رہمن اولیٰ ہے۔ اور یہ استحسان ہے قیاس میں ہبہ اولیٰ ہے۔ کیونکہ وہ ملک ثابت کرتا ہے۔ اور رہن ملک ثابت نہیں کرتا۔ وجہ استحسان یہ ہے کہ مقبوض بحکم رہن مضمون ہوتا ہے۔ اور مقبوض بحکم ہبہ مضمون نہیں ہوتا اور عقد ضمان قوی تر ہوتا ہے۔ بخلاف ہبہ بشرط العوض کے کیونکہ یہ انتہاء بیچ ہوتا ہے۔ اور بیچ رہن سے اولیٰ ہے۔ کیونکہ بیع عقد ضمان ہے۔ جو ظاہر اور باطن میں ملک ثابت کرتا ہے۔ اور رہن ملک ثابت نہیں کرتا ۔ مگر تلف ہونے کے وقت صرف معنی نہ کہ
Chapter on what two men claim. He said: And if one of them claims purchase and the other a gift with possession, meaning from one person, and they both produce evidence and no date is with them, then the purchaser is preferable because purchase is stronger due to being a transaction from both sides, and because it establishes ownership by itself, while ownership in gift is dependent on possession. Similarly, purchase and charity with possession are as we have stated. And gift with possession and charity with possession are equal, so that the qadi will adjudicate between them due to their equality in the aspect of gratuitousness, and there is no preference due to bindingness because it relates to property, and preference is for a meaning existing at present. This is valid for what does not admit division, and similarly for what admits it according to some, because the commonness is incidental; and according to some it is not valid because it is executing a gift in a common property. He said: And if one of them claims purchase and a woman claims that he married her on it, then they are equal due to equality in strength, for each of them is a commutative contract that establishes ownership by itself. This is according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: Purchase is preferable, and the woman has the value from the husband, because it is possible to act on both evidences by giving precedence to purchase, since marriage on a specific property owned by another is valid, and its value becomes obligatory when delivery is impossible. And if one of them claims a pledge with possession and the other a gift with possession, and they both produce evidence, then the pledge is preferable. This is istihsan. In qiyas, the gift is preferable because it establishes ownership, whereas the pledge does not. The reason for istihsan is that the thing taken by virtue of pledge is guaranteed (mamnun), while by virtue of gift it is not guaranteed, and a contract of guarantee is stronger. Unlike a gift with a condition of compensation, because it is ultimately a sale, and a sale is preferable to a pledge because it is a contract of guarantee that establishes ownership in form and meaning, while the pledge does not establish it except at the time of destruction in meaning only, not in form. So also the gift with a condition of compensation.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new chapter (Bab ma yad'iyah al-rajulan) from Al-Hidayah, dealing with competing claims over a specific object (here a slave) where two people present claims based on different legal transactions. The passage addresses three scenarios: (1) purchase vs. gift with possession: purchase is preferred because it is a commutative contract (mu'awada) that transfers ownership immediately, while gift requires possession to complete ownership. (2) purchase vs. marriage (as a mahr): Abu Yusuf holds they are equal because both are commutative contracts that establish ownership by themselves; Muhammad al-Shaybani prefers purchase because it can be given precedence, and the wife gets the value from the husband as the slave is now sold. (3) pledge (rahn) vs. gift: by istihsan, the pledge is preferred because the possession under pledge carries a guarantee (daman), while gift does not; but gift with a condition of compensation (hiba bi-shart al-iwad) is treated like a sale, which is stronger than pledge. The commentary explains the reasoning for each, highlighting the Hanafi use of qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference).

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of departing from qiyas in favor of a stronger legal reason. Here, it is used to prefer pledge over gift despite qiyas.
مضمون (mamnun): That which is guaranteed, i.e., if the object is destroyed, the holder is liable to compensate. In this context, the pledgee is liable, the donee is not.
هبة بشرط العوض (hiba bi-shart al-iwad): A gift with a condition of compensation, which the Hanafis treat as a sale. The text distinguishes it from ordinary gift in that it is stronger than pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, when one claimant proves purchase of a slave and a woman proves the slave was given as her mahr, how are the claims resolved?
A) The purchase claim is preferred.
B) The marriage claim is preferred.
C) They are equal, and the qadi rules between them.
D) The slave is divided between them.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the preference between conflicting claims depends on the type of contract: commutative (mu'awada) beats gratuitous (tabarru'), and contracts with guarantee (daman) are stronger than those without, but intra-Hanafi disputes remain on specific cases.

Purchase Preferred Over Gift with Possession in Absence of Date
Ruling on preferring purchase over gift with both claims undated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a purchase claim is preferred over a gift claim when both have evidence without dates, and explain the reasoning based on the nature of the contracts.

Core Idea
Purchase is stronger than gift because it is a mutual exchange and establishes ownership without possession.

When two people claim the same property from a third party, one alleging a purchase and the other a gift with possession, and both produce witnesses without dates, a judge must decide who has the stronger claim. This passage from the Hanafi tradition outlines the rule and its reasoning, emphasizing the strength of purchase as a commutative contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
Recognize that there are two claimants, each claiming a different type of contract (purchase vs. gift with possession) from the same person, both with witnesses but no dates.
The absence of dates is crucial; if one had a date, that claimant would win.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The purchase claim is preferred over the gift claim. The judge rules in favor of the purchase claimant.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Analyze the proof: purchase is stronger
Purchase is a mutual exchange (mu'awada) from both sides, making it stronger. Additionally, purchase establishes ownership by itself, while gift requires possession (qabd) to complete ownership.
The proof is qiyas based on the nature of the contracts.
4
Compare charitable contracts
If both claims are charitable (gift vs. charity with possession), they are equal because both are purely gratuitous. Luzum (irrevocability) is not considered for preference because it relates to a later stage, not the present claim evaluation.
This shows that the preference for purchase is due to its commercial nature, not any other factor.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on divisible property
For divisible property, some scholars apply the same split ruling (each gets half) because the common ownership is incidental. Others reject it because it would imply a valid gift of an undivided share (musha'), which is impermissible in Hanafi fiqh.
This difference is procedural; the mufta bihi may depend on the prevailing view in the locality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ: اگر دعوی کیا ان میں سے ایک نے خرید کا اور دوسرے نے ہبہ مع القبض کا۔ یعنی ایک ہی شخص ہے۔ اور دونوں نے بینہ قائم کردیا اور تاریخ کسی کے پاس نہیں تو خریدار اولی ہے۔ کیونکہ خرید قوی تر ہے جانبین سے معاوضہ ہونے کی بنا پر ۔ نیز وہ بذات خود مثبت ملک ہے۔ اور ملک ہبہ میں قبضہ پر موقوف ہوتی ہے۔ اسی طرح خرید اور صدقہ مع القبض ہے۔ اور ہبہ مع القبض اور صدقہ مع القبض برابر ہیں یہاں تک کہ محکم کیا جائے گا۔ ان میں مساوی ہونے کا طریق تبرع میں دونوں کے برابر ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ اور لزوم سے کوئی ترجیح نہیں۔ کیونکہ یہ مال کی طرف راجع ہے اور ترجیح ایسے معنی سے ہوتی ہے جو فی الحال قائم ہوں اور یہ حکم نا قابل قسمت چیزوں میں صحیح ہے۔ اور اسی طرح قابل قسمت میں بھی بعض کے نزدیک کیونکہ شیوع طاری ہے۔ اور بعض کے نزدیک صحیح نہیں۔ کیونکہ یہ ہبہ کو شائع میں نافذ کرنا ہے۔

قوله و هذا افيما لا يحتمل ... حكم مذكور (نصف نصف کا فیصلہ ) ان اشیاء میں تو صحیح ہے جو قابلِ قسمت ہیں۔ جیسے داروئستان ۔ اس واسطے کہ ہبہ کا شیوع تو بعد کو طاری ہوا ہے۔ یعنی پہلے تو وہ کل عین کا مستحق ہوا تھا۔ مگر دوسرے مدعی کی مزاحمت سے ہبہ نصف شائع میں رہ گیا۔ اور بعض مشائخ کے نزدیک صحیح نہیں۔ کیونکہ اس میں جبہ مشاع کی تنقید لازم آتی ہے۔ حالانکہ مہبہ مشاع یعنی غیر مقسوم کا سب سیج نہیں ہوتا۔
He said: And if one of them claims purchase and the other claims gift with possession, meaning from one person, and both produce evidence and there is no date with them, then purchase is more deserving because purchase is stronger for being an exchange from both sides and because it establishes [ownership] by itself, while ownership in gift is dependent on possession. Similarly, purchase and charity with possession [are alike] as we explained. And gift with possession and charity with possession are equal, so a judgment is made between them equally due to their equality in being a charitable act. And there is no preference due to irrevocability because it returns to the property, and preference is for a quality existing at present. And this ruling is valid for indivisible property, and similarly for divisible property according to some because the common ownership is incidental, and according to others it is not valid because it would be executing a gift of an undivided share.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on competing claims to property. The scenario: one person claims he purchased the item from Zaid, another claims Zaid gave it to him as a gift with possession. Both produce witnesses, but no dates are mentioned. The ruling: the claim of purchase takes precedence. The reasoning is twofold: first, purchase is a mutual exchange (mu'awada) giving it strength, while gift is purely gratuitous; second, purchase establishes ownership by itself, whereas gift requires possession (qabd) to complete ownership. The text then notes that if both claims are of a charitable nature (gift vs. charity with possession), they are equal because both are acts of donation. Irrevocability (luzum) is not a basis for preference because it relates to a later stage, whereas the claim evaluation is at the present moment. There is an intra-Hanafi difference regarding divisible property: some scholars say the ruling of splitting equally applies even to divisible property because the common ownership arises later; others say it is not valid because it would be executing a gift of an undivided share, which is impermissible (as gift of musha' is not valid in Hanafi fiqh).

Key Terms
شِرَاء (shiraa): Purchase; a contract of sale that transfers ownership immediately by mutual exchange.
هِبَة (hibah): Gift; a gratuitous contract transferring ownership, but requiring possession (qabd) to perfect the transfer.
قَبْض (qabd): Possession; physical taking of property, necessary for completing certain contracts like hibah.
لُزُوم (luzum): Irrevocability; a contract becomes binding and cannot be revoked unilaterally.
شَائِع (shai'i): Undivided share; common ownership in a property not physically partitioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The one claiming purchase.
C) They split the item equally.
D) The item is left with the third person.

Key Takeaway
In undated claims with equal evidence, the Hanafi school prefers a purchase claim over a gift claim because purchase is a commutative contract that establishes ownership without possession.

Conflicting Claims: Purchase vs. Marriage Mahr — Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on competing purchase and marriage claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings of Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on competing claims of purchase and marriage mahr, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of their difference.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf splits the item; Muhammad gives it to the purchaser and makes the husband pay its value.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on how to resolve a conflict between two claims: one party claims he purchased a slave from a defendant, and a woman claims the same slave was her mahr in marriage to the defendant. Both produce evidence but no dates. How does a qadi decide? Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad offer different solutions, each grounded in the nature of the contracts involved. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi jurists prioritize conflicting exchange contracts.

Guided Path
1
State the facts of the case
Identify the parties and their claims: a purchaser claims he bought a slave from the defendant; a woman claims the same slave was her mahr in marriage to the defendant. Both have evidence but no dates.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Yusuf holds that both claims are equal because both purchase and marriage are contracts of exchange (mu'awadah) that establish ownership directly. Muhammad holds that purchase is preferred because it allows acting on both evidences.
3
Analyze the reasoning (dalil) of each
Abu Yusuf: Both contracts are mu'awada, so they are equal in strength; the slave is divided equally. Muhammad: It is possible to apply both evidences by giving the slave to the purchaser and obliging the husband to pay its value (qimah) to the wife, since marriage on property owned by another is valid.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: under Abu Yusuf, the slave is divided equally; under Muhammad, the purchaser gets the slave and the wife gets its value from her husband.
This difference affects the actual distribution of property.
5
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
The text does not explicitly prefer one view, but later Hanafi works often adopt Muhammad's opinion because it harmonizes both proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِذَا ادَّعَى اَحَدُهُمَا الشَّرَاءَ وَادَّعَتِ امْرَأَةٌ أَنَّهُ تَزَوَّجَهَا عَلَيْهِ فَهُمَا سَوَاءٌ لِاسْتِوَانِهِمَا فِي الْقُوَّةِ فَإِنَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا عَقْدُ مُعَاوَضَةٍ يَثْبُتُ الْمِلْكُ بِنَفْسِهِ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفَ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ الشَّرَاءُ أَوْلَى وَلَهَا عَلَى الزَّوْحِ الْقِيْمَةُ لِأَنَّهُ أَمْكَنَ الْعَمَلُ بِالْبَيِّنتَينِ بِتَقْدِيمِ الشَّرَاءِ إِذِ التَّزَوُّجُ عَلَى عَيْنِ مَمْلُوكِ لِلْغَيْرِ صَحِيحٌ وَيَجِبُ قِيمَتُه عِنْدَ تَعَدُّرِ تَسْلِيمِهِ

ترجمہ: اگر دعوی کیا ایک نے خرید کا اور دعویٰ کیا عورت نے کہ اس نے مجھ سے اس پر شادی کی ہے۔ تو یہ دونوں برابر ہیں۔ قوت میں یکساں ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ کیونکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک معاوضہ ہے۔ جو بذات خود ملک ثابت کرتا ہے اور یہ امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک ہے۔ امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ خرید اولی ہے۔ اور عورت کے لئے شوہر پر قیمت ہے۔ کیونکہ دونوں بینوں پر عمل ممکن ہے خرید کو مقدم کر کے اس لئے کہ غیر کے مملوک مال پر نکاح کرنا صحیح ہے۔ اور سپردگی مسعد ر ہونے کے وقت اس کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے۔

تشریح: قوله واذا ادعى عمرو کے قبضہ میں ایک غلام ہے۔ زید نے دعویٰ کیا کہ میں نے یہ غلام عمرو سے خریدا ہے اور ہندہ نے دعویٰ کیا کہ عمرو نے اس غلام کو میرا مہر مقرر کر کے مجھ سے نکاح کیا ہے اور دونوں نے بینہ قائم کر دیا تو دونوں کے دعوے اور بینے برابر ہوں گے۔ کیونکہ شراء اور تزوج دونوں عقود معاوضہ اور بنفسہ مثبت ملک ہیں تو قوت میں دونوں برابر ہوئے۔ امام محمد کے نزدیک دعوی شرا اولی ہے۔ یعنی خرید کا حکم دیا جائے گا۔ اور عورت کے لئے اپنے شوہر پر اس غلام کی قیمت واجب ہوگی۔ کیونکہ دونوں بینوں پر عمل ممکن ہے اس لئے کہ غیر کے مملوک مال عین پر نکاح کرنا جائز ہے۔ اور جب اس کو سپرد نہ کر سکے تو اس کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے۔ پس یہ بات ممکن ہے کہ عورت کے لئے اس کے شوہر پر غلام کی قیمت واجب کی جائے اور مشتری کے لئے خرید کا حکم دیا جائے۔
He said: And when one of them claims purchase and a woman claims that he married her with it [as mahr], then they are equal due to their equality in strength, for each of them is a contract of exchange that establishes ownership by itself. This is according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: Purchase is more deserving, and she has the value from the husband because it is possible to act on both evidences by giving precedence to purchase, since marriage on property owned by another is valid, and its value becomes obligatory when delivery is impossible.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding competing claims: one party claims he purchased a slave from the defendant, and a woman claims the same slave was her mahr in marriage to the defendant. Both produce evidence but no dates. Abu Yusuf holds both claims are equal because both purchase and marriage are mu'awada (exchange) contracts that establish ownership directly, so the slave should be divided equally. Muhammad, however, says purchase is preferred, and the woman is entitled to the value of the slave from her husband. His reasoning: it is possible to apply both evidences by giving the slave to the purchaser and making the husband pay the wife's mahr in monetary value. This is because marriage on property owned by another is valid (the marriage is valid even if the mahr is not owned by the husband at the time), and if the property cannot be delivered (since it now belongs to the purchaser), the equivalent value (qimah) becomes due. Thus, both claims are honored: the purchaser gets the slave, and the wife gets its value from the husband.

Key Terms
مُعَاوَضَة (mu'awadah): An exchange contract where both parties give and receive something of value.
قِيمَة (qimah): Monetary value or compensation paid when the specific item cannot be delivered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, what is the ruling when a purchase claim and a marriage mahr claim conflict on the same property?
A) The property is divided equally between the two claimants.
B) The purchaser gets the property, and the wife gets nothing.
C) The purchaser gets the property, and the wife gets its value from the husband.
D) The wife gets the property, and the purchaser gets its value from the husband.

Key Takeaway
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on conflicting contracts demonstrates how scholars prioritize proofs: Imam Muhammad harmonizes both, while Abu Yusuf treats them as equal.

Pledge vs. Gift: Istihsan Prefers Pledge over Gift
Ruling preferring pledge over gift by istihsan, with qiyas contrast and exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prefers a claim of pledge over a claim of gift by istihsan, and identify the exception for gift with compensation.

Core Idea
A pledge claim beats a gift claim because pledge involves guarantee, making it stronger by istihsan.

When two people claim the same property, one as pledge and one as gift, how do we decide? Strict analogy would favor gift because it transfers ownership, but the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to prefer pledge. The key factor is guarantee (daman): the pledgee is liable for the property, making his claim stronger. However, if the gift is conditional on compensation, it becomes a sale and then takes precedence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the competing claims and the default qiyas ruling
One party claims the property was given as a pledge (rahn) with possession; the other claims it was a gift (hibah) with possession. Both produce evidence. Under strict qiyas (analogy), the gift claim would be preferred because gift transfers ownership, while pledge does not.
Qiyas focuses on the effect on ownership, favoring the claim that transfers ownership.
2
Understand the istihsan reasoning based on guarantee (daman)
The Hanafi school departs from qiyas by istihsan, preferring the pledge claim. The reason is that the property held as a pledge is guaranteed (mamnun) — the pledgee is liable for its loss if it occurs while in his possession. A gift, being gratuitous, does not carry such liability. Since a contract of guarantee is stronger than a gratuitous contract, the pledge claim takes precedence.
Daman (guarantee) is the 'illah (effective cause) for preferring the pledge claim.
3
Recognize the exception: gift with compensation (hiba bi-shart al-'iwad)
If the gift is made with a condition of compensation (i.e., the donee gives something in return), it is effectively a sale. A sale contract is a contract of guarantee that transfers ownership both in form and meaning. Therefore, such a gift (sale) claim is even stronger than a pledge claim and is preferred over pledge by istihsan.
This exception shows that the underlying principle is strength of contract: sale > pledge > gratuitous gift.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِن ادَّعَى أَحَدُهُمَا رَهْنًا وَقَبْضًا وَالْآخَرُ هِبَةً وَقَبْضًا وَأَقَامَا بَيْنَةً فَالرَّهْنُ أولى وَهَذَا اِسْتِحْسَانٌ وَفِى الْقِيَاسِ الْهِبَةُ أولى لأنَّهَا تُثْبِتُ الْمِلْكَ وَالرَّهُنُ لَا يُثْبِتُهُ وَجْهُ الْإِسْتِحْسَانِ اَنَّ الْمَقْبُوضَ بِحُكْمِ الرَّهْنِ مَضْمُونٌ وَبِحُكْمِ الْهِيَةِ غَيْرُ مَضْمُونَ وَعَقْدُ الصَّمَانِ أَقْوَى بِخِلَافِ الْهِبَةِ بِشَرْطِ الْعِوَضِ لِأَنَّه بَيْعٌ اِنْتِهَاء وَالْبَيْعُ أَولَى مِنَ الرَّهَنِ لِأَنَّهُ عَقْدُ ضَمَان يَثْبُتُ المِلكَ صُورَةً وَمَعْنى وَالرَّهْنُ لَا يُثْبِتُه إِلَّا عِنْدَ الْهَلَاكِ مَعْنِّى لَا صُورَةً فَكَذَا الْهِبَةُ بِشَرْطِ الْعِوَضِ

ترجمہ: اگر ایک نے دعوی کیا رہن مع القبض کا اور دوسرے نے ہبہ مع القبض کا اور دونوں نے بینہ قائم کر دیا تو رہن اولیٰ ہے۔ اور یہ استحسان ہے قیاس میں ہبہ اولیٰ ہے۔ کیونکہ وہ ملک ثابت کرتا ہے۔ اور رہن ملک ثابت نہیں کرتا۔ وجہ استحسان یہ ہے کہ مقبوض بحکم رہن مضمون ہوتا ہے۔ اور مقبوض بحکم ہبہ مضمون نہیں ہوتا اور عقد ضمان قوی تر ہوتا ہے۔ بخلاف ہبہ بشرط العوض کے کیونکہ یہ انتہاء بیع ہوتا ہے۔ اور بیع رہن سے اولیٰ ہے۔ کیونکہ بیع عقد ضمان ہے۔ جو ظاہر اور باطن میں ملک ثابت کرتا ہے۔ اور رہن ملک ثابت نہیں کرتا ۔ مگر تلف ہونے کے وقت صرف معنی نہ کہ صورت ۔ پس ایسے ہی ہبہ شرط العوض ہے۔

تشریح: قوله وان ادعا احدهما رهنا .... ایک شخص نے رہن مع القبض کا دعویٰ کیا اور دوسرے نے ہبہ مع القبض کا تو دعوی رہن اولیٰ ہوگا۔ مگر استحسانا قیاس کی رو سے دعوی ہبہ اولیٰ ہونا چاہیئے۔ کیوں کہ ہبہ مثبت ملک ہے اور رہن مثبت ملک نہیں ہے۔ وجہ استحسان یہ ہے کہ مقبوض بحکم رہن مضمون ہوتا ہے۔ اور مقبوض بحکم ہبہ مضمون نہیں ہوتا ۔ اور عقد تبرع سے قوی تر ہے۔ لہذا دعوی رہن اولیٰ ہوگا۔ مگر یہ اس وقت ہے جب ہبہ بلاعوض کا دعوی ہو اور اگر دعوی ہبہ بشرط العوض ہو تو پھر دعویٰ ہبہ اولیٰ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ ہبہ بشرط العوض انتہاء بیع ہوتا ہے اور بیع رہن سے اولیٰ ہے۔
And if one of them claims pledge with possession and the other claims gift with possession, and both produce evidence, then pledge is more deserving. This is istihsan, while by qiyas gift is more deserving because it establishes ownership and pledge does not. The face of istihsan is that the property taken under pledge is guaranteed, while under gift it is not guaranteed, and a contract of guarantee is stronger. Contrary to gift with condition of compensation, for it is ultimately a sale, and sale is more deserving than pledge because sale is a contract of guarantee that establishes ownership in form and meaning, while pledge does not establish it except at the time of destruction, in meaning not form. So also is gift with condition of compensation.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage deals with competing claims: one person claims the property was given to him as a pledge (rahn) with possession, another claims it was given as a gift (hibah) with possession. Both have evidence. The ruling, by istihsan (juristic preference), is that the claim of pledge is preferred. This is contrary to qiyas (analogy), which would prefer gift because gift establishes ownership, while pledge only creates a security interest. The reason for istihsan is that property held as pledge is guaranteed (mamnun) — the pledgee is responsible for it if it is lost — while a donee is not responsible for loss. Since a contract of guarantee is stronger than a gratuitous contract, the pledge claim takes precedence. An exception: if the gift is with a condition of compensation (hiba bi-shart al-'iwad), essentially a sale, then the gift (sale) claim is preferred over pledge, because sale establishes ownership in both form and meaning, whereas pledge only establishes ownership in meaning (when the property is destroyed and the debt remains). Thus, the underlying principle is that a contract with guarantee (daman) is stronger than one without.

Key Terms
رَهْن (rahn): Pledge; a property given as security for a debt, where possession is transferred but ownership remains with the pledgor.
ضَمَان (daman): Guarantee; liability for loss or damage of property in one's possession.
اِسْتِحْسَان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a ruling that is more equitable or practical.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
One person claims a property was given to him as a pledge (rahn), another as a gift (hibah). Both have possession and evidence. Which claim is preferred by istihsan?
A) The gift claim is preferred by istihsan.
B) The pledge claim is preferred by istihsan.
C) They are equal.
D) Neither claim is valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to prefer a pledge claim over a gift claim because pledge involves guarantee, making the contract stronger.

Multiple Claims with Dates: Rules of Evidentiary Preference
Rulings on preference among multiple claimants with evidence including dates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rules of evidentiary preference when multiple outsiders present evidence with and without dates in property disputes.

Core Idea
Earlier date wins; undated evidence yields equal division.

This passage from al-Hidayah outlines the Hanafi approach to resolving property disputes when two outsiders present evidence of ownership. The key factor is whether the evidence includes a date, and if both are dated, which one is earlier. The reasoning is based on proving prior ownership without contest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Determine if the claimants are outsiders (kharij) or possessors. The rules here apply only when both are outsiders (i.e., not the current possessor).
If one is the possessor, different rules may apply.
2
Examine the evidence dates
Check if each evidence includes a date (tariikh) of the transaction. Evidence may be dated or undated.
3
Apply dating rule when both dated and same seller
If both claim purchase from the same person (other than the possessor) and both have dates, the earlier date wins because it proves ownership at a time when there was no contest.
The later claimant must have derived from the earlier or from someone else, but the earlier is stronger.
4
Apply dating rule when each from a different seller
If each claimant proves purchase from a different seller, and both have dates, they are equal. Each establishes ownership for his own seller, so it becomes as if both sellers are present; the judge gives each buyer a choice (e.g., to take from his seller).
This ruling treats the claims as independent and simultaneous.
5
Apply rule when only one evidence is dated
If only one evidence is dated and the other is undated, the property is divided equally. Dating one does not prove it is earlier; the undated evidence could be earlier.
Equal division is a default when priority cannot be determined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ أَقَامَ الْخَارِجَانِ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى الْمِلْكِ وَالتَّارِيخِ فَصَاحِبُ التَّارِيخِ الْأَقْدَمِ أَولَى لِأَنَّهُ أَثْبَتَ أَنَّهُ أَوَّلُ الْمَالِكَيْنِ فَلَا يُتَلَقَّى الْمِلْكَ إِلَّا مِنْ جِهَتِهِ وَلَمْ يَتَلَقَّ الْآخَرُ مِنْهُ قَالَ وَلَوْ ادْعَيَا الشَّرَاءَ مِنْ وَاحِدٍ مَعْنَاهُ مِنْ غَيْرِ صَاحِبِ الْيَدِ وَأَقَامَا الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى تَارِيخَيْنِ فَالْاَوَّلُ أَولَى لِمَا بَيَّنَّا أَنَّهُ أَثْبَتَه فِي وَقْتٍ لَا مُنَازِعَ لَهُ فِيهِ وَإِنْ أَقَامَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى الشَّرَاءِ مِنْ أَخَرَ وَذَكَرا تَارِيحًا فَهُمَا سَوَاءٌ لِأَنَّهُمَا يُثْبِتَانِ الْمِلْكَ لِبَائِعِهِمَا فَيَصِيرُ كَانَّهُمَا حَضَرَا ثُمَّ يُخَيَّرُ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا كَمَا ذَكَرْنَا مِنْ قَبْلُ وَلَوُوُقِّتَتْ إِحْدَى الْبَيِّنَتَيْنِ وَقْتًا وَلَمْ تُوَفَّتِ الْآخَرُ قُضِيَ بَيْنَهُمَا نِصْفَيْنِ لِأَنَّ تَوْقِيْتَ إِحْدَهُمَا لَا يَدُلُ عَلَى تَقَدُّمِ الْمِلْكِ لِجَوَازِ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْآخَرُ أَقْدَمَ
And if the two outsiders produce evidence of ownership with dates, then the one with the earlier date is more deserving, because he proves that he is the first owner, so ownership is only taken from his side, and the other did not take from him. He said: And if both claim purchase from one person, meaning from someone other than the possessor, and they produce evidence with two dates, then the earlier is more deserving, as we explained: he proved ownership at a time when there was no contest. And if each of them produces evidence of purchase from another [seller] and mention a date, then they are equal, because they establish ownership for their respective sellers, so it becomes as if both sellers are present, then each of them is given a choice as we mentioned before. And if one of the two evidences is dated and the other is not, then judgment is given between them in halves, because dating one does not indicate priority of ownership, as it is possible that the other is earlier.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage provides several rulings regarding multiple claimants with evidence and dates. First: two third parties (not the possessor) both claim ownership of the property. If they produce evidence with dates, the one with the earlier date wins because he proves he was the first owner, and the later owner must have acquired from him or from someone else, but the later claim is weaker. Second: if both parties claim purchase from the same seller (someone other than the current possessor) with different dates, again the earlier date is preferred for similar reasoning—he established ownership when no one contested. Third: if each claimant claims purchase from a different seller, and both mention dates, they are equal. The reasoning is that each proves ownership for his own seller, not for himself, so it becomes as if both sellers are present. Then the judge gives each buyer a choice (to take from his seller or something). This is opaque but the ruling is equality. Fourth: if only one evidence has a date and the other does not, the property is divided equally (half each) because the undated evidence could be for a later or earlier period; the date does not prove priority.

Key Terms
خَارِج (kharij): An outsider; a person who is not the current possessor (yad) of the property.
تَارِيخ (tariikh): Date; the time when the transaction (purchase/gift) occurred, used to determine priority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two outsiders claim ownership of property with evidence. One evidence is dated 2020, the other has no date. How is the property decided?
A) The dated evidence wins entirely.
B) The undated evidence wins.
C) The property is split equally.
D) The date is irrelevant; both are rejected.

Key Takeaway
In property disputes among outsiders, an earlier date on evidence grants priority; undated evidence yields equal share.

Sale Before Possession in Real Estate: Shaykhayn vs Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi rulings on property disputes with dates in real estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Shaykhayn’s rule that sale before possession is valid in real estate to resolve a dating dispute, and state how Muhammad’s disagreement changes the outcome.

Core Idea
Sale before possession is valid for real estate but not for movables — this distinction decides who owns disputed property.

This lesson dives into a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: the validity of selling real estate before the buyer takes possession. The issue arises when two claimants each present a dated sale contract but neither proves delivery. The ruling depends on whether pre-possession sale is permitted. The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) allow it; Imam Muhammad disallows it. The passage from Al-Hidayah examines several sub-cases, showing how this core difference yields different outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed rule
Determine whether sale before possession is valid for real estate. This is the pivot of the whole case.
Shaykhayn: valid; Muhammad: invalid.
2
Analyze the first sub-case: no possession, earlier date with non-possessor
Both have dates but no possession. Non-possessor's date is earlier. According to Shaykhayn, the possessor wins because the non-possessor is deemed to have sold to him before possession. According to Muhammad, the non-possessor wins.
This is the key difference.
3
Analyze the second sub-case: both prove possession
When both prove delivery, both sales are valid even according to Muhammad, because possession cures the defect. So the possessor wins unanimously.
Possession is decisive here.
4
Analyze the third sub-case: possessor has earlier date, no possession proven
Possessor's date is earlier, non-possessor's later. Both agree that the non-possessor wins because the possessor is deemed to have sold after taking possession and either did not deliver or got the property back.
Here the earlier date of possessor does not help him.
5
State the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is that in the first sub-case, the property goes to the possessor under Shaykhayn but to the non-possessor under Muhammad.
Mufta bihi is the Shaykhayn's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ وَقَتَتِ الْبَيِّنَتَانِ فِي الْعِقَارِ وَلَمْ يَنْتَا قَبْضًا وَوَقْتُ الْخَارِج أَسْبَقُ يُقْضَى لِصَاحِبِ الْيَدِ عِنْدَهُمَا فَيُجْعَلُ كَأَنَّ الْخَارِجَ اشْتَرَى أَوَّلًا ثُمَّ بَاعَ قَبْلَ الْقَبْضِ مِنْ صَاحِبِ الْيَدِ وَهُوَ جَائِزٌ فِي الْعِقَارِ عِنْدَهُمَا وَعِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٌ يُقْضَى لِلْخَارِجِ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَصِحُ بَيْعُهُ قَبْلَ الْقَبْضِ فَبَقِيَ عَلَى مِلْكِهِ وَإِنْ أَثْبَتَا قَبْضًا يُقْضَى لِصَاحِبِ الْيَدِ لِأَنَّ الْبَيْعَيْنِ جَائِزَانِ عَلَى الْقَوْلَيْنِ وَإِذَا كَانَ وَقْتُ صَاحِبِ الْيَدِ أَسْبَقُ يُقْضَى لِلْخَارِجِ فِي الْوَجْهَيْنِ فَيُجْعَلُ كَأَنَّهُ اشْتَرَاهُ ذُو الْيَدِ وَقَبْضَ ثُمَّ بَاعَ وَلَمْ يُسَلَّمْ أَوْ سَلَّمَ ثُمَّ وَصَلَ إِلَيْهِ بِسَبَبٍ أَخَرَ
And if the two evidences specify a date in real estate and they do not prove possession, and the date of the non-possessor is earlier, judgment is given to the possessor according to them (Shaykhayn). So it is made as if the non-possessor bought first, then sold before possession to the possessor, and that is permissible in real estate according to them. And according to Muhammad, judgment is given to the non-possessor because his sale before possession is not valid, so it remains in his ownership. And if they prove possession, judgment is given to the possessor because both sales are valid according to both positions. And when the date of the possessor is earlier, judgment is given to the non-possessor in both ways, so it is made as if the possessor bought and took possession, then sold and did not deliver, or delivered and then it came to him by another cause.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage covers several sub-cases involving real estate claims when dates are known. The key point of dispute is that the Shaykhayn allow the sale of real estate before the buyer takes possession, while Muhammad does not. Consequently, the rulings differ. First sub-case: both evidences have dates but do not prove possession; the non-possessor's date is earlier. According to the Shaykhayn, the property goes to the possessor because the likely sequence is that the non-possessor bought first, then sold to the possessor before taking possession himself – which is valid for real estate in their view. Muhammad gives it to the non-possessor because the sale to the possessor before possession is invalid, so the property remains with the non-possessor. Second sub-case: both prove possession. Then both agree that judgment goes to the possessor, since both sales and deliveries are valid under both schools; the later delivery to the possessor confirms his ownership. Third sub-case: the possessor's date is earlier. Then both agree that judgment goes to the non-possessor, because the possessor is deemed to have bought first and taken possession, then sold to the non-possessor but either failed to deliver or delivered and got it back by another reason.

Key Terms
عقار (iqaar): Immovable property (real estate), such as houses or land.
وقت (waqat): To specify a date or time in the evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over real estate with dated evidences but no possession proven, if the non-possessor's date is earlier, who gets the property according to Imam Muhammad?
A) The possessor.
B) The non-possessor.
C) The property is divided.
D) The evidences cancel.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, sale of real estate before possession is valid (Shaykhayn's view), which means that when dated evidences conflict without possession, the current possessor prevails.

Wall Ownership: Beams vs. Hanging Pot
Ruling on wall ownership with beams vs. hanging pot

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of priority based on active use (isti'mal) over mere attachment (ta'alluq) to determine wall ownership in a dispute.

Core Idea
Active use (beams/connection) gives ownership rights; passive hanging objects are ignored.

In property disputes over walls, the Hanafi madhhab distinguishes between those who actively use the wall (e.g., by placing beams or interlocking bricks) and those who merely attach objects (e.g., hanging a water pot). This lesson unpacks the ruling from al-Hidayah, showing how qiyas to a dispute over an animal with a load versus a hanging pot establishes the principle that use, not mere attachment, determines priority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed property and claimants
There is a wall. Claimant A has beams (judhun) resting on it or has his building structurally connected (ittisal). Claimant B has a water pot (haradi) hanging on it.
The wall is the subject of a claim (da'wa) between two parties.
2
State the explicit ruling
The wall belongs to the owner of the beams or connection. The haradi is legally insignificant (laysat bi-shay').
This is the direct ruling from the text.
3
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The beam owner is a 'user' (sahib isti'mal) while the pot owner is only an 'attacher' (sahib ta'alluq). Use creates a stronger claim.
This distinction is key to the Hanafi position.
4
Trace the qiyas (analogy)
The situation is analogous to a disputed animal: one has a heavy load (haml) directly on the animal, the other has a water pot (kuz) hanging from it. The load owner's claim is stronger because he uses the animal for transport.
The analogy shows that active use (haml) mirrors the beams' function, while the hanging pot mirrors the haradi.
5
Apply the subsidiary cases
If only one has haradi and the other nothing: the wall is shared. If each has three beams: shared. More than three beams do not affect the outcome.
These illustrate the rule that without a clear user advantage, the wall becomes joint property.

قَالَ وَإِذَا كَانَ الْحَائِطُ لِرَجُلٍ عَلَيْهِ جُدُوعٌ أَوْ مُتَّصِلٌ بِبِنَائِهِ وَلِلْآخَرِ عَلَيْهِ هَرَادِيُّ فَهُوَ لِصَاحِبِ الْجُذُوعِ وَالْإِتِصَالِ وَالْهَرَادِيُّ لَيْسَتْ بِشَيْءٍ لِأَنَّ صَاحِبَ الْجُذُوعِ صَاحِبُ اسْتِعْمَالٍ وَالْآخَرُ صَاحِبُ تَعَلُّقٍ فَصَارَ كَدَابَّةٍ تَنَازَعَا فِيهَا وَلِأَحَدِهِمَا عَلَيْهَا حَمْلٌ وَلِلْآخَرِ كُوزٌ مُعَلَّقٌ وَالْمُرَادُ بِالْإِتِصَالِ مُدَاخَلَةُ لَبِنِ جِدَارِهِ فِيهِ وَلَبِنُ هَذَا فِي جِدَارِهِ وَقَدْ يُسَمَّى إِتِّصَالَ تَرْبِيعٍ وَهَذَا شَاهِدٌ ظَاهِرٌ لِصَاحِبِهِ لِأَنَّ بَعْضَ بِنَائِهِ عَلَى بَعْضٍ هَذَا الْحَائِطِ وَقَوْلُهُ الْهَرَادِيُّ لَيْسَتْ بِشَيْءٍ يَدُلُّ عَلَى أَنَّهُ لَا اعْتِبَارَ لِلْهَرَادِي أَصْلًا وَكَذَا الْبَوَارِي لِأَنَّ الْحَائِطَ لَا يُبْنَى لَهُمَا أَصْلًا حَتَّى لَوْ تَنَازَعَا فِي حَائِطٍ وَلَا حَدِّ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَيْهِ هَرَادِي وَلَيْسَ لِلْآخَرِ عَلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ فَهُوَ بَيْنَهُمَا وَلَوْ كَانَ لِكُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا عَلَيْهِ جُذُوعٌ ثَلَاثَةٌ فَهُوَ بَيْنَهُمَا لِاسْتِوَائِهِمَا وَلَا مُعْتَبَرَ بِالْأَكْثَرِ مِنْهَا بَعْدَ الثَّلَاثَةِ
He said: If a wall belongs to a man and upon it are beams (judhun) or it is connected to his building, and another has upon it a water pot (haradi), then it (the wall) belongs to the owner of the beams and the connection, and the water pot is nothing. Because the owner of the beams is the one who uses (the wall) and the other is one who merely attaches (something). So it is like an animal they dispute over: one has a load upon it and the other a hanging water pot. What is meant by connection is that the bricks of his wall interlock into it (the other's wall) and the bricks of this one are in his wall, and it is sometimes called 'connection of squaring' (ittisal tarbi'), and this is an apparent evidence for its owner because part of his building is upon part of this wall. And his statement 'the water pot is nothing' indicates that the water pot is of no consideration at all, and likewise the matting (bawari) because the wall is not built for them at all. So if they dispute over a wall and one of them has upon it a water pot and the other has nothing upon it, then it is shared between them. And if each of them has upon it three beams, then it is shared between them because they are equal, and no consideration is given to more than three.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This is a legal ruling from the chapter on claims. The text presents a case: a wall exists, one man has beams (judhun) resting on it or has his building structurally connected to it (ittisal), while another man has only a water pot (haradi) hanging on it. The ruling: the wall belongs to the beam/connection owner; the haradi is legally insignificant (ليس بشيء). The reasoning (ta'lil) is based on qiyas: the beam owner is 'the one who uses' (sahib isti'mal) the wall, while the haradi owner is only 'the one who attaches' (sahib ta'alluq). This is analogized to a dispute over an animal where one has a heavy load (haml) directly on it and the other has a water pot (kuz) hanging from it; the load owner has a stronger claim because he uses the animal more directly. The text clarifies that ittisal means structural interlocking of bricks between two walls ('mudakhalat labin'), which is called ittigal tarbi' (connection of squaring). This interlocking serves as apparent evidence (shahid zahir) that the wall belongs to the one whose building is integrated. Then the text states that haradi and similarly matting (bawari) are not considered because a wall is not built for such attachments. However, if only one has haradi and the other has nothing, the wall is shared (between them). This is a subsidiary ruling: when neither has priority of use, they are equal. Another subsidiary: if each has three beams, the wall is shared; and more than three beams do not tip the balance. The Hanafi madhhab holds that the active user prevails over the passive attacher, and mere attachment without use gives no ownership right.

Key Terms
جُذُوع (judhun): Beams or rafters of a building placed on a wall
هَرَادِي (haradi): A water pot or earthenware vessel hung on a wall
اتصال (ittisal): Structural connection where bricks of one wall interlock with another wall
بَوَارِي (bawari): Matting or woven screens placed on a wall

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a wall has beams from one person and a hanging water pot from another, who gets the wall?
A) The owner of the water pot
B) The wall is shared equally
C) The owner of the beams
D) It depends on which is more valuable

Key Takeaway
Active use (beams/structural connection) gives ownership rights over a wall; mere passive attachment (hanging pot, matting) has no legal effect in the Hanafi madhhab.
