Disputes over Beams and Connection: Two Riwayat
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wall ownership with two riwayat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two transmitted riwayat on wall ownership disputes involving beams and connection, and apply the Hanafi principles of tasarruf and yad to evaluate which evidence is stronger.

Core Idea
In wall ownership disputes, active use (tasarruf) outweighs mere possession (yad), but connection can make walls one unit.

This passage dives into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about shared walls. When one party has fewer than three beams and the other has three, there are two transmitted rulings: one gives the entire wall to the owner of three beams, the other divides it proportionally. A second dispute arises when one has beams and the other has structural connection (ittisal). Understanding the weight given to tasarruf versus yad is critical for a qadi adjudicating such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disputes
The passage first addresses a dispute when one party has fewer than three beams and the other has three. Then a second dispute when one has beams and the other has connection. Both are within the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the first dispute: two transmissions
One riwaya says the wall goes to the owner of three beams, the other gets only the spot for his beam. Another riwaya says each gets what is under his wood, with the intermediate space shared. Understand the reasoning (talil) for each.
3
Understand the qiyas argument
The passage states that qiyas would suggest equal sharing because the number of beams is not a strong proof. But the preferred reasoning for the first transmission is that the wall is built for many beams, so zāhir (apparent circumstance) favors the many-beam owner.
4
Analyze the second dispute: beams vs. connection
Two views: the beam owner is more entitled due to tasarruf (active use) being stronger than yad (possession). The second view says the connection owner is more entitled because the walls become one building.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Note that al-Tahawi and al-Jurjani prefer the connection owner view. Consider how a qadi would apply these principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ كَانَ جُذُوعُ أَحَدِهِمَا أَقَلَّ مِنْ ثَلَاثَةٍ فَهُوَ لِصَاحِبِ الثَّلَاثَةِ وَلِلْآخَرِ مَوْضِعُ جُذْعِهِ فِي رِوَايَةٍ وَفِي رِوَايَةٍ لِكُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مَا تَحْتَ خَشَبِهِ ثُمَّ قِيلَ مَا بَيْنَ الْخَشَبِ إِلَى الْخَشَبِ بَيْنَهُمَا وَقِيلَ عَلَى قَدْرِ خَشَبِهِمَا وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَيْنَهُمَا نِصْفَيْنِ لِأَنَّهُ لَا مُعْتَبَرَ بِالْكَثْرَةِ فِي نَفْسِ الْحُجَّةِ وَوَجْهُ الثَّانِي أَنَّ الِاسْتِعْمَالَ مِنْ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ بِقَدَرِ خَشَبَتِهِ وَجْهُ الْأَوَّلِ أَنَّ الْحَائِطَ يُبْنَى لِوَضْعِ كَثِيرِ الْجُذُوعِ دُونَ الْوَاحِدِ وَالْمُثَنَّى فَكَانَ الظَّاهِرُ شَاهِدًا لِصَاحِبِ الْكَثِيرِ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ يَبْقَى لَهُ حَقُّ الْوَضْعِ لِأَنَّ الظَّاهِرَ لَيْسَ بِحُجَّةٍ فِي اسْتِحْقَاقِ يَدِهِ وَلَوْ كَانَ لِأَحَدِهِمَا جُذُوعٌ وَلِلْآخَرِ إِتِّصَالٌ فَالْأَوَّلُ أَوْلَى وَيُرْوَى أَنَّ الثَّانِيَ أَوْلَى وَجْهُ الْأَوَّلِ أَنَّ لِصَاحِبِ الْجُذُوعِ التَّصَرُّفَ وَلِصَاحِبِ الْإِتِّصَالِ الْيَدَ وَالتَّصَرُّفُ أَقْوَى وَجْهُ الثَّانِي أَنَّ الْحَائِطَيْنِ بِالْإِتِّصَالِ يَصِيرَانِ كَبِنَاءٍ وَاحِدٍ وَمِنْ ضَرُورَةِ الْقَضَاءِ لَهُ بِبَعْضِهِ الْقَضَاءُ بِكُلِّهِ ثُمَّ يَبْقَى لِلْآخَرِ حَقُّ وَضْعِ جُذُوعِهِ لِمَا قُلْنَا وَهَذِهِ رِوَايَةُ الطَّحَاوِيِّ وَصَحَّحَهَا الْجُرْجَانِيُّ
And if the beams of one of them are less than three, then the wall is for the owner of three, and for the other is the place for his beam, in one transmission. In another transmission, for each of them is what is under his wood. Then it is said: what is between the wood to the wood is shared between them, and it is said: according to the measure of their wood. And the analogy is that it is half between them because no consideration is given to abundance in the evidence itself. The reasoning for the second is that the use from each is according to the amount of his wood. The reasoning for the first is that the wall is built for placing many beams, not for one or two, so the apparent circumstance is evidence for the owner of the many, except that he retains the right to place because the apparent circumstance is not proof for establishing possession of the wall. And if one of them has beams and the other has connection, then the first is more entitled, and it is transmitted that the second is more entitled. The reasoning for the first is that the beam owner has disposal and the connection owner has physical possession, and disposal is stronger. The reasoning for the second is that the two walls become like one building through connection, and necessarily, ruling for him with part of it requires ruling for him with all of it; then the other retains the right to place his beams as we have said. And this is the transmission of al-Tahawi, and al-Jurjani has deemed it sound.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents two intra-Hanafi disputes on subsidiary rulings about walls. First dispute: When one person has fewer than three beams and the other has exactly three beams. In one riwaya (from the book al-Iqrar), the wall goes entirely to the owner of three beams, and the one with fewer only gets the spot under his beam. In another riwaya (from the book al-Da'wa), each gets the area under his own wood, and the space between beams is either shared equally or divided proportionally. The text then discusses the qiyas: the qiyas would suggest the wall is shared equally because the number of beams is not a strong proof in itself. However, the preferred reasoning supports the first riwaya: the wall is built for many beams, so the apparent circumstance suggests the wall belongs to the one with many beams, but the other's right to place his beam remains because the apparent circumstance does not establish possession. Second dispute: If one has beams and the other has structural connection, which gives stronger claim? The first view says beam owner is more entitled because he has tasarruf (active use), while the connection owner only has yad (possession), and tasarruf is stronger. The second view from al-Tahawi's transmission, deemed sound by al-Jurjani, says connection owner is more entitled because the walls become one unit through connection, so ruling for him with part implies ruling for him with the whole, but he still acknowledges the other's right to place beams. This shows the Hanafi school's internal diversity on the weight of different types of evidence.

Key Terms
رِوَايَة (riwaya): A transmitted statement or ruling from the Imams of the school, often through different books like al-Iqrar and al-Da'wa
تَصَرُّف (tasarruf): Active use or disposal of property, considered stronger than mere possession (yad) in proving ownership
يَد (yad): Physical possession or control, often indicates ownership in property disputes but is weaker than active use

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to one riwaya in this passage, if one person has two beams on a wall and another has three beams, who gets the wall?
A) The one with two beams
B) The wall is shared equally
C) The one with three beams, and the other only gets a spot for his beam
D) The wall goes to the one who built it first

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi property disputes over walls, the number of beams and the presence of connection affect ownership, with tasarruf generally stronger than yad, but connection can flip the priority.

Wall Dispute with Unequal Beams and Attachment
Hanafi dispute over wall ownership with beams and attachment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi narrations on wall ownership when beams are unequal, explain the rationale behind each, and apply the preferred ruling (mufta bihi) to a dispute involving beams or attachment.

Core Idea
Ownership of a shared wall depends on usage and custom; number of beams is evidence but not absolute proof.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya addresses a common property dispute: when two neighbors share a wall, but one has placed many beams and the other has few or only an attached wall. The Hanafi school presents multiple narrations reflecting different legal reasoning (qiyas vs istihsan). Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to weigh evidence in fiqh and appreciate how custom (urf) influences rulings. The passage also includes a comparison with the Shafi'i position and authenticates one narration over another.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two neighbors share a wall. One has three or more beams (juzu'), the other has fewer than three. The dispute is about ownership of the wall.
The scenario also includes the case where one has beams and the other has an attached wall (ittisal).
2
Examine the first narration
First narration (from al-Mabsut, book of Iqrar): the entire wall belongs to the one with three beams; the other only has the right to place his beam on the wall.
Rationale: walls are built for many beams, so the apparent usage supports the one with many.
3
Examine the second narration
Second narration (from al-Mabsut, book of Da'wa): the wall is divided according to the number of beams each has. This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) according to al-Jurjani.
Rationale: usage of each is according to his beams, so division proportionate to beam count is fair.
4
Understand the attachment case
If one has beams and the other has an attached wall (ittisal), the one with beams is preferred because tasarruf (disposition) is stronger than mere yad (possession). However, al-Tahawi narrates the opposite: the one with attachment is preferred.
The preferred view is the one with beams, as tasarruf is stronger than possession.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Shafi'i school holds that qiyas (analogy) requires equal division regardless of beam count, because the number of beams does not affect the proof of ownership.
Hanafi response: custom (urf) and istihsan override qiyas in this case, because walls are built for many beams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تین دھنیاں ہوں تو دیوار دونوں میں مساوی ہوگی۔ کیونکہ دونوں کا تصرف برابر ہے اور تین کے بعد زیادہ کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہے۔
وَإِنْ كَانَ جُذُوعِ أَحَدِهِمَا أَقَلُّ مِنْ ثَلَاثَةٍ فَهُوَ لِصَاحِبِ الثَّلَاثَةِ وَلِلْآخَرِ مَوْضِعُ جُذْعِهِ فِي رِوَايَةٍ وَفِي رِوَايَةٍ لِكُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مَا تَحْتَ خَشَبِهِ ثُمَّ قِيْلُ مَا بَيْنَ الْخَشَبِ إِلَى الْخَشَبِ بَيْنَهُمَا وَقِيلَ عَلَى قَدْرِ خَشْبِهِمَا وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَيْنَهُمَا نِصْفَيْنِ لِأَنَّهُ لَا مُعْتَبَرَ بِالْكَثْرَةِ فِي نَفْسِ الْحُجَّةِ وَوَجْهُ الثَّانِي أَنَّ الْإِسْتِعْمَالَ مِنْ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ بِقَدَرِ خَشْبَتِهِ وَجُهُ الْاَوَّلِ اَنَّ الْحَائِط يُبْنَى لِوَضْعِ كَثِيرِ الْجُذُوعِ دُونَ الْوَاحِدِ وَالْمُثْنى فَكَانَ الظَّاهِرُ شَاهِدًا لِصَاحِبِ الْكَثِيرِ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ يَبْقَى لَهُ حَقُّ الْوَضْعِ لِاَنَّ الظَّاهِرَ لَيْسَ بِحُجَّةٍ فِي اسْتِحْقَاقِ يَدِهِ وَلَوْ كَانَ لِأَحَدِهِمَا جُدُوعٌ وَلِلْآخَرِ إِتِصَالٌ فَالْاَوَّلُ أَوْلى وَيُروى أَنَّ الثَّانِى اَوْلى وَجْهُ الْأَوَّلِ أَنَّ لِصَاحِبِ الْجُذُوعِ التَّصَرُّف وَلِصَاحِبِ الْإِتِصَالِ الْيَدِ وَالتَّصَرُّف أقْوَى وَوَجْهُ الثَّانِي أَنَّ الْحَائِطَيْنِ بِالْإِتِصَالِ يَصِيرَانِ كَبِنَاءٍ وَاحِدٍ وَمِنْ ضُرُورَةِ الْقَضَاءِ لَهُ بِبَعْضِهِ الْقَضَاءُ بِكُلِهِ ثُمَّ يَبْقَى لِلْآخَرِ حَقٌّ وَضْعِ جُدُوعِهِ لِمَا قُلْنَا وَهَذِهِ رِوَايَةٌ الطَّحَاوِي وَصَحْحَهَا الْجُرْجَانِيُّ
ترجمہ اور اگر ان میں سے ایک کی دھنیاں تین سے کم ہوں تو دیوار تین والے کی ہوگی اور دوسرے کے لئے دھنیوں کی جگہ ہوگی۔ ایک روایت میں اور ایک روایت میں ہر ایک کے لئے لکڑیاں رکھنے کی جگہ ہوگی۔ پھر کہا گیا ہے کہ ان کی دھنیوں کے بقدر ہوگی۔ اور قیاس یہی ہے کہ دیوار دونوں میں نصف نصف ہو۔ اس واسطے کہ نفس حجت میں کثرت کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہے۔ دوسری روایت کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ ہر ایک کی طرف سے استعمال اس کی لکڑیوں کی بقدر ہے۔ اور پہلی روایت کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ دیوار بہت سی دھنیاں رکھنے کے لئے بنائی جاتی ہے نہ کہ ایک دو کے لئے ۔ پس ظاہر حال شاہد ہے۔ زائد والے کے لئے لیکن کم والے کو بھی رکھنے کا حق رہے گا۔ کیونکہ ظاہر حال قبضہ کے استحقاق میں حجت نہیں ہے۔ اگر ایک کی دھنیاں رکھی ہوں اور دوسرے کے لئے صرف اتصال ہو تو اول اولیٰ ہوگا۔ اور یہ بھی مروی ہے کہ ثانی اولی ہو گا۔ اوّل کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ دھنیوں والے کو تصرف حاصل ہے اور اتصال والے کو قبضہ اور قبضہ تصرف سے سے قوی تر ہے اور ثانی روایت کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ دونوں دیواریں بذریعہ اتصال بمنزلہ ایک عمارت کے ہو گئیں اور اس کے حق میں بعض دیوار کا حکم ہونا بالضرور مقتضی ہے کہ کل کا حکم اس کے لئے ہے۔ پھر حق رہے گا۔ دوسرے کو اپنی دھنیاں رکھنے کا۔ اور یہ دوسری روایت امام طحاوی کی ہے۔ اس کو فقیہہ جرجانی نے صحیح کہا ہے۔ تشریح قوله في رواية یہ روایت اصل یعنی مبسوط کی کتاب الاقرار کی ہے۔ حيث قال فيه " الـحـالـط كله لصاحب الا جذاع ولصاحب القليل ماتحت جذعہ محیط میں اسی کو اصح کہا ہے۔ دوسری روایت مبسوط کی کتاب الدعویٰ کی ہے ۔ حيث قال فيه " ان الحائط بينهما على قدر الا جذا اس روایت کے مطابق مابين الخشبتین کی بابت مشائخ کا اختلاف ہے۔ بعض نے کہا ہے کہ مشتین کی درمیانی جگہ دونوں میں مساوی ہوگی۔ اور بعض نے کہا ہے کہ ہر ایک کو اس کی دھنیوں کے بقدر ملے گا۔ پھر یہ بنی بر استحسان ہے۔ مقتضائے قیاس یہ ہے کہ دیوار دونوں میں برابر مشترک ہو۔ کیونکہ جو چیز حجت ہے۔ اس کی کثرت کا اعتبار نہیں ہوتا۔ یہ امام ابوحنیفہ سے ایک روایت بھی ہے۔ اور امام شافعی واحد کے قول کا قیاس بھی یہی ہے۔ ثانی یعنی کتاب الدعویٰ والی روایت کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ
باب ما يدعيه الرجلاد
۳۲ ہدایہ جلد - یاز دہم
besturdubooks.wo.com
دیوار کا ہر ایک کے استعمال میں آنا اس کی دھنوں کے بقدر ہے۔ اور استحقاق استعمال ہی کے لحاظ سے ہوتا ہے۔
If there are three beams, the wall will be shared equally between the two. Because both have equal use, and three or more does not matter. And if one of them has beams less than three, then in one narration the wall belongs to the one with three, and the other has the place for his beam. In another narration, each gets what is under his wood. Then it was said: what is between the woods is between them. And it was said: according to the measure of their woods. And analogy requires that it be half each because multitude is not considered in the proof itself. The face of the second (narration) is that the use of each is according to his wood. The face of the first is that the wall is built for placing many beams, not for one or two. So the apparent (usage) supports the one with many, except that the other retains the right to place (his beam) because the apparent is not proof for possession. And if one of them has beams and the other has attachment (of his wall), the first is preferred, and it is narrated that the second is preferred. The face of the first is that the one with beams has disposition (tasarruf) and the one with attachment has possession (yad), and disposition is stronger. The face of the second is that with attachment the two walls become like one building, and from the necessity of adjudicating for him part of it, adjudication for the whole follows. Then the other retains the right to place his beams as we said. This is a narration of al-Tahawi, which al-Jurjani declared authentic. Translation: And if one of them has beams less than three, the wall belongs to the one with three, and the other has the place for his beams. In one narration, and in another narration, each has the place for his wood. Then it was said: it will be according to the measure of their woods. And analogy is that the wall is half-half between them, because in the proof itself multiplicity is not considered. The reason for the second narration is that the use of each is according to his wood. And the reason for the first narration is that the wall is built for placing many beams, not for one or two. So the apparent (state) supports the one with many. But the one with few retains the right to place (his beam), because the apparent is not proof for possession. If one of them has beams and the other has attachment, the first is preferred. And it is also narrated that the second is preferred. The reason for the first is that the one with beams has disposition, and the one with attachment has possession, and possession is stronger than disposition. And the reason for the second narration is that by attachment the two walls become one building, and from the necessity of adjudicating for him part, adjudication for the whole is necessary. Then the other retains the right to place his beams. And this second narration is from Imam al-Tahawi, which al-Jurjani declared authentic. Commentary: 'Qawlahu fi riwayah'—this narration is from the original (Mabsut) book of Iqrar. He said there: the wall all belongs to the one with beams, and the one with few gets what is under his beam. That is also declared more correct. The second narration is from the Mabsut book of Da'wa. He said there: the wall is between them according to the beams. According to this narration, there is a dispute among the mashayikh about the space between the pieces of wood. Some said the space between is shared equally. Some said each gets according to his beams. Then this is based on istihsan. The requirement of analogy is that the wall is equally shared between them, because in proof multiplicity is not considered. This is also a narration from Imam Abu Hanifah. And the analogy of Imam Shafi'i's single saying is the same. The reason for the second (the book of Da'wa narration) is that the wall is used by each according to his beams, and entitlement is based on use.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding a shared wall when neighbors have different numbers of beams (juzu’) resting on it. The passage is an inter- and intra-madhhab dispute. The core issue: if two neighbors share a wall, and one has three or more beams while the other has fewer than three, who owns the wall? The text mentions multiple narrations and positions. Analogy (qiyas) would divide the wall equally between them, because the number of beams does not affect the proof of ownership. However, custom and usage (istihsan) may support giving the wall to the one with more beams, since walls are typically built for many beams, not just one or two. The first narration (from al-Mabsut, book of Iqrar) gives the entire wall to the one with three beams, and the other only the right to place his beam. The second narration (from al-Mabsut, book of Da’wa) divides ownership according to the number of beams. Another scenario: if one has beams and the other has his wall attached (ittisal), the preferred view is that the one with beams has stronger claim because disposition (tasarruf) is stronger than mere possession (yad). But a narration from al-Tahawi prefers the one with attachment, considering the two walls become one building. The commentary explains the basis of each position and notes that the second narration is declared authentic by al-Jurjani. The practical consequence (thamara): ownership and usage rights differ based on which narration is followed. The Hanafi school generally prefers the narration that considers usage and benefit.

Key Terms
جذع / جذوع (juzu’ / judhu’): Beams of wood that rest on the wall to support a roof or upper floor.
اتصال (ittisal): The connection of a neighbor’s wall to the disputed wall, implying shared structure.
تصرف (tasarruf): The act of using and managing the wall, e.g., placing beams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred narration in Ashraf al-Hidaya, how is a shared wall divided when one neighbor has three beams and the other has one beam?
A) The wall belongs entirely to the one with three beams.
B) The wall is divided according to the number of beams each has.
C) The wall is divided equally, half each.
D) The wall belongs to the one with the attached wall.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when beams are unequal, the preferred view divides the wall according to beam count, not equally, based on istihsan and custom.

Courtyard Shared Between Possessors of Rooms
Ruling that courtyard is shared equally despite unequal room ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on division of a shared courtyard when room ownership is unequal, and explain the rationale (ta‘lil) based on equal use.

Core Idea
A shared courtyard is divided equally regardless of room count because both owners have equal right to passage.

Imagine a house with eleven rooms: ten owned by one person, one by another. Who owns the courtyard? The Hanafi school has a clear rule: the courtyard is shared equally. In this lesson, we examine the text from al-Hidayah that lays out this ruling, and we explore the legal reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The text gives a specific case: one owner has ten rooms, another has one room in the same house.
The number of rooms is deliberately unequal to highlight the principle.
2
State the ruling
The courtyard (al-saḥa) is divided half-half (niṣfān) between them.
Arabic: فَالسَّاحَةُ بَيْنَهُمَا نِصْفَانِ
3
Understand the ta‘līl
The reason is that both are equal in its use, which is passing through (al-murūr).
Use includes passage, storing goods, wudu, etc.
4
Extend the principle
The commentary adds that other benefits like storing goods, spilling wudu water, and splitting wood are also equal.
Thus, a single-room owner and a ten-room owner are equal in common use.
5
Note the key terms
الساحة (courtyard) and المرور (passage) are central to this ruling.
Memorize these terms for similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِذَا كَانَتْ دَارٌ مِنْهَا فِي يَدِ رَجُلٍ عَشَرَةُ آبَيَاتٍ وَفِي يَدِ اخَرَ بَيْتٌ فَالسَّاحَةُ بَيْنَهُمَا نِصْفَانِ لِإِسْتِوَائِهِمَا فِي اسْتِعْمَالِهَا وَهُوَ الْمُرُورُ فِيهَا
ترجمہ اگر ایک مکان کے دس کمرے ہوں ایک کے قبضہ میں اور دوسرے کے قبضہ میں صرف ایک کمرہ تو صحن ان میں نصف نصف ہو گا۔ اس کے استعمال میں ان دونوں کے برابر ہونے کی وجہ سے اور وہ اس میں آمد و رفت ہے۔
تشریح... قوله واذا کانت .. ایک مکان میں گیارہ کمرے ہیں۔ جس میں سے دس کمرے ایک شخص کے قبضہ میں ہیں۔ اور ایک کمرہ دوسرے کے قبضہ میں ہے۔ اب اس مکان کے صحن میں دونوں کا جھگڑا ہوا تو صحن دونوں کے درمیان نصف نصف ہوگا۔ کیونکہ محسن کے جو فوائد ہیں۔ (مثلاً گزرنا سامان رکھنا، وضو کا پانی گرانا لکڑی پھاڑنا وغیرہ) ان میں وہ دونوں برابر ہیں۔ پس صاحب بیت ، صاحب منزل، صاحب دار، صاحب بیت واحد اور صاحب بیوت کثیرہ مرور وغیرہ میں برابر ہوں گے۔
He said: And if there is a house in which ten rooms are in the possession of one man, and in the possession of another is one room, then the courtyard is half-half between them because they are equal in its use, which is passing through it. Translation: If a house has ten rooms in the possession of one, and in the possession of another only one room, then the courtyard is half-half between them, due to their equality in its use, and that is passage. Commentary: He said: And if there is a house with eleven rooms. Among them, ten rooms are in the possession of one person, and one room in the possession of another. Now they dispute over the courtyard of the house. Then the courtyard is half-half between them, because the benefits of the place (like passing, storing goods, spilling wudu water, splitting wood, etc.) they are equal in them. So the owner of a house, the owner of a dwelling, the owner of a single room and the owner of many rooms are equal in passage and the like.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling (hukm) stating that even if one owner possesses ten rooms and the other only one, the shared courtyard (saha) is divided equally between them. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that both owners have equal right to use the courtyard for passage and other purposes (e.g., storing goods, performing wudu). The use of the courtyard is not proportional to the number of rooms owned. The commentary clarifies that the benefits of the courtyard are shared equally, so the division is half-half. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that common areas are shared equally regardless of the size of private property, as long as both have equal access and usage rights.

Key Terms
ساحة (saha): The courtyard or open area within a house that is used for passage and common purposes.
مرور (murur): Passage; the right to walk through the courtyard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a house has ten rooms owned by one person and one room owned by another, how is the courtyard divided?
A) 10/11 for the owner of ten rooms, 1/11 for the other.
B) The courtyard belongs entirely to the owner of ten rooms.
C) Half-half between the two owners.
D) The courtyard is not shared; each uses only the part in front of their rooms.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on shared courtyards is based on equality of use (passage), not on the size of private property.

Seller's Claim of Child Born Within Six Months of Sale
Ruling with inter-madhhab dispute on seller's paternity claim for child born within six months of sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a seller claims a child born within six months of sale, explain the istihsan reasoning, and distinguish the opposing qiyas position.

Core Idea
Istihsan overrides qiyas when pregnancy is hidden: seller's paternity claim within six months voids the sale.

This passage deals with a nuanced scenario where a person sells a slave-girl, and within the minimum human gestation period she gives birth. The seller claims the child as his own. The Hanafi school, employing istihsan (juristic preference), accepts the claim and invalidates the sale, while strict qiyas (analogy) and other schools reject it due to an apparent contradiction. We will examine the reasoning behind each position and understand why the Hanafis prefer istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputed claim
Recognize that a slave-girl is sold, she gives birth within six months, and the seller asserts paternity. The dispute centers on whether the claim is valid despite the sale.
Six months is the minimum human gestation period (Quran 46:15, 31:14 combined).
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its basis (istihsan)
By istihsan, the child belongs to the seller, the mother becomes umm walad, and the sale is void. The hidden nature of pregnancy justifies overlooking the contradiction.
Istihsan is a secondary source of law in the Hanafi school, used to avoid hardship.
3
Understand the qiyas position and the opposing view
Qiyas dictates that the seller's claim is invalid because selling the slave-girl is an acknowledgment that she is not umm walad – a direct contradiction. Imam Zufar (Hanafi) and the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools adopt this view.
Imam Zufar's position represents the purely analogical reasoning within the Hanafi school.
4
Trace the istihsan reasoning in detail
The contradiction is overlooked because pregnancy is a hidden fact. The short gestation proves conception occurred during the seller's ownership, making the child his. Therefore, the sale involved an umm walad, which is impermissible.
The rule that an umm walad cannot be sold is established by hadith and ijmā‘.
5
Apply the consequence: sale void, price returned
Once the paternity is established, the sale becomes invalid (fasid). The buyer must return the slave-girl and receive the price back. The seller retains the child and the mother as umm walad.
The mufta bihi (operative fatwa) in the Hanafi school is the istihsan position.

Source Text

قوله واذا باع جارية ... ایک شخص نے باندی فروخت کی اس نے وقت بیچ سے چھ ماہ کم میں بچہ جن اور بائع نے بچہ کا دعوی کیاتو وہ بچہ استحسانا بائع کا لڑکا کا ہوگا۔ اور باندی اس کی اتم ولد ہوگی۔ امام زفر اور ائمہ ثلاثہ کے نزدیک بائع کا دعوی باطل ہے۔ مقتضاء قیاس بھی یہی ہے۔ کیونکہ بائع کا باندی کو فروخت کرنا اس بات کا اقرار ہے کہ وہ اس کی ام ولد نہیں باندی ہے پس اقرار سابق اور دعوی لاحق کے درمیان تناقض ہے۔ لہذا دعویٰ مسموع نہ ہوگا۔ وجہ استحسان یہ ہے کہ قرار حمل ایک مخفی امر ہے۔ اس لئے تناقض کو نظر انداز کیا جائے گا۔ اور ملک بائع میں قرار حمل اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ بچہ بائع کا ہے۔ کیونکہ ولادت چھ ماہ سے کم میں ہے۔ اور جب بطریق مذکور بائع کا دعوی صحیح ہوا تو یہ اصل علوق کی طرف مستند ہو گا۔ معلوم ہوا کہ اس نے اتم ولد کی بیع کی ہے۔ لہذا بی مسخ ہو جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ ام ولد کی بیع جائز نہیں۔ اور ثمن واپس کرنا ضروری ہوگا۔ کیونکہ وہ اس نے ناحق وصول کیا ہے۔
His saying: 'And if he sells a slave-girl...' A person sold a slave-girl. She gave birth to a child less than six months from the time of sale, and the seller claimed the child. Then that child, by istihsan, will be the seller's son, and the slave-girl will be his umm walad. According to Imam Zufar and the three Imams, the seller's claim is void. The requirement of qiyas is also this, because the seller's selling the slave-girl is an acknowledgment that she is not his umm walad but a slave-girl. So there is a contradiction between the previous acknowledgment and the later claim. Therefore the claim would not be heard. The reason for istihsan is that the settling of the pregnancy is a hidden matter. Therefore the contradiction will be overlooked. And the settling of the pregnancy in the seller's ownership is evidence that the child is his, because the birth is less than six months. And when the seller's claim is valid in the mentioned manner, it will be traced back to the original conception. It becomes known that he sold an umm walad. Therefore the sale is invalidated because the sale of an umm walad is not permissible. And the price must be returned because he took it wrongfully.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with a dispute. The scenario: a person sells a slave-girl, and within six months (the minimum human gestation period) she gives birth. The seller claims the child as his own. By istihsan (juristic preference), the child belongs to the seller, and the slave-girl becomes an umm walad, invalidating the sale. The qiyas (analogy) reasoning, supported by Imam Zufar and the three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali), says the claim is invalid because the seller's act of selling acknowledges she is not his umm walad, creating a contradiction. However, istihsan overrides qiyas because pregnancy is a hidden matter; the contradiction is overlooked. The short gestation proves conception occurred before sale. Consequently, the sale is void, and the price must be refunded.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in cases where strict analogy leads to hardship or hidden factors.
ام ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has borne a child to her master; she cannot be sold and becomes free upon her master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does istihsan allow the seller to claim a child born within six months of sale despite the apparent contradiction?
A) Because the seller's ownership at conception is proven by the short gestation
B) Because the buyer consented
C) Because the child's freedom is paramount
D) Because the sale was invalid from the start

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to prioritize hidden realities over apparent contradictions, allowing a seller's paternity claim within the minimum gestation period to invalidate the sale.

Buyer's Claim of Paternity for a Child Born Within Six Months
Ruling that buyer's paternity claim is invalid with dissenting view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects a buyer's paternity claim for a child born within six months of sale and distinguish between istīlād and tahrīr claims.

Core Idea
A buyer's paternity claim for a child born within six months is invalid because only the seller's istīlād claim retroacts to conception in his ownership.

This passage addresses a specific dispute within the Hanafi school: when a child is born to a sold slave-girl within six months of sale, can the buyer claim paternity? The majority holds that the buyer's claim is invalid, while Ibrahim al-Makhfi argues the buyer's ownership gives him a stronger claim. You will learn the reasoning behind the majority position and the key distinction between istīlād and tahrīr claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the timeframes and scenarios
The passage outlines three possible birth periods relative to the sale: less than six months, more than two years, or in between. For each, four claimant configurations exist: seller only, buyer only, both together, or successively. Understand these to see where the disputed ruling fits.
The first scenario (seller alone) was covered previously.
2
Locate the disputed ruling
The majority Hanafi position is that the buyer's claim is invalid, whether made together with the seller's claim or after it. Ibrahim al-Makhfi opposes this, arguing the buyer's ownership makes the claim valid.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyse the proofs
The majority bases its ruling on the nature of the claim: the seller's claim is istīlād (retroactive to conception in his ownership), while the buyer's claim is tahrīr (only establishes freedom, not lineage). Ibrahim relies on the buyer's actual ownership of both mother and child.
Istīlād does not require current ownership; tahrīr does not retroact.
4
Apply the distinction
Because the seller's claim retroacts to the time of conception, it is inherently prior. The buyer's claim, being tahrīr, cannot override this. The majority view is mufta bihi.
The thamarah al-khilaf: child belongs to seller, mother becomes umm walad for seller.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اور اگر مشتری نے اس بچہ کے نسب کا دعویٰ کیا تو اس کے دعوی کا کوئی اعتبار نہ ہو گا خواہ بائع کے دعوئی کے ساتھ کیا ہو یا اس کے بعد ، ابراہیم مخفی فرماتے ہیں کہ مشتری کا دعوہ معتبر ہوگا۔ کیونکہ باندی اور بچہ دونوں میں مشتری کی حقیقی ملک ہے۔ اور بائع کے لئے صرف استحقاق نسب کا حق ہے۔ اور حق معارض حقیقت نہیں ہوتا۔ ہم یہ کہتے ہیں کہ بائع کا دعویٰ بہر حال سابق ہے۔ چنانچہ بائع کا دعوہ اگر مشتری کے دعوہ سے پہلے ہو تب تو اس کے دعوہ کا سابق ہونا ظاہر ہے۔ اور اگر دعوہ مشتری کے بعد ہو تو اس لئے کہ وہ علوق کے وقت سے مستند ہے۔ کیونکہ وہ دعوہ استیلا د ہے۔ جس میں بوجہ قوت فی الحال قیام ملک کی بھی ضرورت نہیں ہوتی۔ بخلاف دعوہ مشتری کے کہ وہ دعوہ تحریر ہے۔
His saying: 'And if the buyer claims it...' It should be known that when a child is born to a sold slave-girl, there are three situations: born less than six months from the time of sale, or more than two years, or in between. Each of these then has four scenarios: the claimant is solely the seller, solely the buyer, both together, or successively. The explanation of the first scenario has been given above.
And if the buyer claims paternity of this child, his claim will be of no effect, whether made together with the seller's claim or after it. Ibrahim al-Makhfi says that the buyer's claim is valid because the slave-girl and the child are both in the buyer's actual ownership. And the seller only has a right to paternity. And a right does not oppose actual ownership. We (Hanafis) say that the seller's claim is in any case prior. So if the seller's claim precedes the buyer's, its priority is obvious. And if his claim is after the buyer's, it is because it traces back to the time of conception, since it is a claim of istīlād, which due to its strength does not require current ownership. Unlike the buyer's claim, which is a claim of tahrīr.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage addresses the buyer's claim when a child is born within six months of sale. The text first outlines three possible gestation periods relative to the sale (less than 6 months, more than 2 years, between). For each, four scenarios exist: only seller claims, only buyer claims, both claim simultaneously, or successively. The first scenario (seller claims alone) was covered previously. Here, the ruling is that the buyer's claim is invalid, whether before or after the seller's claim. Ibrahim al-Makhfi (a Hanafi scholar) dissents, arguing the buyer's ownership gives him a stronger claim. The majority Hanafi position responds that the seller's claim is a claim of istīlād, which retroacts to the time of conception and does not require current ownership, making it inherently prior. The buyer's claim is only a claim of tahrīr (emancipation) and cannot override.

Key Terms
دعوی استیلاد (da'wa al-istīlād): Claim that the child was conceived in the claimant's ownership; strong and retroactive.
دعوی تحریر (da'wa al-tahrīr): Claim that the child was conceived outside ownership; weaker and does not retroact.

C) Because the seller has more witnesses
D) Because the child looks like the seller

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives precedence to the seller's istīlād claim over the buyer's tahrīr claim because istīlād retroacts to the time of conception in the seller's ownership.

Seller's Claim When Child Born More Than Two Years After Sale
Ruling that seller's paternity claim is invalid if child born more than two years after sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a seller's paternity claim when a child is born more than two years after sale, and explain the necessity of buyer confirmation.

Core Idea
A child born more than two years after sale cannot be claimed by the seller without buyer confirmation.

In this passage, al-Marghinani addresses a specific scenario: a seller claims paternity of a child born to a previously sold slave-girl. The timing of the birth is critical. The Hanafi school, relying on the maximum gestational period (hadd al-haml) of two years, rules that if the child is born beyond that period, the seller's claim is invalid unless the buyer confirms it. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The case is: a slave-girl is sold, then later gives birth to a child more than two years after the sale. The seller claims the child is his.
The two-year period is the Hanafi maximum for gestation.
2
Understand why the claim is invalid
Since the child was born after two years, it is certain that conception occurred after the sale, meaning not in the seller's ownership. Paternity requires a link to ownership, so the claim fails.
Certainty (yaqin) is the key: conception in his ownership is not certain.
3
Recognize the exception: buyer confirmation
If the buyer confirms the seller's claim, paternity is established, but it is interpreted as via marriage to avoid attributing the child to adultery. The sale remains valid.
The confirmation allows paternity but does not make the mother an umm walad.
4
Determine the financial consequence
If the buyer confirms, the seller must pay the child's value, and the child becomes free. Without confirmation, no payment.
The child's freedom is contingent on payment of value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وان جاءت به لاكثر من سنتين .... اور اگر وقت بیچ سے دو برس زائد پر بچہ پیدا ہو تو بائع کا دعوہ نسب صحیح نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ علوق کا اس کی ملک سے متصل ہونا بطریق یقین نہیں پایا گیا۔ حالانکہ اس کے ثبوت نسب کی محبت یہی تھی۔ ہاں اگر مشتری بائع کی تصدیق کر دے تو بائع سے اس کا نسب ثابت ہو جائے گا۔ اور یہ استبیلاد بذریعہ نکاح پر محمول ہو گا۔ تا کہ زنا کاری لازم نہ آئے۔ اور بیع باطل نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ ہمیں اس امر کا یقین ہے۔ کہ علوق ملک بائع میں نہیں ہوا تو اس بچہ میں حقیقی آزادی اور اس کی ماں میں حق آزادی ثابت نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب باندی ام ولد نہ ہوئی ۔ تو دعوہ بالغ دعوہ تحریر باقی رہا اور غیر مالک اس کا اہل نہیں ہوتا اور یہاں بائع مالک نہیں ہے۔ تو مشتری کی تصدیق ضروری ہے۔ بہر کیف بائع اس بچہ کی قیمت دے گا اور بچہ بقیمت آزاد ہوگا۔
His saying: 'And if she gives birth after more than two years...' And if the child is born more than two years after the time of sale, then the seller's claim of paternity will not be valid. Because the connection of conception to his ownership is not found with certainty, whereas that was the basis for establishing paternity. Yes, if the buyer confirms the seller's claim, then paternity from the seller will be established. And this istīlād will be considered as via marriage, so that illegitimacy does not occur. And the sale will not be void. Because we are certain that conception did not occur in the seller's ownership. So for this child, true freedom and for its mother the right to freedom will not be established. And when the slave-girl is not umm walad, the claim remains a claim of tahrīr, and a non-owner is not eligible for that. And here the seller is not the owner. So the buyer's confirmation is necessary. In any case, the seller will pay the value of the child, and the child will be freed by payment.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where a child is born more than two years after the sale. The Hanafi school holds that the maximum gestational period is two years (based on a legal position from the time of the Companions). If the birth occurs beyond this period, it is certain that conception occurred after the sale, so the seller's paternity claim is invalid because it cannot be traced to his ownership. However, if the buyer confirms the claim, paternity is established but interpreted as via marriage (to avoid attributing the child to adultery). The sale remains valid because the mother is not an umm walad. The seller must pay the child's value, and the child becomes free by that payment.

Key Terms
حد حمل (hadd al-haml): Maximum gestational period; Hanafis accept two years as the maximum based on Companions' practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The seller provides witnesses
B) The buyer confirms the claim
C) The child must resemble the seller
D) The claim is always invalid

Key Takeaway
The seller's paternity claim on a child born more than two years after sale is invalid unless the buyer confirms it; without confirmation, no paternity and no freedom for the child.

Seller's Paternity Claim in the Intermediate Period
Ruling with proof: seller's paternity claim in intermediate period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the seller's paternity claim when a child is born between six months and two years after the sale and explain why the buyer's confirmation is required.

Core Idea
Seller's paternity claim needs buyer confirmation when birth occurs in the uncertain 6-month to 2-year window.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced scenario in the sale of a slave-girl: if she gives birth in the intermediate period (6 months to 2 years after sale), the seller's claim of paternity is not automatically accepted. Because conception could have occurred after the sale, the seller lacks certainty. Therefore, the buyer's confirmation (tasdiq) becomes the decisive factor. We will examine the ruling, its rationale, and the disagreement with other scholars.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and Hanafi ruling
Identify the time window (6 months to 2 years) and the condition: the claim is valid only if the buyer confirms. If confirmed, the sale is void, the child is free, and the mother becomes umm walad.
Note the contrast with periods less than 6 months (conclusive) and more than 2 years (inconclusive).
2
Identify the underlying legal cause (dalil)
The reason is the possibility (ihtimal) that conception occurred after the sale. Since paternity must be certain, the seller alone cannot prove it. This is a rational inference based on the principle of avoiding uncertainty.
The dalil is not a text but a qiyas on the principle that certainty cannot be overridden by doubt.
3
Understand the role of buyer's confirmation (tasdiq)
The buyer's confirmation provides the needed certainty. By confirming, the buyer essentially acknowledges that the child was conceived during the seller's ownership, which makes the sale defective (since the mother was pregnant at sale).
Without confirmation, the claim is rejected because the seller's assertion alone is insufficient.
4
Recognize the opposing views
Imam Zufar likely holds the claim invalid even with confirmation, while Imam al-Shafi'i may require other evidence. The Hanafi position is that confirmation resolves the uncertainty.
The khilaf is based on how each school treats probability in paternity cases.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafis, with buyer confirmation, paternity is established and sale is void. Under opposing views, the claim may not be accepted, so the child remains with the buyer.
This affects the status of the child (free vs. slave) and the mother (umm walad vs. slave).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله لاكثر من ستة ... اگر باندی چھ ماہ سے زائد اور دو سال سے کم میں بچہ جنے۔ اور بائع بچہ کا دعوی کرے تو دعوی besturdubai W Gelp ہوگا۔ اس کی تصدیق کر دے۔ کیونکہ یہ احتمال موجود ہے کہ علوق بائع کی ملک میں نہ ہوا ہو پس اس کی طرف سے حجت نہیں پائی گئی ۔ لہذا مشتری کی تصدیق ضروری ہے۔ پس اس کی تصدیق پر نسب ثابت، بیع باطل، بچہ آزاد اور اس کی ماں بائع کی اہم ولد ہو جائے گی ۔ خلافًا لزفر والشافعی علی مامر
His saying: 'More than six months...' If the slave-girl gives birth after more than six months and less than two years, and the seller claims the child, then the claim [will be valid only if the buyer] confirms. Because there is a possibility that conception did not occur in the seller's ownership. Therefore no proof is found from his side. Hence the buyer's confirmation is necessary. Then upon his confirmation, paternity is established, the sale is void, the child is free, and his mother becomes the seller's umm walad. Contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage covers the intermediate gestational period (6 months to 2 years). Since both pre-sale and post-sale conception are possible, the seller's paternity claim lacks certainty. Therefore, the buyer's confirmation is required for the claim to be accepted. If the buyer confirms, the child's paternity is established, the sale is invalidated, the child is free, and the mother becomes an umm walad. This contradicts the views of Imam Zufar (who would likely invalidate the claim) and Imam al-Shafi'i (who may not require confirmation). The ruling relies on the possibility that conception could have occurred after the sale, so the seller alone cannot prove paternity.

Key Terms
تصدیق (tasdiq): Confirmation by the buyer that the seller's paternity claim is true; required when conception period is uncertain.
ام ولد (umm walad): A slave-woman who has borne a child to her master; she becomes free upon his death and cannot be sold.
احتمال (ihtimal): Possibility; here, the possibility that conception occurred after the sale, which creates uncertainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The seller's paternity claim in the 6-month to 2-year period requires the buyer's confirmation to establish paternity and void the sale.

The Principle of Asl and Tabi' in Establishing Umm Walad Status
Ruling that seller's paternity claim after child's death does not establish umm walad for the mother.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the seller's paternity claim after the death of the child or mother, and explain the principle of asl wa tabi' that underpins it.

Core Idea
The child is the original in paternity; the mother's umm walad status follows only if the child's paternity is established.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents two contrasting scenarios: when the child dies before the seller's claim, and when the mother dies. The key is the principle of asl wa tabi'—the child is the original (asl) for paternity and freedom, and the mother is subsidiary (tabi'). Understanding this principle is essential for correctly applying rulings on istīlād.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First, if the child dies and the seller claims paternity, the mother does not become umm walad. Second, if the mother dies, the seller's claim establishes paternity and he takes the child.
Both scenarios require the child to be born within six months of sale.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith 'أعتقها ولدها' (Her child freed her) is cited as evidence that the child is the agent of freedom for the mother.
This hadith is reported by Ibn Majah and al-Hakim, with zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'أعتقها' indicates that the child is the one who frees the mother, implying the child is the original (asl) and the mother follows.
This linguistic analysis supports the hierarchical relationship.
4
Apply the asl wa tabi' principle
Since the child is the original, his paternity must be established first. If he dies, his paternity is not needed, so it is not established, and the mother cannot follow. If the mother dies, the child's paternity is still needed, so it is established and the child is taken.
The subsidiary cannot exist without the original.
5
Note the practical difference
In the first case, the seller does not get the child (dead) and the mother remains a regular slave (not umm walad). In the second, the seller takes the living child, who is free, and the mother was already dead.
The child's freedom is real (immediate), while the mother's is only a right to freedom upon the master's death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ فَإِنْ مَاتَ الْوَلَدُ فَادَّعَاهُ الْبَائِعُ وَقَدْ جَاءَتْ بِهِ لِأَقَلَ مِنْ ستَّةِ أَشْهُرٍ لَمْ يَثْبُتِ الْإِسْتِيْلَادُ فِي الَّامِ لِأَنَّهَا تَابِعَةٌ لِلْوَلَدِ وَلَمْ يَثْبُتْ نَسَبُه بَعْدَ الْمَوْتِ لِعَدَمِ حَاجَتِهِ إِلى ذَلِكَ فَلَا يَتَبَعُهُ اسْتِيْلادُ الْامَ وَإِنْ مَاتَتِ الْامَّ فَادَّعَاهُ الْبَائِعُ وَقَدْ جَاءَتْ بِهِ لاقَلَ مِنْ سِتَّةِ أَشْهُرٍ يَثْبُتُ النَّسَبُ فِي الْوَلَدِ وَاَخَذَهُ الْبَائِعُ لِاَنَّ الْوَلَدَ هُوَ الْأَصْلُ فِي النَّسَبِ فَلَا ـرُّهُ فَوَاتُ التَّبِعَ وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ الْوَلَدُ أَصْلًا لِأَنَّهَا تُضَافُ إِلَيْهِ يُقَالُ اُمُّ الْوَلَدِ وَيَسْتَفِيدُ الْحُرِّيَةُ مِنْ جِهَتِهِ لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ أَعْتَقَهَا وَلَدُهَا وَالثَّابِتِ لَهَا حَقُّ الْحُرِّيَةِ وَلَهُ حَقِيقَتِهَا وَالْأَدْنَى يَتَّبِعُ الْأَعْلَى
He said: If the child dies and the seller claims it, and she gave birth to it in less than six months, then istīlād is not established in the mother because she is subsidiary to the child, and his paternity is not established after death due to his lack of need for that. So istīlād of the mother does not follow it. And if the mother dies and the seller claims it, and she gave birth to it in less than six months, paternity is established in the child and the seller takes him, because the child is the original in paternity. So the loss of the subsidiary does not harm [the claim]. And the child is considered the original because she is attributed to him; it is said 'mother of the child', and she gains freedom from his side due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'her child freed her'. And what is established for her is the right to freedom, and for him is true freedom. And the lower follows the higher.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents two sub-rulings regarding the death of the child or mother before a paternity claim. First, if the child dies (within six months of sale), the seller's subsequent claim does not establish the mother as umm walad. The reason: the child's paternity is the basis; since the dead child has no need for paternity, paternity is not established, and the mother cannot follow as subsidiary. Second, if the mother dies and the seller claims the surviving child (born within six months), paternity is established and the seller takes the child. The child is the original for establishing lineage; the mother is subsidiary. The child's freedom is real (he is free immediately), whereas the mother gains only the right to freedom (she becomes free upon master's death). The underlying principle is that the subsidiary follows the original.

Key Terms
اصل و تابع (asl wa tabi'): Principle that the child is the original (asl) in establishing lineage and freedom, and the mother is subsidiary (tabi').

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the mother's status follows the child, and the child's paternity is not established after death
B) Because the mother had already been sold
C) Because the seller did not pay the price
D) Because the child was not born in the seller's house

Key Takeaway
The child is the original in paternity; without the child's paternity, the mother cannot gain umm walad status.

Paternity Claim After Mother's Death
Ruling on paternity claim after mother's death with proof and distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a seller's paternity claim remains valid after the mother's death, applying the Hanafi distinction between haqiqat al-hurriyyah and haqq al-hurriyyah.

Core Idea
The mother's death does not block paternity claim because the child—not the mother—is the legal origin of lineage.

In the previous lesson, we saw that if a seller claims paternity over a child born within six months of sale, the child becomes free and the seller takes him. Now we learn that even if the mother dies before the claim, the ruling remains unchanged. Why? Because the child is the original (asl) in lineage, and the mother is merely a subsidiary (tabi'). The child's need for paternity is independent of the mother's existence. This lesson unpacks the proof and the important distinction between actual freedom (haqiqat al-hurriyyah) and the right to freedom (haqq al-hurriyyah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The scenario: a slave woman is sold, gives birth within six months, then dies. The seller claims paternity after her death. The ruling: paternity is established for the child, and the seller takes the child as his free son.
The child must be born within six months of the sale—less than a full gestation period, proving conception occurred before sale.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'Her child freed her' (أَعْتَقَهَا وَلَدُهَا). This is the proof that the mother's freedom derives from the child, not the other way around.
The hadith establishes the child as the source of freedom for the mother.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the child is the original? Because the mother is attributed to the child (umm al-walad), and she gains freedom through him. If the child's paternity is established, the mother's right to freedom follows. Therefore, her death does not affect the child's paternity.
The child's lineage is established directly; the mother's status is derivative.
4
Note the key distinction between haqiqah and haqq
The child attains actual freedom (haqiqat al-hurriyyah) immediately upon paternity claim—he becomes free. The mother, however, only obtains a right to freedom (haqq al-hurriyyah)—she cannot be sold, but she remains a slave until the master's death. This distinction shows why the child's freedom is more fundamental.
The mother's right to freedom is a lesser status; the child's actual freedom is the higher (a'la). The lower follows the higher.
5
Evaluate the practical implication
Since the child is free and the mother is dead, the seller (now father) takes the child as his son. No compensation is due to the buyer because the child was never part of the sale—the mother was sold, but the child's paternity was unknown at sale and is now established.
The buyer may seek a refund for the mother if she was sold with a defect, but that is a separate issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ مَاتَتِ الْامَّ فَادَّعَاهُ الْبَائِعُ وَقَدْ جَاءَتْ بِهِ لِاقَلَ مِنْ سِتَّةِ أَشْهُرٍ يَثْبُتُ النَّسَبُ فِي الْوَلَدِ وَاَخَذَهُ الْبَائِعُ لِاَنَّ الْوَلَدَ هُوَ الْأَصْلُ فِي النَّسَبِ فَلَا ـرُّهُ فَوَاتُ التَّبِعَ وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ الْوَلَدُ أَصْلًا لِأَنَّهَا تُضَافُ إِلَيْهِ يُقَالُ اُمُّ الْوَلَدِ وَيَسْتَفِيدُ الْحُرِّيَةُ مِنْ جِهَتِهِ لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ أَعْتَقَهَا وَلَدُهَا وَالثَّابِتِ لَهَا حَقُّ الْحُرِّيَةِ وَلَهُ حَقِيقَتِهَا وَالْأَدْنَى يَتَّبِعُ الْأَعْلَى
And if the mother dies, and the seller claims him, and she gave birth to him in less than six months, paternity is established in the child and the seller takes him, because the child is the original in paternity. So the loss of the subsidiary does not harm [the claim]. And the child is considered the original because she is attributed to him; it is said 'mother of the child', and she gains freedom from his side due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'her child freed her'. And what is established for her is the right to freedom, and for him is true freedom. And the lower follows the higher.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage continues the previous ruling. When the mother has died but the child (born within six months of sale) is alive, the seller's paternity claim is effective. The child's paternity is established, and the seller takes the child. The child is the original (asl) in lineage; the mother is subsidiary (tabi'). The mother's death does not affect the claim because the child needs paternity. The child is considered the original because: (1) the mother is attributed to him (ummu'l-walad), (2) she gains freedom through him, as per the hadith 'her child freed her'. Importantly, the mother only acquires the right to freedom (i.e., she is freed upon the master's death), while the child acquires actual freedom (he is free immediately). This illustrates the principle that the lower follows the higher: the mother's status derives from the child's.

Key Terms
حقیقت الحرية (haqiqat al-hurriyyah): Actual, true freedom that the child attains immediately upon paternity claim; unlike the mother's right to freedom.
حق الحرية (haqq al-hurriyyah): The right to freedom that the mother acquires; she cannot be sold but remains a slave until the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the child is the primary legal subject in paternity, so the mother's death does not hinder the seller's claim; the child gains actual freedom while the mother only had a right to it.

Ruling on Sale of Pregnant Slave Girl and Claim of Child
Intra-Hanafi dispute on returning price in sale of pregnant slave claiming child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the return of price when a sold pregnant slave gives birth and the seller claims the child, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The seller's claim of the child after freeing the mother does not affect the sale's invalidity; the full price must be returned.

This passage from the Hidayah deals with a complex scenario: a man sells his pregnant slave girl, then after the birth she is freed by the buyer, and the seller claims the child as his own. The legal question is whether the seller must return the entire price or only a portion. The Hanafi school has an internal division on this point, rooted in the status of the mother's value. Let us unpack the reasoning and identify the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling Context
The act is the sale of a pregnant slave girl (umm walad) who later gives birth in the buyer's possession. The ruling concerns the return of price (thaman) when the seller claims the child after the buyer has freed the mother.
Note the distinction: the mother is freed by the buyer, not the child.
2
State the Two Hanafi Positions
Abu Hanifah holds that the seller must return the entire price because the mother is not mutaqawwim (recognized value) in his view. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that only the child's share of the price is returned, as they consider the mother's value to be mutaqawwim.
3
Identify the Underlying Illa (Legal Cause)
The dispute hinges on whether the mother (umm walad) is considered mutaqawwim. Abu Hanifah says no; the Sahibayn say yes. This determines liability for her portion of the price.
The child's value is agreed to be mutaqawwim.
4
Analyze the Proof Structure
The text provides a deeper analogy: when the buyer frees the mother, the preventing factor (itq) occurs in the dependent (tabi'), not the principal (asl - the child). Thus the seller's claim in the child is valid. If the buyer freed the child instead, the preventing factor occurs in the principal, voiding the claim. This is analogized to the child of the maghrur and the mustawlada bi al-nikah.
5
Determine the Tarjih and Mufta Bihi
The commentator states that Abu Hanifah's view (return full price) is correct (huwa al-sahih). This is the mufta bihi position in this text.
Later fatwa practice may rely on this tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يدعيه الرجلان. وَيَرُدُّ الثَّمَنَ كُلَهُ فِي قَوْلِ أَبِي حَنِيفَةً وَقَالَا يَرُدُّ حِصَّةَ الْوَلَدِ وَلَا يَرُّدُ حِصَّةَ الْأُمِّ لِأَنَّهُ تَبَيَّنَ أَنَّهُ بَاعَ أُمَّ وَلَدِهِ وَمَالِيَتُهَا غَيْرَ مَتَقَوَّمَةٍ عِنْدَهُ فِي الْعَقْدِ وَالْعَصْبِ فَلا يَضْمَنُهَا الْمُشْتَرِى وَعِنْدَهُمَا مُتَقَوَّمَةٌ فَيَضْمَنُهَا قَالَ وَفِي الْجَامِعِ الصَّغِيرِ وَإِذَا حَبَلَتِ الْجَارِيَةُ فِى مِلْكِ رَجُلٍ فَبَاعَهَا فَوَلَدَتْ فِي يَدِ الْمُشْتَرِى فَادَّعَى الْبَائِعِ الْوَلَدَ وَقَدْ اعْتَقَ الْمُشْتَرِى الْامَّ فَهُوَ ابْنَهُ وَيَرُدُّ عَلَيْهِ بِحِصَّتِهِ مِنَ الثَّمَنِ وَلَوْ كَانَ الْمُشْتَرِى اَعْتَقَ الْوَلَدَ فَدَعْوَتُهُ بَاطِلَةٌ وَجْهُ الْفَرَقِ اَنَّ الْأَصْلَ فِي هَذَا الْبَابِ الْوَلَدُ وَالْاُمُّ تَابِعَةٌ لَهُ عَلَى مَامَرَّ وَفِي الْوَجْهِ الْأَوَّلِ قَامَ الْمَانِعُ مِنَ الدَّعْوَةِ وَالْإِسْتِيلادِ وَهُوَ الْعِتْقُ فِي التَّبَعِ وَهُوَ الْاُمُّ فَلَا يَمْتَنِعُ ثُبُوتُه فِى الْاَصْلِ وَهُوَ الْوَلَدُ وَلَيْسَ مِنْ ضُرُورَاتِهِ كَمَا فِي وَلَدِ الْمَغْرُورِ فَإِنَّهُ حُرِّ وَأُمُّهُ أُمَّةٍ لِمَوْلَاهَا وَكَمَا فِي الْمَسْتَوْلَدَةِ بِالنِّكَاحِ وَفِي الْفَضْلِ الثَّانِي قَامَ الْمَانِعُ بِالْأَصْلِ وَهُوَ الْوَلَدُ فَيَمْتَنِعُ تُبُوتُه فِيْهِ وَفِي التَّبِعِ وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ الْإِعْتَاقَ مَانِعًا لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَحْتَمِلُ النَّقْضَ كَحَقِ اسْتَلْحَاقِ النَّسَبِ وَحَقِ الْإِسْتِبْلَادِ فَاسْتَوَيَا مِنْ هَذَا الْوَجْهِ وَالثَّابِتُ مِنَ الْمُشْتَرِى حَقِيقَةُ الْإِعْتَاقِ وَالثَّابِتُ فِي الْامِ حَقُّ الحُرِّيَةِ وَفِي الْوَلَدِ لِلْبَائِعِ حَقُّ الدَّعْوَةِ وَالْحَقُّ لَا يُعَارِضُ الْحَقِيقَةَ وَالتَّدْبِيْرَ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْإِعْتَاقِ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَحْتَمِلُ النَّقْضَ وَقَدْ ثَبَتَ بِهِ بَعْضَ آثَارِ الْحُرِّيَةِ وَقَوْلُهُ فِى الْفَضْلِ الْأَوَّلِ يَرُدُ عَلَيْهِ بِحِصَّتِهِ مِنَ الثَّمَنِ قَولُهُمَا وَعِنْدَهُ بِكُلِّ الثَّمَنِ هُوَ الصَّحِيحُ كَمَا ذَكَرْنَا فِي فَضْلِ الْمَوْتِ
Chapter on what two men claim. He returns the entire price in the saying of Abu Hanifah, and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he returns the share of the child and does not return the share of the mother, because it became clear that he sold his umm walad, and her market value (maliyyah) is not considered (ghayr mutaqawwimah) in his view in contract and usurpation, so the buyer is not liable for her; and in their view it is considered (mutaqawwimah), so he is liable for her. He said: And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: When a slave girl becomes pregnant in a man's ownership and he sells her, then she gives birth in the hand of the buyer, and the seller claims the child while the buyer has freed the mother, then the child is his (the seller's) son, and he returns to him its share of the price. But if the buyer freed the child, then his claim is void. The aspect of the distinction is that the principle (asl) in this chapter is the child, and the mother is dependent on it, as has passed. In the first situation, the preventing factor from the claim of lineage and istilad, which is emancipation (itq), occurred in the dependent (tabi'), which is the mother; so it does not prevent its establishment in the principle, which is the child, and it is not among its necessities, just as in the child of the deceived (maghrur) — for he is free and his mother remains a slave for her master — and just as in the child born from a slave woman through marriage (mustawlada bi al-nikah). In the second situation, the preventing factor occurred in the principle, which is the child, so its establishment is prevented in it and in the dependent. And emancipation was a preventer only because it does not admit of being undone (naqd), similar to the right of claiming lineage (istilhaq al-nasab) and the right of istilad; so they are equal in this respect. What is established from the buyer is the reality of emancipation, and what is established in the mother is the right of freedom, and in the child the seller has the right of claiming; and a right does not oppose reality. Tadbir is like emancipation because it does not admit of being undone, and some effects of freedom are established by it. His statement in the first situation: 'he returns to him its share of the price' is their saying; and in his view he returns all of the price, and that is correct, as we mentioned in the chapter on death.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage from the Hidayah commentary deals with the situation where a man sells his pregnant slave girl, and after the sale, the child is born in the buyer's possession. The seller then claims the child as his own (establishing nasab). The legal issue concerns the return of the price (thaman). The text presents the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad). According to Abu Hanifah, the seller must return the entire price because the sale of an umm walad is invalid (batil) once it is discovered that she was an umm walad at the time of sale (since her market value is not recognized in his view). According to the Sahibayn, the seller only returns the portion of the price corresponding to the child, not the mother's portion, because they consider the mother's value to be recognized. The text then quotes from al-Jami' al-Saghir to illustrate a similar case: if the buyer frees the mother after the sale, the seller can still claim the child and must return the child's share of the price. But if the buyer frees the child, the seller's claim is void. The reasoning (wajh al-farq) is based on the principle that in this chapter, the child is the original (asl) and the mother is dependent (tabi'). When the preventing factor (emancipation) occurs in the dependent (mother), it does not prevent the claim in the original (child). This is analogized to the child of the maghrur (deceived man) and the child from a slave woman married (mustawlada bi al-nikah), where the child is free but the mother remains a slave. When the prevention occurs in the original (child), it prevents in both. Emancipation is a permanent act that cannot be undone, similar to the right of claiming lineage and istilad. The buyer's act is actual emancipation, while the seller's is only a right, and a right does not override reality. Tadbir (declaring the slave free after death) is treated like emancipation. The text concludes that in the first scenario, the Sahibayn's position is that the seller returns only the child's share, while Abu Hanifah's position (returning the full price) is considered correct (al-sahih).

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave girl who has borne a child to her master; she becomes free upon her master's death and cannot be sold.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Having a recognized market value in Islamic law, entitling the owner to compensation if lost.
استيلاد (istilad): The master's right to claim the child as his own, making the mother an umm walad.
دعوة (الولد) (da'wah (al-walad)): The seller's claim that the child born from the sold slave girl is his own child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling when the buyer frees the mother of the slave girl after purchase, and the seller claims the child?
A) The seller's claim is void, and he keeps the full price.
B) The seller's claim is valid, and he must return the entire price (Abu Hanifah's view).
C) The seller's claim is valid, and he returns only the child's share of the price (Sahibayn's view).
D) The seller must return the mother's share of the price.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute over the return of price when a sold pregnant slave's child is claimed, the relied-upon position (Abu Hanifah) returns the full price because the mother's value is not recognized.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Mutaqawwim Status of an Umm Walad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the value of an umm walad and the effect of returning the child after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the mutaqawwim status of an umm walad, and apply the ruling to calculate refund when the child is returned or freed.

Core Idea
The mother's status as umm walad does not automatically follow the child's lineage; freeing the child prevents the seller's claim, but freeing the mother does not.

This passage from al-Hidayah, commenting on al-Jami', addresses a subtle fiqh issue: when an umm walad is sold together with her child, and the child is later returned or freed, how is the price adjusted? The answer hinges on an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether an umm walad has a recognized market value (mutaqawwim) or not, and on the principle that in istilad claims the child is the original and the mother is subsidiary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
First, note that the core of the passage is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that an umm walad is not mutaqawwim, meaning she has no guaranteed value in sale or usurpation; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that she is mutaqawwim and thus her value is guaranteed.
This dispute appears early in the passage.
2
Work through the apportionment example
The passage gives a concrete example: an umm walad and child bought for 10 dinars, with assumed values of 15 for the mother and 10 for the child. The price is apportioned: 6 dinars for the mother, 4 for the child. If the buyer returns the child to the seller, the seller must return the child's portion (4 dirhams) to avoid combining the substitute and the substituted. If the mother dies, according to the Sahibayn, the seller's liability for the mother's portion is dropped; according to Imam Abu Hanifa, nothing is dropped.
The apportionment uses the 'value method' (taqwim).
3
Analyze the two scenarios of freeing
A passage from al-Jami' is quoted: if the buyer frees the mother, the seller's claim that the child is his (istilad) is valid and the child's portion is returned. If the buyer frees the child, the seller's claim is void. The reason: in istilad, the child is the original (asl) and the mother is subsidiary (tabi'). Prevention of the ruling in the subsidiary does not affect the original; prevention in the original prevents in the subsidiary.
The word 'prevention' (imumtina') refers to the impediment created by freeing.
4
Address the potential objection
One might object: if the child's lineage is established from the seller, the mother should become his umm walad, which would void the sale. The text responds with two examples: walad maghrur (child born from a deceived marriage) and walad mustawlada (child born from marriage to another's slave-woman). In both cases, the child is free and lineage is established, but the mother does not become umm walad. Thus, the mother's status does not automatically follow.
This shows the principle that the mother's umm walad status is not a necessary consequence of the child's lineage.
5
Understand the final reasoning on freeing vs. right
Finally, the passage explains that the buyer's actual freeing (itq) and the seller's right of istilad/istilhaq are both irrevocable, but actual freeing prevails over a mere right. Hence, when the buyer frees the child, the seller's claim is invalid; when the buyer frees the mother, the child is still claimable because the freeing is in the subsidiary.
This is a tarjih based on the strength of actual ownership vs. right.
