In the contract of sale and not in usurpation. So the buyer will not be a guarantor of it. That is, he will be entitled to recover his full price. According to the Sahibayn, its value is valuable (mutaqawwim), so the buyer will be a guarantor of it. Understand it thus: For example, the buyer purchased the mentioned umm walad for ten dinars, and assuming her as a slave-woman valued at fifteen dinars and the child as a slave valued at ten dinars, then the mentioned price of ten dinars was against the slave-woman with child, so it was distributed such that six dinars correspond to the slave-woman and four dinars to the child. Then, since the buyer returned the child to the seller, the seller must return the four dinars so that the substitute and the substituted are not combined. And since the umm walad died with the buyer, her six dinars are dropped from the seller. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, nothing is dropped from the death of the slave-woman, as if she were free. His statement: 'And in al-Jami'...' He quotes the phrase from al-Jami' so that it becomes known that the ruling of freeing the sold slave-woman and her child is like the ruling of their death. The meaning of the phrase is that a man owned a slave-woman who became pregnant. Then he sold her. The slave-woman gave birth to a child in the buyer's possession less than six months after the sale. The seller claimed that the child was his, even though the buyer had freed the mother. The seller's claim is valid and the child's lineage is established, and he will return the child's portion of the total price. And if the buyer freed the child but not the mother, then the seller's claim of lineage is void. The reason for the difference is that in the chapter of claiming istilad, the child is the original and the mother is subsidiary. In the first case (freeing the mother), the preventing factor (i.e., freeing and freedom) is found in the mother, who is subsidiary. So it will not be effective in the original, meaning lineage will be established in the child, because the prevention of the ruling in the subsidiary does not necessitate prevention in the original. It is possible that someone might object: when the establishment of the claim of istilad is not prevented for the seller, then the child's lineage from the seller is established, because conception certainly occurred in the seller's ownership (as assumed), and among the rulings of the child's lineage being established is that his mother becomes the seller's umm walad. So the seller's sale and the buyer's freeing should be void. From this it becomes clear that he had sold his umm walad, and the sale of an umm walad is void. But according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the value of the umm walad [؟] The author of Hidayah answers with 'And it is not necessary that if the child's freedom is established, then the mother's freedom as umm walad is also established. For example, a free child of deception (walad maghrur) and a free child of a purchased slave-woman (walad mustawlada) become free, even though their mothers do not become umm walad and remain slave-women. The case of walad maghrur is that a slave-woman deceived a free man saying 'I am free, marry me.' He married her and a child was born. Then the slave-woman's master came and proved she was his slave-woman. The child will be given to the master and will be free by payment of value. So the child's lineage is established, even though the mother does not become umm walad. The case of walad mustawlada is that one marries another's slave-woman and has a child from her; the child's lineage will be established, even though the mother does not become umm walad. In short, from the establishment of lineage or freedom of the child, it does not necessitate that his mother becomes umm walad or free. His statement: 'And in the second section...' And the second case (freeing the child) the preventing factor is found in the child, who is the original, so the establishment of lineage is prevented in the original, i.e., the child, and when it is prevented in the original, it is also prevented in the subsidiary, because prevention of the ruling in the original necessitates prevention in the subsidiary as well. His statement: 'And it is only because freeing...' In the issue under discussion, the buyer's freeing of the slave-woman and her child is a preventer of the claim of istilad and claim of lineage because, like the right of istilad and lineage, freeing is also irrevocable. So the occurrence of freeing from the buyer and the existence of the right of istilhaq and istilad from the seller are equal in that they cannot be broken. Then here the freeing is given preference in the sense that the buyer's freeing is actual, while the seller has only a right of freeing over the slave-woman and a right of claiming lineage over the child, and the right of freeing and lineage cannot compete with actual freedom.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from al-Hidayah regarding the purchase of an umm walad (a slave-woman who has borne her master a child) along with her child. It begins by explaining an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) concerning whether the umm walad has a valuable (mutaqawwim) market value. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the umm walad is not a guaranteed commodity in sale or usurpation because she is considered free in terms of value; thus if she dies with the buyer, the buyer cannot recover her price from the seller. The Sahibayn hold that she is valuable, so the buyer is a guarantor and can recover her price if she perishes. The commentary illustrates with a concrete example: the buyer purchases an umm walad and her child for ten dinars. The mother is valued at fifteen dinars and the child at ten dinars. The price is apportioned: six dinars for the mother and four for the child. If the buyer returns the child to the seller (because it became apparent that the child was free or belonged to the seller), the seller must return the four dinars to avoid combining the substitute and the substituted. If the mother dies in the buyer's possession, the six dinars are dropped from the seller according to the Sahibayn, but not according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The commentary then quotes a passage from al-Jami' (one of Imam Muhammad's six books of Zahir al-Riwayah) to show that the ruling of freeing the mother or child is analogous to their death. It discusses two scenarios: (a) The seller claims that the child is his after the buyer has freed the mother. Here, the child's lineage is established because the preventing factor (freeing) occurred in the subsidiary (the mother), not in the original (the child). The seller must return the child's portion of the price. (b) The buyer frees the child but not the mother. In this case, the freeing is in the original, so the seller's claim is invalid. The reason: in matters of istilad (claim of motherhood), the child is the original and the mother is subsidiary. Prevention in the subsidiary does not affect the original. The commentary addresses a possible objection: if the child's lineage from the seller is established, then his mother should become the seller's umm walad, which would invalidate the sale. The response, quoting the Hidayah, is that it is not necessary that the mother becomes umm walad simply because the child's lineage or freedom is established. Examples are given: walad maghrur (child born from a deceived marriage) and walad mustawlada (child born from a marriage to another's slave-woman both become free without their mothers becoming umm walad. The passage concludes with explaining the second scenario where the child is freed: the prevention is in the original, so the lineage claim fails in both the child and the mother. Finally, it notes that freeing by the buyer and the seller's right of claim are both irrevocable, but actual freeing (by the buyer) prevails over a mere right of freeing or lineage claim.

Key Terms
ام ولد (umm walad): A slave-woman who has borne a child to her master, which gives her a special status: she cannot be sold and becomes free upon her master's death.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Having a recognized market value in Islamic law, such that it can be guaranteed in contracts or compensated in cases of damage.
استیلاد (istilad): The claim or status of a slave-woman becoming an umm walad by bearing her master's child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, if the buyer frees the child but not the mother, the seller's claim of lineage is:
A) Valid, and the child's portion must be returned.
B) Invalid, because the freeing of the child (the original) prevents the claim.
C) Valid, but only if the mother also dies.
D) Invalid, because the seller has no right to claim.

Key Takeaway
An umm walad's status does not automatically follow her child's lineage; the child is the original in istilad claims, and freeing the child prevents the seller's claim while freeing the mother does not.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Sale and Paternity Claims: When a Seller's Claim Voids a Sale
Ruling on paternity claim voiding sales and other contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a seller claims paternity of a slave born in his possession after the slave has been resold, and distinguish between voidable and irrevocable transactions in this context.

Core Idea
A seller's paternity claim over a slave born in his possession voids all prior voidable contracts but not irrevocable ones or a prior valid paternity claim by the buyer.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from the Hanafi sale (buyu') chapter: the effect of a paternity claim by the original seller of a slave who was born in his possession. The ruling hinges on a key distinction between contracts that are subject to annulment (muhtamil al-naqd) and those that are irrevocable (like freeing). Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the correct ruling in complex transactions involving slaves, as well as for analogizing to other areas where rights conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act is the paternity claim by the original seller after a sale. The ruling: the sale is void, and the child is established as the seller's son (thus free).
The claim is not itself a contract; it overrides prior voidable contracts.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is analogical reasoning (qiyas): the paternity claim is an irrevocable right (not subject to annulment), while the sale is a voidable contract. The claim prevails because it cannot be undone.
Qiyas is used to extend the same reasoning to other voidable contracts.
3
Trace the scope of application
The same ruling applies to other voidable contracts: kitaba, pledge, lease, and marriage of the slave. These are all subject to annulment.
Each of these contracts can be undone by the paternity claim.
4
Note the exceptions
The claim does not void freeing (i'taq) or tadbir (post-death freedom) because these are irrevocable. Also, if the buyer claimed paternity first, the seller's subsequent claim is invalid because the buyer's claim established an irrevocable lineage.
The priority of claims matters: first claim prevails if irrevocable.
5
Evaluate the objection and answer
An objection asks how a mere paternity claim (giving a right to freedom) can equal actual freeing. The answer: both are equally irrevocable in terms of not being subject to annulment, and the buyer's claim is preferred because it was first without opposition.
This clarifies the logic behind the priority rule.

نے فروخت کیا ایسا غلام جو اس کے پاس پیدا ہوا ہے۔ مشتری نے اس کو دوسرے کے ہاتھ فروخت کر دیا۔ پھر بائع اول نے اس کے نسب کا دعوی کیا تو وہ اس کا بیٹا ہے۔ اور بیع باطل ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ بیع محتمل نقض ہے۔ اور بائع کو جو حق دعوہ ہے وہ محتمل نقض نہیں ہے تو اس کی وجہ سے بیع ٹوٹ جائے گی۔ اسی طرح اگر مشتری نے بچہ کو مکاتب کیا یا اس کو رہن رکھا یا اس کو بیاہ دیا۔ پھر دعوی ہوا۔ کیونکہ یہ عوارض محتمل نقض ہیں تو یہ سب توڑ دیئے جائیں گے اور دعویٰ صحیح ہو جائے گا بخلاف اعتقاق اور تدبیر کے۔ جیسا کہ گزر چکا اور بخلاف اس کے جب پہلے مشتری نے اس کا دعوی کیا۔ پھر بائع نے کہ بائع سے نسب ثابت نہ ہوگا کیونکہ جو نسب مشتری سے ثابت ہے محتمل نقض نہیں ہے تو یہ اس کے آزاد کرنے کی طرح ہو گیا۔ تشریح: ایک شخص نے ایسا غلام فروخت کیا جو اس کے پاس پیدا ہوا ہے۔ یعنی اصل علوق اس کی ملک میں ہوا ہے۔ مشتری نے اس کو دوسرے کے ہاتھ فروخت کر دیا پھر بائع اول نے اس کے نسب کا دعویٰ کیا۔ تو اس سے نسب ثابت ہو جائے گا اور اول ثانی دونوں بیع ٹوٹ جائیں گی۔ اس لئے کہ بیع ایسی چیز ہے جو ٹوٹ سکتی ہے اور بائع کو جو حق دعوہ نسب کا ہے وہ نہیں ٹوٹ سکتا۔ پس حق دعوہ کی وجہ سے بیع توڑ دی جائے گی۔ اسی طرح اگر مشتری نے بچہ کو مکاتب کیا یا رہن رکھ دیا۔ یا اس کو کسی کے ساتھ بیاہ دیا پھر بائع نے اس بچہ کے نسب کا دعویٰ کیا تب بھی یہی حکم ہے کہ یہ تصرفات توڑ دئیے جائیں گے۔ کیونکہ یہ عوارض ایسے ہیں جو ٹوٹ سکتے ہیں۔ لیکن دعویٰ بائع نہیں ٹوٹ سکتا۔ قوله بخلاف ما اذا ادعاه.... الخ اور اگر پہلے بچہ کا دعوی مشتری نے کیا۔ پھر بائع نے کیا تو بائع سے نسب ثابت نہ ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ جو نسب مشتری سے ثابت ہو چکا وہ قابل نقض نہیں ہے تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے مشتری نے اس کو آزاد کر دیا ہو۔ سوال: اعتقاق سے حقیقی حریت ثابت ہے اور دعوی نسب سے حق حریت پس دونوں مساوی کہاں ہوئے اور بائع و مشتری کا دعوی نسب حق حریت ثابت ہونے میں مساوی ہے تو مرجح کہاں ہوا؟ جواب: احتمال نقض نہ ہونے میں تو عتق و دعوی دونوں مساوی ہیں۔ رہی ترجیح سو دعوی مشتری کو دعوی بائع پر اس حیثیت سے ترجیح ہے کہ بچہ بذریعہ دعوی اولی ثبوت نسب سے ایسے وقت میں بے نیاز ہو گیا کہ اس میں کوئی مزاحم نہیں ہے۔
He said: And whoever sells a slave who was born in his possession, and the buyer sells him to another, then the first seller claims him, then he is his son and the sale is void, because the sale is subject to annulment, and his right of claim is not subject to annulment, so he annuls the sale for his sake. Similarly, if the buyer had made a kitaba contract with the child, or gave him as a pledge, or leased him, or made a kitaba contract with the mother, or gave her as a pledge, or married her, then the claim was made, because these incidents are subject to annulment, so all that is annulled and the claim is valid, contrary to freeing (i'taq) and tadbir (declaration of freedom after death), as has passed, and contrary to the case where the buyer claimed him first, then the seller claimed him, where lineage from the seller is not established because the lineage established from the buyer is not subject to annulment, so it becomes like his freeing. Translation: Someone sold a slave who was born in his possession. The buyer sold him to another. Then the first seller claimed his lineage, then he is his son, and the sale will be void. Because the sale is subject to annulment. And the right of claim that the seller has is not subject to annulment, so due to it the sale will be broken. Similarly, if the buyer made a kitaba contract with the child, or gave him as a pledge, or married him, then the claim was made. Because these incidents are subject to annulment, so all of them will be broken and the claim will be valid, contrary to freeing and tadbir, as has passed, and contrary to the case where the buyer claimed him first, then the seller claimed him: lineage from the seller will not be established, because the lineage established from the buyer is not subject to annulment, so it becomes like his freeing. Explanation: A person sold a slave who was born in his possession, i.e., the original conception was in his ownership. The buyer sold him to another. Then the first seller claimed his lineage. Then lineage will be established from him, and both the first and second sales will be void. Because sale is a thing that can be broken, and the seller has a right of claiming lineage that cannot be broken. So due to the right of claim, the sale will be broken. Similarly, if the buyer made a kitaba contract with the child, or gave him as a pledge, or married him to someone, then the seller claimed the lineage of this child, then also the same ruling applies: these dispositions will be broken. Because these incidents are such that they can be broken. But the seller's claim cannot be broken. His statement: 'Contrary to the case where he claimed him...' And if the buyer first claimed the child, then the seller claimed him, then lineage from the seller will not be established. Because the lineage that is established from the buyer is not subject to annulment, so it becomes as if the buyer had freed him. Question: From freeing, actual freedom is established, and from claim of lineage, a right to freedom is established; so how are they equal? And the seller's and buyer's claim of lineage are equal in establishing a right to freedom, so where is the preponderance? Answer: In terms of not being subject to annulment, freeing and claim are equal. As for preponderance, the buyer's claim is preferred over the seller's claim in the sense that the child, through the first claim of lineage, becomes independent at a time when there is no one to oppose him.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents a clear ruling from the Hanafi matn regarding the sale of a slave born in the seller's ownership. The ruling states that if the original seller later claims the slave as his son (i.e., establishes paternity), the sale is void because the right to claim paternity is an irrevocable right that overrides any voidable contracts. The commentary explains that this applies not only to sale but also to other contracts such as kitaba (contract of manumission by payment), pledge (rahn), lease (ijara), and marriage (tazwij) – all of which are subject to annulment when the paternity claim is made. However, if the buyer had already freed the slave (i'taq) or made a tadbir (declaration that the slave will be free after the master's death), then the claim is not accepted because these are irrevocable. The same applies if the buyer had already claimed the child's paternity first: that claim establishes lineage irrevocably, so a subsequent claim by the seller is invalid. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: how can a mere claim of paternity (which gives only a right to freedom) be equal to actual freeing (which gives real freedom)? The answer is that both are equally irrevocable (la yahtamilu al-naqd), and the buyer's claim is preponderant because it was made first without any opposing claimant.

Key Terms
محتمل النقض (muhtamil al-naqd): Capable of being annulled or revoked. In this context, certain contracts like sale, kitaba, pledge, lease, and marriage can be voided by a paternity claim.
دعوى النسب (da'wa al-nasab): A legal claim of paternity, which if proven (by evidence or acknowledgement), establishes lineage and carries certain rights.
عتاق (i'taq): Freeing a slave; an irrevocable act that cannot be undone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer sells a slave to a third party, and then the original seller claims the slave as his son, what happens to the sale contracts?
A) Both sales remain valid because the slave was born in the seller's possession.
B) The first sale is void, but the second sale remains valid.
C) Both sales are void because the paternity claim is irrevocable.
D) Only the second sale is void because the buyer had no right to sell.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: an irrevocable right (like paternity) overrides voidable contracts; but if the same right was established earlier by another, it cannot be overridden.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Seller's Paternity Claim and Its Effect on Transactions Involving the Slave
Ruling on seller's paternity claim invalidating subsequent dispositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a seller's paternity claim over a sold slave and apply it to distinguish between revocable and irrevocable transactions.

Core Idea
A seller's paternity claim invalidates the sale and revocable transactions, but not emancipation.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, lineage (nasab) is a fundamental right that can retroactively nullify certain property transactions. This passage from al-Hidayah explores the legal effect of a seller's claim that a slave he sold is actually his free child. The ruling hinges on whether the transaction in question is subject to revocation (naqd) or irreversible. By understanding this distinction, you can determine which of the buyer's acts survive the seller's claim and which are voided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the seller's claim of paternity (da'wah) over a slave born in his possession establishes the slave as the seller's free son. The initial sale between seller and buyer becomes void (batil) retroactively.
The claim is valid only if the slave was born in the seller's ownership.
2
List the transactions subject to revocation
Identify which subsequent transactions by the buyer are also invalidated: sale, kitabah (contract of manumission by payment), rahn (pledge), ijara (lease), and tazwij (marriage) of the slave or the mother. These are all revocable because they do not establish permanent freedom.
The common factor is that these transactions can be undone; they lack finality.
3
Identify the exceptions: irreversible acts
Emancipation (i'taq) and tadbir (postponed emancipation) are not revoked because they establish permanent freedom, which is stronger than the lineage claim.
The principle: freedom once granted cannot be revoked, even by a later paternity claim.
4
Compare with the buyer's prior claim
If the buyer claimed paternity first, the seller's later claim fails because the lineage from the buyer is irrevocable once established. This parallels the rule for emancipation.
Priority matters: the first claim (whether by buyer or seller) prevails, but only if the transaction is revocable.
5
Understand the talil (legal reasoning)
The rationale is that the right to claim lineage (haqq al-da'wah) is superior to property rights (like sale) but not superior to the right of freedom. Therefore, it can revoke property transactions but not acts that confer freedom.
This ranking of rights is a distinctive feature of Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ بَاعَ عَبْدًا وُلِدَ عِنْدَهُ وَبَاعَهُ الْمُشْتَرِي مِنْ آخَرَ ثُمَّ ادَّعَاهُ الْبَائِعُ الْأَوَّلُ فَهُوَ ابْنُهُ وَيَبْطُلُ الْبَيْعُ لِأَنَّ الْبَيْعَ يَحْتَمِلُ النَّقْضَ وَمَا لَهُ مِنْ حَقِّ الدَّعْوَةِ لَا يَحْتَمِلُهُ فَيَنْقُضُ الْبَيْعُ لِأَجْلِهِ وَكَذَا إِذَا كَاتَبَ الْوَلَدَ أَوْ رَهَنَهُ أَوْ آجَرَهُ أَوْ كَاتَبَ الْأُمَّ أَوْ رَهَنَهَا أَوْ زَوَّجَهَا ثُمَّ كَانَتِ الدَّعْوَةُ لِأَنَّ هَذِهِ الْعَوَارِضَ تَحْتَمِلُ النَّقْضَ فَيُنْقَضُ ذَلِكَ كُلُّهُ وَتَصِحُّ الدَّعْوَةُ بِخِلَافِ الْإِعْتَاقِ وَالتَّدْبِيرِ عَلَى مَا مَرَّ وَبِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا ادَّعَاهُ الْمُشْتَرِي أَوَّلًا ثُمَّ ادَّعَاهُ الْبَائِعُ حَيْثُ لَا يَثْبُتُ النَّسَبُ مِنَ الْبَائِعِ لِأَنَّ النَّسَبَ الثَّابِتَ مِنَ الْمُشْتَرِي لَا يَحْتَمِلُ النَّقْضَ فَصَارَ كَاعْتِاقِهِ
Whoever sold a slave who was born in his possession, and the buyer sold him to another, then the first seller claims him, then he is his son and the sale becomes void because the sale is subject to revocation, and his right of claim is not subject to revocation, so the sale is revoked for its sake. Likewise if he made a kitabah on the child, or pledged him as collateral, or leased him, or made a kitabah on the mother, or pledged her, or married her, and then the claim occurs, because these transactions are subject to revocation, so all that is revoked and the claim becomes valid, unlike emancipation and tadbir as previously mentioned, and unlike when the buyer claimed first then the seller claimed, where lineage is not established from the seller because the lineage established from the buyer is not subject to revocation, so it became like his emancipation.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn of al-Hidayah. The issue: a seller claims paternity of a slave he sold, who was conceived in his ownership. The claim invalidates the sale and any subsequent revocable transactions (like kitabah, rahn, ijara, tazwij) by the buyer, because these are all subject to legal revocation. However, if the buyer had emancipated or made a tadbir (postponed emancipation) on the slave, those are not revoked because they establish permanent freedom. Similarly, if the buyer claimed paternity first, the seller's later claim fails because the established lineage cannot be overturned. The rationale is that lineage claims are stronger than sale contracts but weaker than irreversible acts like emancipation.

Key Terms
دعوة (da'wah): A claim of paternity (lineage) by a seller over a slave born in his possession.
نقض (naqd): Legal revocation or invalidation of a transaction due to a superior right.
إعتاق (i'taq): Emancipation of a slave, granting freedom.
تدبير (tadbir): A contract where the slave is freed upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a seller claims paternity of a sold slave, which of the following buyer's actions would NOT be invalidated?
A) Leasing the slave
B) Pledging the slave as collateral
C) Emancipating the slave
D) Selling the slave to another

Key Takeaway
A seller's paternity claim over a slave born in his possession invalidates the sale and all revocable transactions, but not emancipation or tadbir, and a prior claim by the buyer blocks the seller's claim.

Claiming Lineage of One Twin Establishes Both
Ruling on lineage of twins, including case of sale and emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that claiming lineage of one twin establishes both, and evaluate its effect on prior sales and emancipations.

Core Idea
Claiming one twin establishes both as free sons; prior sales become void retroactively.

This lesson examines a distinctive Hanafi ruling: when a seller claims paternity over one of twin slaves, both are established as his free children. The key is that twins are conceived from one fluid and born within six months, so they necessarily share the same lineage. The passage from al-Hidayah, quoting al-Jami' al-Saghir, illustrates the practical consequences: the buyer's emancipation is void because the child was always free. We will unpack the reasoning and the distinction from a single child case.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the Hanafi rule: claiming lineage of one twin establishes the lineage of both. This is because they are from one fluid (min ma'in wahid).
The rule is based on the impossibility of separate conception within six months.
2
Explain the rationale
Understand why: twins are born less than six months apart, and the minimum pregnancy is six months. Therefore, the second twin cannot have been conceived after the first; they share one conception.
This is a biological fact recognized by Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply to the al-Jami' al-Saghir case
Work through the scenario: seller has twin slaves, sells one, buyer frees him, then seller claims the remaining twin as his son.
The seller's claim is made while he still possesses the other twin.
4
Determine the consequences
Conclude that both twins become free sons of the seller. The sale and emancipation are void because the sold twin was free from the start (hurr al-asl).
The invalidation is not because the claim directly cancels the sale, but because the child's free status is revealed.
5
Distinguish from single child and other cases
Contrast with a single child: there, the emancipation is void directly due to the claim. Also note the sub-case where conception was not in the seller's ownership—only the claimed child is freed (da'wat tahrir).
This distinction demonstrates the limited scope of the claim when the original conception is not in the claimant's ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنِ ادَّعَى نَسَبَ أَحَدِ التَّوْأَمَيْنِ ثَبَتَ نَسَبُهُمَا مِنْهُ لِأَنَّهُمَا مِنْ مَاءٍ وَاحِدٍ فَمِنْ ضَرُورَةِ ثُبُوتِ نَسَبِ أَحَدِهِمَا ثُبُوتُ نَسَبِ الْآخَرِ وَهَذَا لِأَنَّ التَّوْأَمَيْنِ وَلَدَانِ بَيْنَ وِلَادَتِهِمَا أَقَلُّ مِنْ سِتَّةِ أَشْهُرٍ فَلَا يُتَصَوَّرُ عُلُوقُ الثَّانِي حَادِثًا لِأَنَّهُ لَا حَمْلَ أَقَلَّ مِنْ سِتَّةِ أَشْهُرٍ. وَفِي الْجَامِعِ الصَّغِيرِ: إِذَا كَانَ فِي يَدِهِ غُلَامَانِ تَوْأَمَانِ وُلِدَا عِنْدَهُ فَبَاعَ أَحَدَهُمَا وَاعْتَقَهُ الْمُشْتَرِي ثُمَّ ادَّعَى الْبَائِعُ الَّذِي فِي يَدِهِ فَهُمَا ابْنَاهُ وَبَطَلَ عِتْقُ الْمُشْتَرِي لِأَنَّهُ لَمَّا ثَبَتَ نَسَبُ الْوَلَدِ الَّذِي عِنْدَهُ لِمُصَادَفَةِ الْعُلُوقِ وَالدَّعْوَةُ مِلْكُهُ إِذِ الْمَسْأَلَةُ مَفْرُوضَةٌ فِيهِ ثَبَتَ بِهِ حُرِّيَّةُ الْأَصْلِ فَيَثْبُتُ نَسَبُ الْآخَرِ وَحُرِّيَّةُ الْأَصْلِ فِيهِ ضَرُورَةً لِأَنَّهُمَا تَوْأَمَانِ فَتَبَيَّنَ أَنَّ عِتْقَ الْمُشْتَرِي وَشِرَاءَهُ لَاقَى حُرَّ الْأَصْلِ فَبَطَلَ. بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا كَانَ الْوَلَدُ وَاحِدًا لِأَنَّ هُنَاكَ يَبْطُلُ الْعِتْقُ فِيهِ مَقْصُودًا لِحَقِّ دَعْوَةِ الْبَائِعِ وَهَهُنَا ثَبَتَ تَبَعًا لِحُرِّيَّةِ الْأَصْلِ فَافْتَرَقَا. وَلَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ أَصْلُ الْعُلُوقِ فِي مِلْكِهِ ثَبَتَ نَسَبُ الْوَلَدِ الَّذِي عِنْدَهُ وَلَا يَنْقُضُ الْبَيْعُ فِيمَا بَاعَ لِأَنَّ هَذِهِ دَعْوَةُ تَحْرِيرٍ لِانْعِدَامِ شَاهِدِ الِاتِّصَالِ فَيُقْتَصَرُ عَلَى مَحَلِّ وِلَايَتِهِ
Whoever claimed the lineage of one of twins, the lineage of both is established from him because they are from one fluid. So from the necessity of establishing the lineage of one of them is the establishment of the lineage of the other. This is because twins are two children between whose birth there is less than six months, so the conception of the second occurring later is inconceivable because there is no pregnancy less than six months. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If there are in his possession two twin slaves born in his presence, and he sells one of them and the buyer emancipates him, then the seller claims the one in his possession, then both are his sons and the buyer's emancipation is void because when the lineage of the child with him is established due to the occurrence of conception and the claim is his property since the case is assumed in him, thereby the original freedom is established. Then the lineage of the other and original freedom in him are established necessarily because they are twins. So it becomes clear that the buyer's emancipation and purchase encountered a free original, so it is void. Unlike when the child is single, because there the emancipation is void intendedly due to the seller's right of claim, and here it is established secondarily due to original freedom, so they differ. And if the original conception was not in his ownership, the lineage of the child with him is established and the sale is not revoked regarding what he sold because this is a claim of liberation due to the absence of evidence of connection, so it is confined to the place of his authority.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from al-Hidayah concerning twins. The key principle: because twins share a single conception (from one fluid), establishing the lineage of one necessarily establishes the lineage of the other. The minimum period of pregnancy is six months, so twins born less than six months apart must be from the same conception. Al-Jami' al-Saghir provides a scenario: a seller has two twin slaves born to him, sells one, the buyer frees him. The seller then claims the remaining twin. The seller's claim establishes both as his free sons, retroactively invalidating the buyer's emancipation and purchase because the sold twin is revealed to be originally free. This differs from a single child case where the emancipation is void directly due to the seller's claim; here it is void as a secondary consequence of the original freedom. A further condition: if the original conception was not in the seller's ownership, only the child in his possession is claimed, and the sale of the other is not invalidated because the claim is one of liberation (the child is free), not lineage.

Key Terms
توأمان (taw'aman): Twins born from the same pregnancy, less than six months apart.
دعوة تحرير (da'wat tahrir): A claim of liberation (that a child is free) when the original conception was not in the claimant's ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of twins from al-Jami' al-Saghir, why is the buyer's emancipation of the sold twin void?
A) Because the seller's claim invalidates all buyer's actions
B) Because the sold twin is revealed to be originally free
C) Because the buyer had no right to emancipate
D) Because the claim was made before the emancipation

Key Takeaway
Establishing the lineage of one twin necessarily establishes the other because they share a single conception, retroactively freeing both and invalidating prior transactions.

The Effect of Denial on Acknowledgment of Lineage
Intra-Hanafi dispute on nullification of acknowledgment by denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the nullification of an acknowledgment of lineage by denial, and apply the Hanafi principle to determine the validity of a later claim.

Core Idea
Lineage once established cannot be broken; an acknowledgment of lineage is not nullified by the mere denial of the person acknowledged.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a critical intra-madhhab dispute. The core question: if a seller acknowledges a child's lineage to someone else to prevent a later claim, and then the buyer (the person acknowledged) denies the acknowledgment, does the acknowledgment collapse? The answer differs between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). Understanding this difference is essential for applying the Hanafi legal method to cases involving lineage and acknowledgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenario
First, grasp the fact pattern: a child slave is sold, and the seller, fearing a later claim of lineage (e.g., someone claiming the child as his son), makes an acknowledgment (iqrar) that the child belongs to someone else. Later, that someone else (the buyer) denies the acknowledgment. The dispute is whether that denial nullifies the acknowledgment, thus allowing the seller to later claim the child himself.
The child's lineage is at stake; the acknowledgment is a defensive move by the seller.
2
State the Sahibayn's position and their evidence
The Sahibayn argue that denial makes the acknowledgment 'as if it never was' (kaan lam yakun). They draw analogy (qiyas) to cases where coercion and jest nullify an acknowledgment, and to the case of wala' (patronage) where a buyer's denial nullifies the seller's acknowledgment of manumission.
They emphasize that while lineage itself is permanent, the acknowledgment of it can be nullified, just as coercion/jest nullify the act of acknowledgment.
3
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and his evidence
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that lineage, once established, cannot be undone; therefore an acknowledgment of lineage remains valid even if the person acknowledged denies it. His evidence: like testimony to lineage, once given and rejected due to something extrinsic, the witness cannot later claim the child himself. The rights of the child and the person acknowledged have already attached to the lineage through the acknowledgment.
Abu Hanifa distinguishes lineage from wala': wala' can be overridden by a stronger claim (e.g., paternal side pulling wala'), but lineage is permanent.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Sahibayn's analogy
Al-Marghinani (the author of Al-Hidayah) responds on behalf of Abu Hanifa by challenging the analogy to wala'. He notes that the wala' case itself is disputed (Abu Hanifa disagrees with its application) and even if conceded, wala' is inherently weaker and can be broken by a stronger claim, whereas lineage cannot. The analogy to coercion/jest is also distinguished because those affect the integrity of the consent, not the nature of the thing acknowledged.
The analytical move is to reject the qiyas by pointing out a crucial difference (farq) between lineage and wala'.
5
Apply the dispute to a legal stratagem (hilah)
The passage concludes by presenting a hilah based on Abu Hanifa's principle: the seller can have a third party acknowledge the child's lineage, and that acknowledgment will stand regardless of denial or silence. Alternatively, the seller can acknowledge the child as the son of a deceased person to avoid the possibility of denial. This shows how the ruling translates into practical legal planning.
The hilah works only if Abu Hanifa's view is operative (mufta bihi). The text implies his view is preferred.

سکوت اختیار کرے۔ بہر حال مقر کا دعوی صحیح ہوگا۔ اور بقول شیخ مرسی جو حیلہ صاحبین کے قول پر بھی جاری ہو سکتا ہے۔ یہ ہے کہ بائع اقرار کر لے کہ یہ غلام فلاں میت کا بیٹا ہے تو اس کی طرف سے تکذیب نہ ہوگی اور اس کے بعد مقر کا دعوی صحیح نہیں ہوسکتا۔
Excerpted from its principle regarding one who sells a child and fears a claim [of lineage] after that, so he cuts off the claim by [asserting] lineage to another. Translation: The evidence of the two companions (Sahibayn) is that by the rejection of the slave, the acknowledgment becomes as if it never was, and the acknowledgment of lineage is nullified by rejection. Although lineage is not susceptible to being broken, do you not see that in it coercion and jest take effect? So it becomes like the case where the buyer acknowledges to the seller that he freed the purchased [slave] and the seller denies it. Then the buyer says 'I freed him' so the patronage (wala') transfers to him. Contrary to when the seller confirms him, because he then claims a lineage that is established from another. And contrary to when he neither confirms nor denies, because the right of the person acknowledged (muqarr lahu) has become attached due to the acknowledgment on the basis of his possible confirmation. So this becomes like the child of a woman of li'an, whose lineage is not established from anyone other than the man who imprecated (mula'in), because the imprecator has the option to retract his accusation. The evidence of Imam Abu Hanifa is that lineage is something that, once established, is not susceptible to being broken, and an acknowledgment of such a thing is not nullified by rejection, so it remains. Therefore the claim of the acknowledger of lineage is prevented. Just as if someone testified to the lineage of a young child against another, and due to some accusation his testimony was rejected, then he himself claimed the child, his claim is not accepted. And this is because by the acknowledgment, the right of the person acknowledged has become attached to the lineage, considering that he may confirm it, to the extent that if after denial he confirms, the lineage is established from the person acknowledged. Similarly, the right of the child has also become attached, so it will not be nullified by the mere rejection of the person acknowledged. The issue of wala' is based on this same difference. Even if it is conceded, wala' is sometimes invalidated by the occurrence of a stronger factor, like the pulling of wala' from the mother's side to the father's tribe. And here, a stronger factor has come upon the suspended wala', namely the claim of the buyer, so the wala' is invalidated, unlike lineage. And this, according to Imam Abu Hanifa's principle, can serve as a way out for the person who sells a young child and then fears a claim of lineage against it: he can acknowledge the child's lineage to someone else, thereby cutting off the claim. Explanation: His statement 'for them the acknowledgment...' The evidence of the two companions is that when the acknowledgment of the acknowledger becomes effective due to the denial of the person acknowledged, it becomes as if it never was, so the claim is valid. It may be said that 'as if it never was' is not valid because lineage is among things not susceptible to being broken. The author of Hidayah answers from the two companions: lineage indeed cannot be broken, but the acknowledgment of lineage can be nullified by rejection. Do you not see that in it coercion and jest take effect? Thus, if someone is compelled to acknowledge that his slave is his son and he acknowledges, lineage is not established. Similarly, if someone jokingly says 'he is my son', lineage is not established. His statement 'so it becomes as if...' The ruling of this mentioned case becomes like that: if the buyer acknowledges to the seller that he freed the purchased (slave) before the sale, then the buyer's manumission is valid and wala' transfers to him. Contrary to if the possessor of the child slave confirms the master and says 'yes, he is my son', then the master's claim is not established, because after the acknowledgment of the slave, he is claiming a lineage that is established from another, i.e., he himself has acknowledged it. Also, if the master's slave neither confirms nor denies, the master's claim does not establish lineage either, because the right of the slave has become attached to the child's lineage, considering that if the slave confirms, his lineage is established. So this child becomes like the child of li'an, whose lineage is not established from anyone other than the mula'in, because the mula'in has the option to retract his accusation. Thus, if he says 'I lied or made a mistake in accusing my wife of adultery', the child's lineage is established from him. His statement 'and for Abu Hanifa...' Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence is that lineage is among things that, once established, are not susceptible to being broken, and an acknowledgment of such a thing remains as it is, even if it is not established for the person acknowledged. Therefore his claim based on his own acknowledgment is void. Like a person who testifies to the lineage of a young child against another, and due to some accusation his testimony is rejected, then he himself claims the child, his claim is not accepted. His statement 'and this is because...' The reason it is not nullified is that by the acknowledgment, the right of the person acknowledged has become attached to the lineage, considering that he may confirm it. To the extent that if after denial he confirms, the lineage is established from the person acknowledged. And since establishment of lineage after denial is permissible, his right of claim remains, and with the survival of his right, the claim of the acknowledger is not valid. Also, by this acknowledgment, the child's right has become attached, because he needs lineage, so it will not be nullified by the mere rejection of the person acknowledged. His statement 'and the issue of wala'...' The answer to the two companions' citation of the wala' issue is that firstly, it does not constitute a proof, because the wala' issue itself is subject to this same difference; i.e., according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer will not get wala'. Even if we concede that the wala' issue is agreed upon, there is a difference between wala' and lineage: wala' is something that is invalidated by the occurrence of a stronger factor, like the pulling of wala' from the mother's side to the father's tribe. And here a stronger factor has come upon the suspended wala', namely the buyer's claim, so the suspended wala' is broken. Unlike lineage, which does not break. So analogy of lineage to wala' is not valid. His statement 'like the pulling of wala'...' The scenario of pulling wala' is that a person frees his slave girl, and the freed slave girl marries Zayd's slave, and they have children. If the children commit a crime, the 'aql (blood money) of the children will be upon the maternal patrons (mawali al-umm), because the children's father is not among the people of wala'. So the children are joined to the mother's tribe, and their wala' follows the mother's wala' to her manumitter. But if the father is freed, he pulls the wala' of the children to his own manumitter. So it is known that when a stronger wala' comes upon a weaker wala', it breaks the weaker and takes it to itself. In any case, wala' can break, but lineage cannot. His statement 'upon the suspended wala'...' By 'suspended wala'' is meant that which belongs to the seller. It is called suspended because it is in abeyance until confirmation after denial is found, then it becomes certain from his side. So it breaks due to the buyer's claim. His statement 'and this can serve as a way out...' The author of Hidayah says: This mentioned acknowledgment of the seller can, according to Imam Abu Hanifa's principle, be a legal stratagem (hilah) for him: if someone sells a young slave and fears that afterwards someone might claim him as his son and the sale would be nullified, the way to cut off his claim is that he gets someone else to acknowledge the slave's lineage; this acknowledgment will not break, because whether the person acknowledges confirms, denies, or remains silent, the claim of the acknowledger will be valid. And according to Shaykh Mursi, a stratagem that can also work according to the two companions' view is that the seller acknowledges that this slave is the son of a certain deceased person; then there will be no denial from that side, and afterwards the claim of the claimant cannot be valid.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), regarding the effect of a buyer's denial on the seller's prior acknowledgment of a child slave's lineage. The discussion arises in the context of the Hidayah's commentary on a case where a seller, fearing a future claim of lineage against the child, makes an acknowledgment to cut off that claim. The Sahibayn argue that if the buyer (the one to whom acknowledgment was made) denies the acknowledgment, the acknowledgment becomes null and void, allowing the seller to later claim the child himself. They analogize this to cases of coercion and jest nullifying an acknowledgment, and to the case of wala' (patronage) where an acknowledgment of manumission is nullified by the buyer's denial. Imam Abu Hanifa, however, insists that lineage, once established, cannot be undone; therefore an acknowledgment of lineage remains effective regardless of denial, preventing the seller from later claiming the child. He distinguishes lineage from wala', noting that wala' can be overridden by a stronger claim (e.g., when a father's manumitter pulls wala' from the mother's side), but lineage is permanent. The passage then presents a legal stratagem (hilah) based on Abu Hanifa's principle: the seller can arrange for a third party to acknowledge the child's lineage, and that acknowledgment will stick, cutting off any later claim. Alternatively, according to Shaykh Mursi, the seller can acknowledge the child as the son of a deceased person, eliminating the possibility of denial. This entire discussion highlights the Hanafi legal method of distinguishing between rulings based on the nature of the right involved and using analogical reasoning with careful attention to differences.

Key Terms
نسب (nasab): Lineage or paternal filiation; a legal relationship that once established is considered permanent and cannot be undone by subsequent denial.
اقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession; a legal declaration by which a person admits a right or relationship.
ولاء (wala'): Patronage; the legal relationship of clientage established by manumission or contract, carrying inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is the seller's acknowledgment of a child's lineage not nullified by the buyer's denial?
A) Because the buyer has no right to deny
B) Because lineage once established cannot be undone
C) Because the seller can change his mind
D) Because the child must confirm

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, lineage is permanent; an acknowledgment of lineage remains valid even if the person acknowledged later denies it, a principle that allows for strategic legal planning (hiyal).

Permissible Legal Stratagem (Hila) to Prevent a Claim of Paternity
Describes a hila to preempt paternity claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi hila to preempt a paternity claim against a seller of a minor slave and identify its conditions.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of lineage is unbreakable: use it as a shield before a paternity claim arises.

This lesson explores a practical legal stratagem (hila) from the Hidayah, designed to protect a seller of a minor slave from a future claim of paternity that would invalidate the sale. The stratagem relies on a fundamental Hanafi principle: once lineage is acknowledged by a competent party, that acknowledgment is binding and cannot be undone. We will examine two variations of the hila and their underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Understand the risk: after selling a minor slave, someone might claim the slave as his son, thereby establishing the seller's lack of ownership and nullifying the sale. The seller needs a preemptive legal protection.
The sale of a minor slave is valid only if the seller owns him; a paternity claim would retroactively prove he was never property.
2
State the first hila (Abu Hanifa's basis)
The seller arranges for a third party (a stranger) to acknowledge that the slave is his son. Under Abu Hanifa's principle, this acknowledgment is irrevocable regardless of the third party's subsequent confirmation, denial, or silence.
The third party's later stance does not affect the acknowledgment's validity.
3
State the second hila (Sahibayn's basis)
The seller himself acknowledges that the slave is the son of a deceased person. Since the deceased cannot deny, the acknowledgment stands unchallenged, preventing any future claim.
This variation works even on the Sahibayn's view because there is no one to challenge the acknowledgment.
4
Analyze the legal principle
The common thread: acknowledgment of lineage, once valid, is binding and cannot be rescinded or contradicted by later claims. This principle is the foundation of the hila.
This is a substantive Hanafi rule in family law, extended here to sales.
5
Evaluate the stratagem's permissibility
Note that this hila is not deceitful or fraudulent; it uses a valid legal mechanism (acknowledgment) to protect a legitimate sale. The Hanafi school permits such stratagems when the means are lawful.
Other schools may be more restrictive, but within Hanafi fiqh, this is a recognized device.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وهذا يصلح مخرجا ..... صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ بائع کا اقرار مذکور امام ابوحنیفہ کے اصول پر اس کا حلیہ بن سکتا ہے۔ کہ اگر کسی نے کوئی صغیر غلام فروخت کیا اور اس کو یہ اندیشہ ہوا۔ کہ ممکن ہے اس کے بعد کوئی شخص اپنے پسر ہونے کا دعوی کرے اور بیع ٹوٹ جائے۔ تو اس کے دعوی کو کاٹنے کا طریقہ یہ ہے کہ وہ کسی غیر سے اس کے نسب کا اقرار کرا دے پس یہ اقرار نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ اس لئے کہ وہ تصدیق کرے یا تکذیب یا سکوت اختیار کرے۔ بہر حال مقر کا دعوی صحیح ہوگا۔ اور بقول شیخ مرسی جو حیلہ صاحبین کے قول پر بھی جاری ہو سکتا ہے۔ یہ ہے کہ بائع اقرار کر لے کہ یہ غلام فلاں میت کا بیٹا ہے تو اس کی طرف سے تکذیب نہ ہوگی اور اس کے بعد مقر کا دعوی صحیح نہیں ہوسکتا۔
His statement: And this can serve as a way out... Sahib al-Hidaya says: The seller's aforementioned acknowledgment can, according to Abu Hanifa's principle, be made into a legal stratagem. That is, if someone sells a minor slave and fears that later someone might claim him as a son and the sale be rescinded, the way to counter that claim is to have a third party acknowledge the child's lineage. Then this acknowledgment will not be broken, because whether the third party confirms, denies, or remains silent, the acknowledger's claim will be valid. Also, according to Shaykh Mursi, a stratagem that also works on the Sahibayn's view is that the seller himself acknowledge that the slave is the son of a deceased person; then there will be no denial from that side, and subsequent claimants cannot successfully claim.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents a permissible legal stratagem (hila) from the Hidayah commentary to protect a seller of a minor slave from later claims of paternity that could invalidate the sale. According to Abu Hanifa's principle that lineage once acknowledged is unbreakable, the seller can have an unrelated third party acknowledge that the child is his son. This third-party acknowledgment stands regardless of the child's later confirmation or denial, preventing the seller from losing the sale. Alternatively, according to Shaykh Mursi, the seller himself can acknowledge the child as the son of a deceased person, which also blocks future claims because there is no one to deny it. This works even on the Sahibayn's view because the acknowledgment is not subject to the same repudiation. The stratagem is a practical application of the legal principle that acknowledgment of lineage is binding and cannot be undone.

Key Terms
حیلہ (hila): A legal stratagem or permissible device to achieve a lawful end without violating the spirit of the law.
بائع (ba'i'): Seller, the person selling the minor slave in this context.
اقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or admission; in legal context, a statement by a person that establishes a right or status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The Sahibayn completely reject all legal stratagems.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a preemptive acknowledgment of lineage can be used as a valid legal stratagem to protect a sale against future paternity claims.

Child of a Purchased Slave-Girl Who Is Subsequently Claimed
States ruling and reasoning for child of a claimed slave-girl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the child of a maghrur, explain the basis in Ijma, and apply the rule of prevention (man') to determine liability and valuation date.

Core Idea
The deceived father's liability arises from prevention, not intercourse, and is assessed on litigation day.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common scenario in classical Islamic commerce: a person buys a slave-girl, she gives birth, and later a third party proves ownership of the slave-girl. What happens to the child? The Hanafi ruling, grounded in the Ijma of the Companions, establishes a nuanced position: the child is free in relation to the father but a slave in relation to the true owner, with the father paying compensation. The key factor is 'prevention' — the father's act of withholding the child triggers liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the core dispute
Recognize the buyer (father), the slave-girl, the child, and the claimant who proves ownership. The ruling concerns the child's status and the father's liability.
2
Understand the 'maghrur' concept
The father is a maghrur — he had intercourse under the mistaken belief of ownership. This is a protected status, not usurpation. His child is 'free by value'.
Key term: مَغْرُور (maghrūr)
3
Analyze the basis of liability: prevention (man')
The father is liable only because he prevents the true owner from taking the child. This occurs when he litigates (i.e., refuses to hand over the child). The valuation date is therefore the day of litigation, not birth or purchase.
Key term: مَنْع (manʿ)
4
Examine the derived cases
The text applies the prevention principle to various scenarios: child dies (no liability, no prevention), father kills child (liability, prevention exists), another kills child (father receives diya but must pay value to owner). The father can recover the value from the seller of the slave-girl due to warranty.
5
Validate the proof: Ijma of the Companions
The ruling is supported by Ijma (consensus of the Companions), making it a definitive (qat'i) source. No ikhtilaf is reported on this basic ruling.
Dalil is Ijma, which is a primary source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنِ اشْتَرَى جَارِيَةً فَوَلَدَتْ وَلَدًا عِنْدَهُ فَاسْتَحَقَّهَا رَجُلٌ غَرِمَ الْأَبُ قِيمَةَ الْوَلَدِ يَوْمَ يُخَاصِمُ لِأَنَّهُ وَلَدُ الْمَغْرُورِ فَإِنَّ الْمَغْرُورَ مَنْ يَطَأُ امْرَأَةً مُعْتَمِدًا عَلَى مِلْكِ يَمِينٍ أَوْ نِكَاحٍ فَتَلِدُ مِنْهُ ثُمَّ تُسْتَحَقُّ وَوَلَدُ الْمَغْرُورِ حُرٌّ بِالْقِيمَةِ بِإِجْمَاعِ الصَّحَابَةِ وَلِأَنَّ النَّظَرَ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ وَاجِبٌ فَيُجْعَلُ الْوَلَدُ حُرَّ الْأَصْلِ فِي حَقِّ أَبِيهِ رَقِيقًا فِي حَقِّ مُدَّعِيهِ نَظَرًا لَهُمَا ثُمَّ الْوَلَدُ حَاصِلٌ فِي يَدِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ صُنْعِهِ فَلَا يَضْمَنُهُ إِلَّا بِالْمَنْعِ كَمَا فِي وَلَدِ الْمَغْصُوبَةِ فَلِهَذَا تُعْتَبَرُ قِيمَةُ الْوَلَدِ يَوْمَ الْخُصُومَةِ لِأَنَّهُ يَوْمُ الْمَنْعِ وَلَوْ مَاتَ الْوَلَدُ لَا شَيْءَ عَلَى الْأَبِ لِانْعِدَامِ الْمَنْعِ وَكَذَا لَوْ تَرَكَ مَالًا لِأَنَّ الْإِرْثَ لَيْسَ بِبَدَلٍ عَنْهُ وَالْمَالُ لِأَبِيهِ لِأَنَّهُ حُرُّ الْأَصْلِ فِي حَقِّهِ فَيَرِثُهُ وَلَوْ قَتَلَهُ الْأَبُ غَرِمَ قِيمَتَهُ لِوُجُودِ الْمَنْعِ وَكَذَا لَوْ قَتَلَهُ غَيْرُهُ فَأَخَذَ دِيَتَهُ لِأَنَّ سَلَامَةَ بَدَلِهِ كَسَلَامَتِهِ لَهُ وَمَنْعُ بَدَلِهِ كَمَنْعِهِ فَيَغْرَمُ قِيمَتَهُ كَمَا إِذَا كَانَ حَيًّا وَيَرْجِعُ بِقِيمَةِ الْوَلَدِ عَلَى بَائِعِهِ لِأَنَّهُ ضَمِنَ لَهُ سَلَامَتَهُ كَمَا يَرْجِعُ بِثَمَنِهِ بِخِلَافِ الْعُقَرِ لِأَنَّهُ لَزِمَهُ لِاسْتِيفَاءِ مَنَافِعِهَا فَلَا يَرْجِعُ بِهِ عَلَى الْبَائِعِ وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ بِالصَّوَابِ.
He said: And whoever buys a slave-girl and she gives birth to a child in his possession, then a man proves her ownership, the father must pay the value of the child on the day he litigates, because it is the child of the deceived (maghrur). Indeed, the deceived is one who has intercourse with a woman relying on ownership or marriage, and she gives birth from him, then she is claimed. And the child of the deceived is free by value by Ijma of the Companions. And because consideration of both sides is obligatory, so the child is made free in origin with respect to his father, and a slave with respect to his claimant, out of consideration for them. Then the child comes into his (father's) possession without any action of his, so he is not liable for him except by prevention (man'), as in the case of the child of a usurped woman. For this reason, the value of the child is considered on the day of litigation, because it is the day of prevention. And if the child dies, nothing is upon the father due to absence of prevention. Similarly, if he leaves property, because inheritance is not a substitute for him, and the wealth belongs to his father because the child is free-origin with respect to him, so he inherits from him. And if the father kills him, he pays his value due to existence of prevention. Similarly, if someone else kills him and he takes his blood-money, because safety of his substitute is like his own safety, and prevention of his substitute is like prevention of him, so he pays his value as if he were alive. And he recovers the value of the child from the seller because the seller guaranteed his safety, just as he recovers its price. Unlike usufruct ('uqar) because it is due to enjoyment of benefits, so he does not recover it from the seller. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah deals with the case of a person who buys a slave-girl, she gives birth to a child, and then a third party proves the slave-girl was actually his property (istihqaq). The father (the buyer) is liable to pay the value of the child on the day of litigation. The child is considered 'free by value' — meaning the child is free but the father must pay compensation to the true owner. This ruling is based on the Ijma of the Companions (radiyallahu anhum). The reasoning is that the father is a 'maghrur' (deceived), having intercourse based on apparent ownership. The child's status is split: free in relation to the father (so the father inherits from him), but a slave in relation to the claimant (so the father pays his value). The father's liability arises from 'man' (prevention) — by keeping the child, he prevents the owner from taking possession. Thus, valuation is at the time of litigation (when prevention occurs). If the child dies there is no liability, since prevention is absent. If the child leaves property, the father inherits it. If the father kills the child, he pays value; if another kills him, the father receives the blood-money but must pay the value to the owner. The father can then recover the paid value from the seller of the slave-girl, since the seller warranted her safety. This differs from usufruct claims, which are not recoverable from the seller.

Key Terms
مَغْرُور (maghrūr): A person who is deceived; one who has intercourse with a woman under the mistaken belief that she is his property or wife.
مَنْع (manʿ): Prevention; the act of withholding the child from the rightful owner, which triggers liability.
قِيمَة (qīmah): Value; the monetary compensation paid for the child, assessed on the day of litigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, the child of a maghrur is considered free by value. On what basis is the child's value assessed on the day of litigation?
A) Because the child was born on that day.
B) Because the father purchased the slave-girl on that day.
C) Because litigation is the day of prevention (man').
D) Because the child reaches majority on that day.

Key Takeaway
The father's liability for the child of a claimed slave-girl arises from prevention (man'), assessed on the day of litigation, and is grounded in the Ijma of the Companions.

Child of a Deceived Buyer: Ruling and Proof
Ruling with proof on the child of a maghrur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the child of a maghrur and explain the valuation on the day of litigation, the father's recourse against the seller, and the intra-Hanafi difference regarding the 'uqur.

Core Idea
The child of a deceived buyer is free upon payment of its value, by consensus of the Companions.

This lesson examines a classic transaction case: a man buys a slave-girl, she bears a child, then a third party proves rightful ownership of the mother. The buyer is a 'maghrur' (deceived). What happens to the child? The Hanafi answer balances the father's good faith and the true owner's property right, relying on the Companions' consensus. We'll follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the father's payment for the child. The ruling is that he must pay the child's value to the true owner. The child becomes free.
The ruling is wajib on the father.
2
Locate the dalil
The author explicitly cites the consensus of the Companions (ijma' al-sahaba). This is a qat'i dalil.
The exact phrase: 'وَلَدُ الْمَغْرُورِ حُرٌّ بِالْقِيْمَةِ بِإِجْمَاعِ الصَّحَابَةِ'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The ijma proves the ruling with certainty. The reasoning behind the ijma (dual consideration) is also given: the child is free regarding the father, a slave regarding the claimant.
This dual nature resolves the conflict.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi difference on whether the father recovers the 'uqur (dower) from the seller. The text notes that according to the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) he does recover it.
The apparent position is that he does not recover the 'uqur (only the child's value).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The passage itself does not explicitly choose; it presents both. The mufta bihi in later Hanafi works may differ. Focus on the core ruling which is established by ijma.
The child's value is always paid on the day of litigation.

قَالَ وَمَنِ اشْتَرَى جَارِيَةً فَوَلَدَتْ وَلَدًا عِنْدَهُ فَاسْتَحَقَّهَا رَجُلٌ غَرِمَ الْأَبُ قِيْمَةَ الْوَلَدِ يَوْمَ يُخَاصِمُ لِأَنَّهُ وَلَدُ الْمَغْرُورِ فَإِنَّ الْمَغْرُورَ مَنْ يَطَأُ امْرَأَةً مُعْتَمِدًا عَلَى مِلْكِ يَمِينٍ أَوْ نِكَاحٍ فَتَلِدُ مِنْهُ ثُمَّ تَسْتَحِقُ وَوَلَدُ الْمَغْرُورِ حُرٌّ بِالْقِيْمَةِ بِإِجْمَاعِ الصَّحَابَةِ وَلِأَنَّ النَّظَرَ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ وَاجِبٌ فَيُجْعَلُ الْوَلَدُ حُرَّ الْأَصْلِ فِي حَقِّ أَبِيْهِ رَقِيقًا فِي حَقِّ مُدَّعِيْهِ نَظَرًا لَهُمَا ثُمَّ الْوَلْدُ حَاصِلٌ فِي يَدِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ صُنْعِهِ فَلَا يَضْمَنُهُ إِلَّا بِالْمَنْعِ كَمَا فِي وَلَدِ الْمَغْصُوبَةِ فَلِهَذَا تُعْتَبَرُ قِيمَةُ الْوَلَدِ يَوْمَ الْخُصُومَةِ لِأَنَّهُ يَوْمُ الْمَنْعِ وَلَوْ مَاتَ الْوَلَدُ لَا شَيْءَ عَلَى الْأَبِ لِانْعِدَامِ الْمَنْعِ وَكَذَا لَوْ تَرَكَ مَالًا لِأَنَّ الْإِرْثَ لَيْسَ بِبَدَلٍ عَنْهُ وَالْمَالُ لِأَبِيْهِ لِأَنَّهُ حُرُّ الْأَصْلِ فِي حَقِهِ فَيَرِثُهُ وَلَوْ قَتَلَهُ الْأَبُ غَرِمَ قِيمَتَهُ لِوُجُودِ الْمَنْعِ وَكَذَا لَوْ قَتَلَهُ غَيْرُهُ فَأَخَذَ دِيَتَهُ لِأَنَّ سَلَامَةَ بَدَلِهِ كَسَلَامَتِهِ لَهُ وَمَنْعُ بَدَلِهِ كَمَنْعِهِ فَيَغْرَمُ قِيمَتَهُ كَمَا إِذَا كَانَ حَيًّا وَيَرْجِعُ بِقِيْمَةِ الْوَلَدِ عَلَى بَائِعِهِ لِأَنَّهُ ضَمِنَ لَهُ سَلَامَتَهُ كَمَا يَرْجِعُ بِثَمَنِهِ بِخِلَافِ الْعُقَرِ لِأَنَّهُ لَزِمَهُ لِاسْتِيفَاءِ مَنَافِعِهَا فَلَا يَرْجِعُ بِهِ عَلَى الْبَائِعِ وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ بِالصَّوَابِ

ترجمہ کسی نے باندی خریدی اس نے خریدار کے پاس بچہ جنا اور کسی نے باندی کا استحقاق ثابت کر دیا۔ تو باپ بچہ کی خصومت کے روز والی قیمت کا تاوان دے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ فریب خوردہ کا بچہ ہے۔ اس لئے کہ مغرور وہ شخص ہے جو ملک یمین یا ملک نکاح کے اعتماد پر کسی عورت سے وطی کرے اور وہ اس سے بچہ بنے پھر عورت استحقاق میں لے لی جائے اور فریب خوردہ کا بچہ باجماع صحابہ بقیمت آزاد ہوتا ہے۔ اور اس لئے بھی کہ دونوں جانبوں کا لحاظ ضروری ہے۔ پس باپ کے حق میں بچہ کو اصلی آزاد اور اس کے مدعی کے حق میں رقیق قرار دیا جائے گا۔ دونوں کا لحاظ رکھتے ہوئے۔ پھر بچہ باپ کے قبضہ میں بلا تعدی آیا ہے تو وہ ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ مگر روکنے کی وجہ سے جیسے مغصوبہ باندی کے بچہ میں ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا بچہ کی وہ قیمت معتبر ہوگی جو خصومت کے روز ہے۔ کیونکہ روکنے کا دن وہی ہے۔ اور اگر بچہ مر گیا تو باپ پر کچھ تاوان نہ ہوگا روکنے نہ پائے جانے کی وجہ سے۔ اسی طرح اگر بچہ نے کچھ مال چھوڑا ہو کیونکہ میراث اس بچہ کا عوض نہیں ہے۔ اور مال اس کے باپ کا ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ وہ بچہ باپ کے حق میں اصلی آزاد ہے۔ تو باپ اس کا وارث ہوگا۔ اور اگر باپ نے بچہ کو مار ڈالا تو اس کی قیمت کا تاوان دے گا۔ اس لئے کہ باپ کو اس کا عوض ملنا بمنزلہ خود بچہ کے ملنے کے ہے۔ اور باپ کا دیت روکنا بمنزلہ بچہ روکنے کے ہے تو اس کی قیمت کا ضامن ہوگا۔ جیسے بچہ زندہ ہونے کی صورت میں ہوتا تھا۔ اور بچہ کی قیمت اپنے بائع سے واپس لے گا۔ کیونکہ بائع مشتری کے لئے مبیعہ باندی کی سلامتی کا ضامن تھا۔ بخلاف عقر کے۔ کیونکہ یہ تو باندی کے منافع حاصل کرنے کی وجہ سے لازم ہوا ہے تو اس کو بائع سے واپس نہیں لے سکتا۔ وعند الأئمة الثلاثة يرجع بالعقر أيضا
He said: Whoever buys a slave-girl and she gives birth to a child in his possession, then a man establishes his right to her, the father pays the value of the child on the day he litigates, because it is the child of the deceived (maghrur). Indeed, the maghrur is one who has intercourse with a woman relying on ownership of right hand (milk yamin) or marriage (nikah), then she gives birth from him, then she is taken away by right. The child of the maghrur is free by payment of value, by consensus of the Companions, and because consideration of both sides is necessary: the child is made free in origin regarding his father, and a slave regarding the claimant, out of consideration for both. Then the child comes into his hand without his action, so he does not guarantee it except by preventing (delivery), as in the case of the child of a usurped slave-girl. Therefore the value of the child is considered on the day of litigation because that is the day of prevention. If the child dies, nothing is due from the father because there is no prevention. Likewise if the child leaves property, because inheritance is not a substitute for him, and the property goes to his father because he is free in origin regarding him, so the father inherits from him. If the father kills him, he pays his value because prevention exists. Likewise if someone else kills him and he takes his blood money, because the safety of his substitute is like his own safety, and preventing his substitute is like preventing him, so he pays his value just as if he were alive. And he recovers the value of the child from his seller because he guaranteed its safety to him, just as he recovers its price. This differs from the 'uqur (dower) because it became due for enjoying her benefits, so he does not recover it from the seller. And Allah knows best.

Translation: Someone bought a slave-girl; she gave birth to a child at the buyer's; and someone proved ownership of the slave-girl. So the father will pay the value of the child on the day of litigation, because this is the child of the deceived (maghrur). The maghrur is one who has intercourse with a woman relying on milk yamin or nikah, and she bears a child from him, then she is taken by claim. The child of the maghrur is free by payment of value by the consensus of the Companions, and also because consideration of both sides is necessary. So the child is declared free in origin regarding the father, and a slave regarding the claimant, out of consideration for both. Then the child came into the father's possession without transgression, so he is not liable except by prevention, as in the case of the child of a usurped slave-girl. Hence the value considered is that of the day of litigation, because that is the day of prevention. If the child dies, nothing is due from the father due to absence of prevention. Likewise if the child leaves property, because inheritance is not substitute for him, and the property goes to the father because the child is free in origin regarding him. If the father kills him, he pays his value because prevention exists. Likewise if someone else kills him and the father takes his blood money, because the father's receiving the substitute is like receiving the child himself, and preventing the substitute is like preventing the child, so he pays the value as if the child were alive. And he recovers the value of the child from his seller because the seller guaranteed its safety to him, just as he recovers its price. Unlike the 'uqur, because that became due for enjoying her benefits, so he cannot recover it from the seller. According to the three Imams, he also recovers the 'uqur.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a ruling with its proofs. It discusses the case of a man who buys a slave-girl (jariya) in good faith, she gives birth to a child, and then a third party proves rightful ownership of the slave-girl. The buyer is called 'maghrur' (deceived). The ruling: the child is legally free but must be ransomed by paying its value to the rightful owner of the mother. This is based on the consensus (ijma) of the Companions. The reasoning is dual consideration: for the father (the maghrur) the child is free (since the father is free and intercourse was based on apparent ownership), but for the claimant (the true owner) the child is property (since the mother was a slave). The compromise is that the child is free but the father pays its value. The valuation is on the day of litigation, because that is when the father prevents the true owner from taking the child. If the child dies before litigation, no payment is due because there was no prevention. If the child leaves an inheritance, the father inherits because the child is free regarding him. If the father kills the child, he must pay its value (similar to the living case). If someone else kills the child and the father receives the blood money, the father still pays the child's value to the owner, because the blood money substitutes for the child and its prevention. The father can then recover the child's value from the seller of the slave-girl, as the seller guaranteed the slave-girl's safety (the child is considered part of her). However, the 'uqur (the dower paid for intercourse with the slave-girl in case of dispute) is not recoverable from the seller because it is compensation for enjoyment. The end notes that according to the three Imams (likely Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad), the 'uqur is also recoverable from the seller, indicating a different view within the school.

Key Terms
مغرور (maghrūr): One who is deceived; a person who in good faith has intercourse with a woman thinking she is his slave or wife, but she is later claimed by another.
عقر (ʿuqur): The dower paid to a slave-girl for intercourse, or compensation for using her; here it refers to the compensation paid to the rightful owner.
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Claim of rightful ownership; establishing that a property (here the slave-girl) rightfully belongs to someone else.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling on the child of a maghrur, on what day is the child's value assessed?
A) The day the slave-girl was purchased.
B) The day the child was born.
C) The day of litigation (when the father prevents the true owner).
D) The day the true owner makes his claim.

Key Takeaway
By consensus of the Companions, the child of a deceived buyer (maghrur) is free upon payment of its value, valued on the day the father prevents the true owner from taking the child.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
The Child of the Maghrur: Free by Consensus, Ransomed by Value
Explanation of the maghrur case with ijma and dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the child of a maghrur, explain the ijma-based proof for the child's freedom, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi dispute on recovery of 'aqr.

Core Idea
The child of a maghrur is free by ijma; the father pays its value as ransom.

This passage addresses a classical sale problem: a man buys a slave-girl, she bears a child, he claims paternity, then the slave-girl turns out to belong to someone else. The child's status, the father's liability, and what he can recover from the seller are all settled by consensus and rational reasoning, but a subtle dispute divides Abu Hanifa and his three leading disciples on the 'aqr compensation.

Guided Path
1
Define the maghrur
Understand who a maghrur is: a person who has intercourse with a woman believing she is his property or spouse, but she later proves to belong to another.
The term is central to the ruling.
2
Trace the ijma proof
Identify the consensus of the Companions that the child of a maghrur is free and its ransom (value) is wajib on the father. Note that the only ikhtilaf is on how the ransom is paid, not on freedom or obligation.
The text cites Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Razi for this ijma.
3
Assess the ransom details
The child's value is assessed on the day of litigation (not birth or discovery), because detention of the child becomes actionable then. If the child dies before litigation, no ransom. If father kills the child, he pays value.
Rationale: the father is a guarantor of the child only from the day he detains it.
4
Recovery from the seller
The father recovers the price of the slave-girl and the child's value from the seller (since the seller guaranteed safety of the sold item).
But the 'aqr (dower-like compensation paid to the true owner) is not recoverable according to Abu Hanifa.
5
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute on 'aqr
Abu Hanifa holds that 'aqr is compensation for benefit derived (istimta'), not a loss from the sale, so it is not recoverable. The three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar) analogise it to the price and child's value, and rule it recoverable.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position as per the main text.
